Oath Not to Eat Fat: Back Fat Ruling and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains ruling and dispute on back fat in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating back fat in an oath not to eat fat, explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Back fat is meat, not fat, for oaths in Hanafi fiqh by preferred view.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines a nuanced dispute: when a person swears not to eat fat, does eating back fat violate the oath? Abu Hanifa says no, the Sahibayn say yes. The disagreement turns on whether back fat is classified as fat or meat. Understanding this ruling requires analyzing the evidence used by each side and the role of custom and linguistic usage. The preferred position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's view, supported by many later scholars.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Imam Abu Hanifa)
Identify that Abu Hanifa rules no violation: eating back fat does not break an oath not to eat fat.
This is the matn's statement: 'لا يحنث'.
2
Identify the opponent's view (Sahibayn)
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule violation because back fat has the property of melting by fire, characteristic of fat.
They focus on the physical property (خاصية الشحم).
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's counter-proof
Abu Hanifa argues back fat is real meat by origin (from blood), usage (as meat), and effect (provides strength). Thus it is excluded from the term 'fat' in oaths.
This is a talil (reasoning) based on essence and usage.
4
Consider the textual refinements
Note distinctions: kidney fat vs. fat on meat, and the four types of fat listed in al-Kafi. Also note the role of custom (urf) in restricting meaning.
Al-Qadi al-Isbijabi and al-Hidayah provide further clarifications.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
Practical difference: violation requires kaffarah. The relied-upon position (supported by Fath al-Qadir, al-Tahawi) is no violation for back fat. Also, Malik and al-Shafi'i agree with Abu Hanifa in one narration.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبشحم الظهر في شحماً) أي لو حلف لا يأكل شحماً فأكل شحم الظهر لا يحنث فهو معطوف على قوله وبسمك وهذا عند الإمام. وقالا يحنث لوجود خاصية الشحم فيه وهو الذوب بالنار . وله أنه لحم حقيقة ألا ترى أنه ينشأ من الدم ويستعمل استعماله ويحصل به قوته ولهذا يحنث بأكله في اليمين على أكل اللحم إجماعاً كما في المحيط، ولا يحنث ببيعه في اليمين على بيع الشحم . قال القاضي الاسبيجابي : إن أريد بشحم الظهر شحم الكلية فقولهما أظهر وإن أريد به شحم اللحم فقوله أظهر اهـ. وفي فتح القدير : صحح غير واحد قول أبي حنيفة وذكر الطحاوي قول محمد مع أبي حنيفة وهو قول مالك والشافعي في الأصح. وقيد بشحم الظهر لأنه يحنث بشحم البطن اتفاقاً. وذكر في الكافي أن الشحوم أربعة : شحم البطن، وشحم الظهر، وشحم مختلط بالعظم، وشحم على ظاهر الأمعاء. واتفقوا على أنه يحنث بشحم البطن والثلاثة على الخلاف اهـ. واليمين على شراء اللحم كهي على أكله كما في التبيين. وفي فتح القدير: وما في الكافي لا يخلو من نظر بل لا ينبغي خلاف في عدم الحنث بما على الأمعاء في العظم. قال الإمام السرخسي: إن أحداً . لم يقل بأن . مخ العظم شحم .اهـ. وكذا لا ينبغي خلاف في الحنث بما على الأمعاء لأنه لا يختلف في تسميته شحماً .اهـ. وفسر في الهداية شحم الظهر بأنه اللحم السمين. وأشار المصنف إلى أن المأمور بشراء اللحم إذا اشترى شحم الظهر لا يجوز على الأمر وهو مروي عن محمد وهو دليل للإمام أيضاً كما في المحيط. الحواشي السعدية : إن العرف العملي يصلح مقيداً عند بعض مشايخ بلخ لما ذكر في كتب الأصول في مسألة إذا كانت الحقيقة مستعملة والمجاز متعارفاً .اهـ. وهذه النقول تؤذن بأنه لا يحنث بركوب الآدمي في لا يركب حيواناً فإيراد الفرع على ما في الفتح كما في البحر غير وارد لأن العادة حيث كانت مخصصة انصرفت يمينه إلى ما يركب عادة فتدبر .
His statement: (And by back fat in fat) meaning if one swore not to eat fat and ate back fat, he does not violate; it is conjoined to his statement 'and by fish' and this is according to Imam [Abu Hanifa]. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: he violates due to the presence of the property of fat in it, which is melting by fire. And for him (Abu Hanifa), it is real meat. Do you not see that it originates from blood and is used as meat and provides strength? Hence, he violates by eating it in an oath on eating meat by consensus as in al-Muhit, and he does not violate by selling it in an oath on selling fat. Al-Qadi al-Isbijabi said: If by back fat is meant kidney fat, then their statement is more apparent; if meant the fat of meat, then his statement is more apparent. In Fath al-Qadir: Many corrected Abu Hanifa's statement, and al-Tahawi mentioned Muhammad's statement with Abu Hanifa, and it is the stated opinion of Malik and al-Shafi'i. And it is restricted to back fat because he violates by belly fat by agreement. And it is mentioned in al-Kafi that fats are four: belly fat, back fat, fat mixed with bone, and fat on the surface of the intestines. And they agreed that he violates by belly fat, and the three are subject to disagreement. And the oath on purchasing meat is like the oath on eating it as in al-Tabyin. In Fath al-Qadir: What is in al-Kafi is not free from consideration; rather, there should be no dispute about not violating by what is on the intestines in the bone. Imam al-Sarakhsi said: No one said that bone marrow is fat. Likewise, there should be no dispute about violating by what is on the intestines because it is unanimously called fat. And in al-Hidayah, back fat is explained as fatty meat. And the author indicated that if one commanded to buy meat buys back fat, it is not permissible, and it is narrated from Muhammad and is a proof for Imam as well as in al-Muhit. Al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyyah: Practical custom can serve as a restrictor according to some scholars of Balkh as mentioned in usul books on the issue of when the literal meaning is used but common metaphor is customary. And these quotations indicate that he does not violate by riding a human in an oath not to ride an animal, so citing the branch based on what is in al-Fath as in al-Bahr is not sound because when custom is specialized, his oath turns to what is usually ridden, so reflect.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage from the sharh explains the ruling on back fat (shahm al-dhahr) in an oath not to eat fat. The matn states that if one swears not to eat fat and eats back fat, he does not violate according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree and say he does violate because back fat has the property of melting by fire, which is characteristic of fat. Abu Hanifa argues that back fat is actually meat: it originates from blood, is used like meat, and provides strength. For this reason, there is consensus that eating back fat violates an oath not to eat meat. Additionally, selling back fat does not violate an oath not to sell fat. Al-Qadi al-Isbijabi distinguishes: if back fat means kidney fat, then the Sahibayn's view is stronger; if it means the fat on meat, then Abu Hanifa's view is stronger. Fath al-Qadir states that many scholars have preferred Abu Hanifa's position, and that Muhammad agreed with Abu Hanifa. All agree that belly fat causes violation. The four types of fat are listed: belly fat, back fat, fat mixed with bone, and fat on the surface of the intestines. Only belly fat causes violation by consensus. The passage also notes that the ruling on purchasing meat follows the same pattern as eating. Finally, a note from al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyyah connects the discussion to the principle that custom can restrict the literal meaning, and gives the example of riding: if one swears not to ride an animal, custom excludes riding a human.

Key Terms
شحم الظهر (shahm al-dhahr): Back fat; a type of fat that is considered meat by Abu Hanifa, not included in oaths not to eat fat.
خاصية الشحم (khassiyyat al-shahm): The property of fat, i.e., melting by fire; used by Sahibayn to classify back fat as fat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi position, if someone swears not to eat fat and eats back fat (shahm al-dhahr), what is the ruling?
A) He violates the oath because back fat melts by fire.
B) He does not violate because back fat is considered meat.
C) He violates only if the back fat is from a specific animal.
D) He must consult a judge to determine the ruling.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, back fat is legally meat, so eating it does not violate an oath not to eat fat.

Tail Fat (Alyah): A Third Category in Oaths
States that tail fat does not violate an oath against meat or fat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that tail fat (alyah) is a distinct category in Hanafi law and apply this principle to determine if an oath against meat or fat is violated.

Core Idea
Tail fat (alyah) is neither meat nor fat — it is a third category, so an oath against either does not cover it.

Oaths often involve specific terms like 'meat' or 'fat'. But what if someone swears not to eat either and then eats a part of the animal that is neither? The Hanafi school classifies tail fat (alyah) as a separate entity, distinct from meat and fat. This lesson explores the reasoning behind this classification and its practical effect on oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the oath
Determine what the person swore off (meat or fat) and what they actually ate (tail fat).
The oath is specific: 'I will not eat meat' or 'I will not eat fat'.
2
Analyze the linguistic meaning
Consider whether the word 'meat' or 'fat' in its usual sense includes tail fat. Tail fat is not commonly referred to as meat or used as cooking fat in the same way.
Custom (urf) plays a key role here.
3
Apply the legal principle of distinct categories
Recognize that tail fat is a third type, separate from both meat and fat. The text explicitly states it is a 'third type' and is not used as meat or fat.
This is a ruling based on the reality of the substance and common usage.
4
State the ruling
Conclude that eating tail fat does not violate the oath, because it is not included in the meaning or custom of what was sworn off.
The oath remains intact; no expiation is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وبألية في شحماً ولحماً) أي لا يحنث يأكل ألية لو حلف لا يأكل لحماً أو حلف لا يأكل شحماً لأنها نوع . ثالث حتى لا تستعمل استعمال اللحوم والشحوم فلا يتناولها اللفظ معنى ولا عرفاً
His statement: (And by tail fat in fat and meat) meaning he does not violate by eating tail fat if he swore not to eat meat or swore not to eat fat because it is a third type; it is not used as meat or fat, so the word does not include it by meaning or custom.
Source pages p. 540

Commentary
This short passage from the sharh clarifies the ruling on tail fat (alyah) in oaths. The matn states that if one swears not to eat meat or not to eat fat, eating tail fat does not cause violation. The reason is that tail fat is a third distinct category; it is neither meat nor fat in common usage. Therefore, the literal meaning of 'meat' or 'fat' does not cover it, and custom also does not include it. Thus, the oath is not violated.

Key Terms
ألية (alyah): Tail fat (particularly of sheep); considered a separate substance, not included in oaths of meat or fat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not eat fat' and then eats tail fat (alyah), what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) He violates the oath because tail fat is a type of fat.
B) He does not violate because tail fat is considered meat.
C) He does not violate because tail fat is a third category distinct from fat.
D) He violates only if the tail fat is from a specific animal.

Key Takeaway
Tail fat (alyah) is a distinct third category in Hanafi law, so an oath against meat or fat is not violated by eating it.

Oath Not to Eat This Wheat: Bread vs. Grain in Hanafi Fiqh
Explains intra-Hanafi dispute on oath scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the scope of an oath not to eat a specific wheat and distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding bread, raw grain, fried grain, flour, and sawiq.

Core Idea
Swearing not to eat this wheat only breaks by biting the grain itself, not by bread or other derivatives, according to Abu Hanifa.

This lesson examines an important intra-Hanafi dispute about the interpretation of an oath when the object is a specific food item. The key question: does eating bread made from that wheat violate the oath? Imam Abu Hanifa insists on the literal meaning of the grain, while his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, consider customary usage. We will unpack their arguments, explore the nuances of raw vs. fried grain and sawiq, and understand how the ruling is applied in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Determine what exactly the oath prohibits: 'I will not eat this wheat.' The dispute is whether 'wheat' includes its processed forms like bread.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that only biting (qadm) the grain itself—whether raw or fried—violates the oath. Eating bread, flour, or sawiq does not violate.
His principle: literal meaning of the word overrides customary metaphor.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that eating bread also violates because customarily 'not eating wheat' includes not eating its common products like bread. They differ on sawiq: Muhammad says sawiq violates, Abu Yusuf disagrees.
They rely on the general metaphor and custom in oaths.
4
Analyze the reasoning
Abu Hanifa argues that the word 'wheat' has actual uses—boiled, fried, eaten raw—so the literal meaning is alive and overrides the metaphorical extension. The Sahibayn argue that when wheat is mentioned in context of eating, bread is the primary intended form.
This follows the usul principle: haqiqah takes precedence over majaz unless custom is overwhelming.
5
Consider the practical application
The author distinguishes between specific wheat ('this wheat') and general wheat. For specific wheat, Abu Hanifa's view is followed. For general wheat, the Sahibayn's view may apply. Also, if the swearer intended bread, his intention is respected.
The mufta bihi depends on the wording and custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وبالخبز في هذا البر) أي لا يحنث بأكل الخبز في حلفه لا يأكل هذا البر فلا يحنث إلا بالقضم من عينها عند الإمام. وقالا : إن أكل من خبزها حنث أيضاً لأنه مفهوم منه عرفاً. ولأبي حنيفة أن لها حقيقة مستعملة فإنها تغلى وتقلى وتؤكل قضماً قاضية على المجاز المتعارف كما هو الأصل عنده، ولو قضمها حنث عندهما على الصحيح لعموم المجاز كما إذا حلف لا يضع قدمه في دار فلان وإليه الإشارة بقوله حنث في الخبز أيضاً؛ كذا في الهداية. وصحح في الذخيرة عنهما أنه لا يحنث بأكل عينها. وفي فتح القدير والمحيط : إنما يحنث بأكل عينها عند الإمام إذا لم تكن نيئة بأن كانت مقلية كالبليلة في عرفنا أما إذا قضمها نيئة لم يحنث لأنه غير مستعمل أصلاً. وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو أكل من دقيقها أو سويقها فإنه لا يحنث بالأولى عند الإمام. وأما عندهما فقالوا : لو أكل من سويقها حنث عند محمد خلافاً لأبي يوسف. فيحتاج أبو يوسف إلى الفرق بين الخبز والسويق والفرق أن الحنطة إذا ذكرت مقرونة بالأكل يراد بها الخبز دون السويق، ومحمد اعتبر عموم المجاز. وأطلقه المصنف فشمل ما إذا نوى عينها أو لم تكن له نية كما في البدائع ولا يخفى أنه إذا نوى أكل الخبز فإنه يصدق لأنه شدد على نفسه . وقيد بكون الحنطة معينة لأنه لو حلف لا يأكل حنطة ينبغي أن يكون جوابه كجوابهما، ذكره شيخ الإسلام ولا يخفى أن تحكم والدليل المذكور المتفق على إيراده في جميع المعينة والمنكرة وهو أن عينها مأكول ؛ كذا في فتح القدير ولا فرق في الحكم بين أن يقول لا آكل من هذه الحنطة أو هذه الحنطة كما في البدائع .
His statement: (And by bread in this wheat) meaning he does not violate by eating bread in his oath not to eat this wheat; he only violates by biting the grain itself according to Imam [Abu Hanifa]. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: if he eats its bread, he also violates because it is understood customarily. For Abu Hanifa, it has an actual literal usage; it is boiled, fried, and eaten as bites, which overrides the common metaphor as is his principle, and if he bites it, he violates according to both on the correct view due to the generality of metaphor, just as if he swore not to place his foot in So-and-so's house. And to this there is reference in his statement 'he violates by bread also' as in al-Hidayah. And in al-Dhakhirah, from both of them they corrected that he does not violate by eating the grain itself. In Fath al-Qadir and al-Muhit: He only violates by eating the grain itself according to Imam if it is not raw, i.e., fried like balila in our custom; but if he bites it raw, he does not violate because it is not used at all. And the author indicated that if he ate its flour or sawiq (roasted grain meal), he does not violate, a fortiori according to Imam. As for them (the two), they said: if he ate its sawiq, he violates according to Muhammad contrary to Abu Yusuf. So Abu Yusuf needs to differentiate between bread and sawiq, and the difference is that when wheat is mentioned in connection with eating, bread is intended, not sawiq. And Muhammad considered the general metaphor. And the author left it unrestricted, so it includes whether he intended the grain itself or had no intention as in al-Bada'i. And it is not hidden that if he intended to eat bread, he is believed because he imposed a stricter condition on himself. And it is conditioned that the wheat be specific because if he swore not to eat wheat, his answer should be like their answer, mentioned by Sheikh al-Islam. And it is not hidden that the evidence agreed upon in all cases, specific and general, is that the grain itself is eaten. In Fath al-Qadir, there is no difference in the ruling between saying 'I will not eat from this wheat' or 'I will not eat this wheat' as in al-Bada'i.
Source pages p. 540

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling when someone swears not to eat a specific quantity of wheat (or barley, as 'burr' can mean both). The matn states that eating bread made from that wheat does not violate the oath according to Imam Abu Hanifa; only eating the raw grain itself (by biting it) does. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, holding that eating bread also violates because customarily, 'eating wheat' includes bread. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the word 'wheat' has an actual literal usage (it is boiled, fried, eaten as bites), and that literal meaning prevails over the common metaphorical use (bread). However, if one bites the raw grain, he violates according to the Sahibayn due to the generality of the metaphor. The text further notes differences: according to Abu Hanifa, even eating the grain itself only violates if it is fried (cooked in a certain way) in common custom; raw grain does not cause violation. As for flour or sawiq (roasted grain meal), Abu Hanifa says no violation by priority; the Sahibayn differ with Muhammad saying flour/sawiq causes violation and Abu Yusuf disagreeing. The final fatwa seems to consider that if the wheat is specific (e.g., 'this wheat'), the oath is not violated by eating bread; but if the oath is general ('I will not eat wheat'), then the Sahibayn's view applies. The author also notes that if the swearer intended to include bread, he is believed because he imposed extra restriction on himself.

Key Terms
البر (al-burr): Wheat or barley; in oaths, it refers to the grain itself, not its processed forms like bread, unless custom requires otherwise.
قضم (qadm): Biting or eating the grain raw or fried; only this form violates according to Abu Hanifa.
سويق (sawiq): Roasted grain meal; a processed form of grain. Its status in oaths is disputed among the Sahibayn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling when someone swears 'I will not eat this wheat' and then eats bread made from it, based on the view of Imam Abu Hanifa?
A) He violates the oath, and must perform expiation.
B) He does not violate the oath, because bread is not the literal grain.
C) He violates only if the bread is made from raw grains.
D) The ruling depends on whether he intended to include bread.

Key Takeaway
In an oath not to eat a specific grain, Abu Hanifa holds that the literal meaning of the grain overrides custom, so processed forms like bread are not included unless custom is overwhelming or the oath is general.

Eating Bread from Forbidden Flour: Violating the Oath
Explains that eating bread from flour violates the oath, but raw flour does not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether eating bread or raw flour violates an oath not to eat a specified flour.

Core Idea
An oath not to eat flour includes its common use (bread) but not raw flour.

In Hanafi fiqh, oaths are interpreted by customary usage ('urf). When someone swears not to eat a particular flour, the question arises: does eating bread from that flour count? Does raw flour count? This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the ruling based on what is typically consumed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the oath
First, note the specific item sworn upon: the flour itself (al-daqeeq). The oath is 'I will not eat this flour'.
2
Consider customary consumption
In Hanafi law, the scope of an oath follows customary usage ('urf). Flour is normally eaten as bread, not raw. Therefore, the oath is violated by eating bread made from that flour, but not by eating raw flour.
The term used for raw flour is 'safh' or 'saqeet', which refers to eating dry flour without preparation.
3
Apply the ruling
State the result: if the person eats bread from the flour, he has violated the oath and must expiate (kaffarah). If he eats raw flour, he has not violated the oath.
4
Note the textual variant
The passage includes a marginal note about a different issue (fat on intestines), but the core ruling remains clear and is not affected by that variant.
Ignore the variant for this lesson; focus on the main ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وفي هذا الدقيق يحنث بخبزه لا بسفه) أي في حلفه لا يأكل هذا الدقيق لا قوله : (بل لا ينبغي خلافه في عدم الحنث بما على الإمعاء في العظم) عبارة الفتح بما في العظم» فقوله «على الإمعاء لعله من زيادات النساخ قوله : (وأشار المصنف إلى أن المأمور بشراء للحم كذا في بعض النسخ وفي بعضها الشحم بدل «اللحم وهي أظهر .
His statement: (And in this flour, he violates by its bread, not by its raw flour) meaning in his oath not to eat this flour, he does not [violate] by raw flour. Then a comment: (Rather, there should be no dispute about not violating by what is on the intestines in the bone) is the wording of Fath al-Qadir 'what is in the bone'; so his statement 'on the intestines' is probably from the copyists' additions. His statement: (And the author indicated that the one commanded to buy meat... as in some copies, and in some copies 'fat' instead of 'meat', and that is more apparent.)
Source pages p. 540

Commentary
This short passage covers the ruling from the matn regarding an oath not to eat a specific flour. The base text (Kanz al-Daqaiq) states that if one swears not to eat a particular flour, he violates the oath by eating bread made from that flour, but not by eating the raw flour itself (safh or saqeet). The sharh explains this clearly: the flour is typically processed into bread, so bread is included in the oath, while raw flour is not commonly eaten and thus not included. The passage then includes some textual notes from Fath al-Qadir regarding a previous discussion about fat on intestines and a variant reading, but these are secondary.

Key Terms
دقيق (daqeeq): Flour; in oaths, eating bread from it violates, but eating raw flour does not.
سفه (safh / saqeet): Raw flour or the act of eating it; does not violate the oath not to eat flour.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not eat this flour' and then eats raw flour from it, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) He violates the oath because flour is flour whether raw or cooked.
B) He does not violate the oath because raw flour is not customarily eaten.
C) He violates only if he makes bread with it.
D) He must expiate for the oath either way.

Key Takeaway
An oath not to eat flour is violated by customary consumption (bread), not by non-customary forms (raw flour).

Oaths on Flour: When Eating Bread Breaks the Oath
Explaining when an oath about flour is broken by bread.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an oath regarding flour and apply the principle of custom (urf) to determine when the oath is broken.

Core Idea
An oath about flour is broken by its bread, not by raw flour, because custom diverts meaning to the usual product.

This lesson examines a key ruling from Kitab al-Ayman about oaths concerning flour. The Hanafi school holds that if someone swears not to eat a specific flour, he breaks his oath by eating bread made from it, but not by swallowing the raw flour. This is based on the principle of istihsan (juristic preference) supported by custom (urf). We will unpack the reasoning and see how custom shapes the interpretation of oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is an oath regarding flour. The ruling is that the oath is broken by eating its bread, not by eating it raw.
2
Locate the dalil
The text relies on istihsan and custom. There is no explicit scriptural proof; rather, rational preference and common practice.
This is a case of dalil aqli (rational proof).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Custom diverts the literal meaning of 'flour' to 'what is usually made from it' because that is how people understand the oath.
The Hanafi principle: al-urf muhakkam (custom is authoritative).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools may not accept custom as a determinant, but Hanafi fiqh strongly relies on urf.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon position is that custom determines the meaning of terms in oaths.
The mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في الأكل والشرب واللبس والكلام. هذا الدقيق يحنث بخبزه لا بسفه. والخبز ما اعتاده بلده. فإذا حلف لا يأكل خبزاً حنث بأكل خبز البر والشعير، والشواء والطبيخ على اللحم. والرأس ما يباع في مصره هو معتاد بينهم يسمى يحنث بأكل عينه لأن عينه غير مأكول بخلاف الحنطة فانصرف إلى ما يتخذ منه. فلو استفه كما هو لم يحنث على الصحيح لتعين المجاز مراداً كما لو أكل عين النخلة كما قدمناه. وإن عنى أكل الدقيق بعينه لم يحنث بأكل خبزه لأنه نوى الحقيقة. وفي المحيط: وكذلك لو أكل من عصيدته يحنث لأنه قد يؤكل كذلك لأن أكل الدقيق هكذا يكون عند العقلاء فينصرف إلى ما اهـ. وفي الظهيرية: حلف أن لا يأكل من هذا الدقيق فاتخذ منه خبيصاً. قال الفقيه أو الليث: أخاف أن يحنثه. اهـ. وفي الخبيص الحلواء فلو قال المصنف حنث بما يتخذ منه لكان أولى. قوله: (والخبز ما اعتاده بلده فإذا حلف لا يأكل خبزاً حنث بأكل خبز البر والشعير) لأنه هو المعتاد في غالب البلاد. فلو أكل من خبز القطائف لا يحنث لأنه لا خبزاً مطلقاً إلا إذا نواه لأنه يحتمله. ولو أكل خبز الأرز بالعراق لم يحنث لأنه غير متعارف عندهم حتى لو كان بطبرستان أو في بلد طعامهم ذلك حنث. ولا يحنث بخبز الشعير إن كان مصرياً لأنهم لا يعتادون إلا خبز البر، ويحنث الحجازي واليمني بخبز الذرة لأنهم يعتادونه. ودخل في الخبز الكماج لأنه خبز وزيادة للاختصاص باسم الزيادة لا للنقص. ولا يحنث بالثريد لأنه لا يسمى خبزاً مطلقاً. وفي الخلاصة: حلف لا يأكل من هذا الخبز فأكله بعدما تفتت لا يحنث لأنه لا يسمى خبزاً. ولا يحنث بالعصيد والططماج ولا يحنث لو دقه فشربه. وعن أبي حنيفة في حيلة أكله أن يدقه فيلقيه في عصيده ويطبخ حتى يصير الخبز هالكاً. وقد سئل المحقق ابن الهمام عن بدوي اعتاد أكل خبز الشعير فدخل البلدة المعتاد فيها أكل خبز لا يأكل إلا الشعير فحلف لا يأكل خبزاً قال: فقلت لا ينعقد إلا على عرف نفسه فيحنث بالشعير لأنه لم ينعقد على عرف الناس إلا لأن الحالف يتعاطاه فهو منهم فينصرف كلامه لذلك وهذا منتف فيمن لم يوافقهم بل هو مجانب لهم اهـ. وفي الظهيرية: يحنث بأكل الزما ورد وهو ما يقطع من الخبز مستدبراً بعد أن كان محشواً بالبيض وغيره. ولو أكل الخبز مبلولاً حنث. وفي الخانية أنه يحنث بأكل الرقاق. اهـ. وينبغي أن يخص ذلك بالرقاق البيساني بمصر، أما الرقاق الذي يحشى بالسكر واللوز فلا يدخل تحت اسم الخبز في عرفنا كما لا يخفى. وفي الظهيرية: لو حلف لا يأكل خبز فلانة الخابزة والخابزة هي التي تضرب الخبز في التنور دون إلى تعجنه وتهيئه للضرب فإن أكل من خبز التي ضربته حنث الحنطة واستمر هو وإلا فلا اهـ.
Book of Oaths, Chapter on Oaths in Eating, Drinking, Clothing, and Speech. This flour: he breaks his oath by its bread, not by swallowing it. Bread is what his town customarily [eats]. If he swears not to eat bread, he breaks his oath by eating wheat and barley bread, roast and cooked meat. The head that is sold in his city is customarily among them called [something]; he breaks his oath by eating its substance because its substance is not eaten, unlike wheat, so it is diverted to what is made from it. If he swallows it as is, he does not break his oath on the correct view because the metaphor is intended, just as if he ate the substance of a date palm as we previously mentioned. If he meant eating the flour itself, he does not break his oath by eating its bread because he intended the literal meaning. In al-Muhit: Likewise, if he eats from its thick porridge, he breaks his oath because it may be eaten that way, since eating flour thus is done by rational people, so it is diverted to that. In al-Zahiriyyah: He swore not to eat from this flour, so he made halva from it. Al-Faqih or al-Layth said: I fear that he would break his oath. In halva is sweetmeat; if the author said 'he breaks his oath by what is made from it', it would be better. His statement: (And bread is what his town customarily eats; if he swears not to eat bread, he breaks his oath by eating wheat and barley bread) because that is what is customary in most lands. If he eats from layered pastry bread, he does not break his oath because it is not absolute bread unless he intended it, because it can bear that. If he eats rice bread in Iraq, he does not break his oath because it is not customary among them; even if he were in Tabaristan or a land where that is their food, he breaks his oath. He does not break his oath by barley bread if he is Egyptian because they customarily eat only wheat bread; a Hijazi or Yemeni breaks his oath by corn bread because they customarily eat it. Khubz includes kamaj because it is bread and more; specificity is by the name of the addition, not by deficiency. He does not break his oath by tharid (bread soaked in broth) because it is not called absolute bread. In al-Khulasa: He swore not to eat from this bread and ate it after it crumbled; he does not break his oath because it is not called bread. He does not break his oath by thick porridge or [ططماج]. He does not break his oath if he grinds it and drinks it. From Abu Hanifa regarding a trick to eat it: that he grind it and throw it into his porridge and cook until the bread becomes ruined. The investigator Ibn al-Humam was asked about a Bedouin accustomed to eating barley bread who entered a town where the custom is to eat wheat bread; he swore not to eat bread. He said: I said it is only concluded according to his own custom, so he breaks his oath by barley because it is not concluded according to people's custom unless the one swearing engages in it; he is among them, so his speech is diverted to that, and this is absent from one who does not agree with them, rather he is opposed to them. In al-Zahiriyyah: He breaks his oath by eating zamawrad, which is cut from bread circularly after being stuffed with eggs etc. If he eats bread moistened, he breaks his oath. In al-Khaniyyah: He breaks his oath by eating thin bread. It should be specified that this refers to the thin bread of Baysan in Egypt; as for thin bread stuffed with sugar and almonds, it does not fall under the name bread in our custom, as is not hidden. In al-Zahiriyyah: If he swears not to eat the bread of so-and-so the baker (al-khabizah), and the baker is the one who places the bread in the oven without kneading it and preparing it for placing; if he eats the bread of the one who placed it, he breaks his oath; as for the wheat, it remains as is, otherwise no.
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on oaths regarding flour and bread. The core ruling is that an oath not to eat a specific flour is broken by eating its bread, not by swallowing the raw flour, because custom (urf) diverts the meaning to the usual product. Similarly, if one swears not to eat bread, he breaks it by eating the common bread of his town (wheat or barley) but not by unusual breads like rice bread unless he intends them. The text introduces a hilah (legal trick) from Abu Hanifa to avoid breaking the oath: grinding the bread and mixing it into porridge until it is no longer recognisable as bread. The passage also addresses regional variations: a Bedouin accustomed to barley bread who moves to a wheat-bread town and swears not to eat bread is judged by his own custom, so barley bread breaks his oath. The discussion of thin bread (ruqaq) shows that only certain types are considered bread in Egyptian custom. The baker example distinguishes between the one who prepares the dough and the one who bakes; the oath applies to the baker's bread only if she performed the baking. The text relies on istihsan (juristic preference) based on custom, not explicit textual proof.

Key Terms
الخبز (al-khubz): Bread that is commonly eaten in one's locality, such as wheat or barley bread.
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath sworn by Allah, binding to fulfill conditions or break it requires expiation.
الحيلة (al-hilah): A permissible legal trick to avoid breaking an oath without lying, such as grinding bread into porridge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears not to eat 'this flour'. He then eats bread made from it. Does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because flour is the ingredient of bread.
B) No, because bread is not flour.
C) Yes, because custom diverts the meaning to bread.
D) No, unless he specifically intended bread.

Key Takeaway
Custom (urf) determines the meaning of the object of an oath; thus, if one swears not to eat flour, the oath is broken by eating its bread, not by swallowing the raw flour.

What Counts as Bread in Oaths? The Hanafi Custom-Based Definition
Explaining how custom defines bread for oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of custom (urf) to determine whether a particular food counts as bread for the purpose of an oath and distinguish between different regional customs.

Core Idea
Bread is defined by the custom of the swearer's locality, not by a universal definition — wheat, barley, corn, or rice breads depend on what is common.

The previous lesson established that an oath about flour is broken by its bread. But what exactly is 'bread'? The Hanafi school uses a custom-based definition: bread is what the people of that town commonly eat. This lesson explores the extensive discussion in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq about different types of bread (wheat, barley, rice, corn, thin bread, stuffed bread, etc.) and how regional custom determines whether a particular food breaks an oath not to eat bread.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
The text states: 'Bread is what his town customarily [eats].' This is the guiding principle.
2
Apply to common breads
In most lands, wheat and barley bread are customary, so they break the oath.
The text says: 'because that is what is customary in most lands.'
3
Apply to uncommon breads
Rice bread in Iraq is not customary, so it does not break the oath unless intended.
The text says: 'if he eats rice bread in Iraq, he does not break his oath because it is not customary among them.'
4
Consider regional variation
A Hijazi or Yemeni breaks his oath by corn bread because they customarily eat it.
The text explicitly differentiates by region.
5
Evaluate marginal cases
Types like kamaj (bread with additions), tharid (soaked bread), ruqaq (thin bread) are considered bread or not based on custom.
Kamaj is bread; tharid is not; ruqaq depends on local usage.

Source Text

واستمر هو وإلا فلا اهـ.
Book of Oaths, Chapter on Oaths in Eating, Drinking, Clothing, and Speech. This flour: he breaks his oath by its bread, not by swallowing it. Bread is what his town customarily [eats]. If he swears not to eat bread, he breaks his oath by eating wheat and barley bread, roast and cooked meat. The head that is sold in his city is customarily among them called [something]; he breaks his oath by eating its substance because its substance is not eaten, unlike wheat, so it is diverted to what is made from it. If he swallows it as is, he does not break his oath on the correct view because the metaphor is intended, just as if he ate the substance of a date palm as we previously mentioned. If he meant eating the flour itself, he does not break his oath by eating its bread because he intended the literal meaning. In al-Muhit: Likewise, if he eats from its thick porridge, he breaks his oath because it may be eaten that way, since eating flour thus is done by rational people, so it is diverted to that. In al-Zahiriyyah: He swore not to eat from this flour, so he made halva from it. Al-Faqih or al-Layth said: I fear that he would break his oath. In halva is sweetmeat; if the author said 'he breaks his oath by what is made from it', it would be better. His statement: (And bread is what his town customarily eats; if he swears not to eat bread, he breaks his oath by eating wheat and barley bread) because that is what is customary in most lands. If he eats from layered pastry bread, he does not break his oath because it is not absolute bread unless he intended it, because it can bear that. If he eats rice bread in Iraq, he does not break his oath because it is not customary among them; even if he were in Tabaristan or a land where that is their food, he breaks his oath. Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on oaths regarding flour and bread. The core ruling is that an oath not to eat a specific flour is broken by eating its bread, not by swallowing the raw flour, because custom (urf) diverts the meaning to the usual product. Similarly, if one swears not to eat bread, he breaks it by eating the common bread of his town (wheat or barley) but not by unusual breads like rice bread unless he intends them. The text introduces a hilah (legal trick) from Abu Hanifa to avoid breaking the oath: grinding the bread and mixing it into porridge until it is no longer recognisable as bread. The passage also addresses regional variations: a Bedouin accustomed to barley bread who moves to a wheat-bread town and swears not to eat bread is judged by his own custom, so barley bread breaks his oath. The discussion of thin bread (ruqaq) shows that only certain types are considered bread in Egyptian custom. The baker example distinguishes between the one who prepares the dough and the one who bakes; the oath applies to the baker's bread only if she performed the baking. The text relies on istihsan (juristic preference) based on custom, not explicit textual proof.

Key Terms
الخبز (al-khubz): Bread; in Hanafi oaths, defined by the custom of the swearer's locality.
الكماج (al-kamaj): A type of bread that is bread plus additional ingredients; still considered bread.
الثريد (al-tharid): Bread soaked in broth; not considered absolute bread.
الرقاق (al-ruqaq): Thin bread; considered bread in some regions like Baysan, but not if stuffed with sweets.
العرف (al-urf): Custom; a source of law in Hanafi fiqh that determines meanings in oaths.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person from Iraq (where rice bread is not customary) swears not to eat bread. He then eats rice bread. According to Hanafi fiqh, has he broken his oath?
A) Yes, because rice bread is a form of bread.
B) No, because rice bread is not customary in Iraq.
C) Yes, if he intended to include rice bread.
D) No, because bread must be made from wheat.

Key Takeaway
Custom (urf) determines what counts as bread in oaths; only foods commonly eaten as bread in the swearer's locality break the oath.

Avoiding Oath Breaking: The Hanafi Legal Trick (Hilah) and Custom of the Swearer
Explaining the legal trick and the individual custom rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a permissible legal trick (hilah) to avoid breaking an oath without lying and apply the principle that the oath of a person from a specific custom is interpreted according to his own custom.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa permits grinding bread into porridge to avoid breaking an oath, and a Bedouin's oath is judged by his own custom, not the majority's.

This lesson explores two important secondary rulings from the same passage. First, the Hanafi school permits a legal trick (hilah) to avoid breaking an oath: if one has sworn not to eat bread, he can grind it into porridge and eat it, as it is no longer recognisable as bread. Second, the passage addresses a Bedouin who is accustomed to barley bread but enters a wheat-bread town and swears not to eat bread. Ibn al-Humam rules that his oath is bound by his own custom, so eating barley bread breaks it. These examples show the flexibility of Hanafi fiqh in accommodating individual circumstances and providing lawful alternatives.

Guided Path
1
State the hilah
Abu Hanifa says to grind the bread and mix it into porridge until it becomes ruined (no longer bread).
2
Understand the rationale
The hilah works because the bread loses its name and identity; thus, eating it no longer constitutes eating bread.
The text says: 'حتى يصير الخبز هالكاً' (until the bread becomes ruined).
3
Apply the Bedouin case
The Bedouin's custom is barley bread; his oath not to eat bread is understood according to his own custom, not the town's.
Ibn al-Humam's fatwa.
4
Distinguish from the general rule
The general rule uses the town's custom, but if the swearer is not from that town, his own custom applies.
This is an exception based on individual circumstances.
5
Evaluate limitations
The hilah is permissible only if it does not involve deception; it is a legitimate way to keep the oath.
The text does not discuss limits, but Hanafi fiqh generally allows hilah as long as it doesn't violate sharia.

Source Text

كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في الأكل والشرب واللبس والكلام. هذا الدقيق يحنث بخبزه لا بسفه. والخبز ما اعتاده بلده. فإذا حلف لا يأكل خبزاً حنث بأكل خبز البر والشعير، والشواء والطبيخ على اللحم. والرأس ما يباع في مصره هو معتاد بينهم يسمى يحنث بأكل عينه لأن عينه غير مأكول بخلاف الحنطة فانصرف إلى ما يتخذ منه. فلو استفه كما هو لم يحنث على الصحيح لتعين المجاز مراداً كما لو أكل عين النخلة كما قدمناه. وإن عنى أكل الدقيق بعينه لم يحنث بأكل خبزه لأنه نوى الحقيقة. وفي المحيط: وكذلك لو أكل من عصيدته يحنث لأنه قد يؤكل كذلك لأن أكل الدقيق هكذا يكون عند العقلاء فينصرف إلى ما اهـ. وفي الظهيرية: حلف أن لا يأكل من هذا الدقيق فاتخذ منه خبيصاً. قال الفقيه أو الليث: أخاف أن يحنثه. اهـ. وفي الخبيص الحلواء فلو قال المصنف حنث بما يتخذ منه لكان أولى. قوله: (والخبز ما اعتاده بلده فإذا حلف لا يأكل خبزاً حنث بأكل خبز البر والشعير) لأنه هو المعتاد في غالب البلاد. فلو أكل من خبز القطائف لا يحنث لأنه لا خبزاً مطلقاً إلا إذا نواه لأنه يحتمله. ولو أكل خبز الأرز بالعراق لم يحنث لأنه غير متعارف عندهم حتى لو كان بطبرستان أو في بلد طعامهم ذلك حنث. ولا يحنث بخبز الشعير إن كان مصرياً لأنهم لا يعتادون إلا خبز البر، ويحنث الحجازي واليمني بخبز الذرة لأنهم يعتادونه. ودخل في الخبز الكماج لأنه خبز وزيادة للاختصاص باسم الزيادة لا للنقص. ولا يحنث بالثريد لأنه لا يسمى خبزاً مطلقاً. وفي الخلاصة: حلف لا يأكل من هذا الخبز فأكله بعدما تفتت لا يحنث لأنه لا يسمى خبزاً. ولا يحنث بالعصيد والططماج ولا يحنث لو دقه فشربه. وعن أبي حنيفة في حيلة أكله أن يدقه فيلقيه في عصيده ويطبخ حتى يصير الخبز هالكاً. وقد سئل المحقق ابن الهمام عن بدوي اعتاد أكل خبز الشعير فدخل البلدة المعتاد فيها أكل خبز لا يأكل إلا الشعير فحلف لا يأكل خبزاً قال: فقلت لا ينعقد إلا على عرف نفسه فيحنث بالشعير لأنه لم ينعقد على عرف الناس إلا لأن الحالف يتعاطاه فهو منهم فينصرف كلامه لذلك وهذا منتف فيمن لم يوافقهم بل هو مجانب لهم اهـ. وفي الظهيرية: يحنث بأكل الزما ورد وهو ما يقطع من الخبز مستدبراً بعد أن كان محشواً بالبيض وغيره. ولو أكل الخبز مبلولاً حنث. وفي الخانية أنه يحنث بأكل الرقاق. اهـ. وينبغي أن يخص ذلك بالرقاق البيساني بمصر، أما الرقاق الذي يحشى بالسكر واللوز فلا يدخل تحت اسم الخبز في عرفنا كما لا يخفى. وفي الظهيرية: لو حلف لا يأكل خبز فلانة الخابزة والخابزة هي التي تضرب الخبز في التنور دون إلى تعجنه وتهيئه للضرب فإن أكل من خبز التي ضربته حنث الحنطة واستمر هو وإلا فلا اهـ.
Book of Oaths, Chapter on Oaths in Eating, Drinking, Clothing, and Speech. This flour: he breaks his oath by its bread, not by swallowing it. Bread is what his town customarily [eats]. If he swears not to eat bread, he breaks his oath by eating wheat and barley bread, roast and cooked meat. The head that is sold in his city is customarily among them called [something]; he breaks his oath by eating its substance because its substance is not eaten, unlike wheat, so it is diverted to what is made from it. If he swallows it as is, he does not break his oath on the correct view because the metaphor is intended, just as if he ate the substance of a date palm as we previously mentioned. If he meant eating the flour itself, he does not break his oath by eating its bread because he intended the literal meaning. In al-Muhit: Likewise, if he eats from its thick porridge, he breaks his oath because it may be eaten that way, since eating flour thus is done by rational people, so it is diverted to that. In al-Zahiriyyah: He swore not to eat from this flour, so he made halva from it. Al-Faqih or al-Layth said: I fear that he would break his oath. In halva is sweetmeat; if the author said 'he breaks his oath by what is made from it', it would be better. His statement: (And bread is what his town customarily eats; if he swears not to eat bread, he breaks his oath by eating wheat and barley bread) because that is what is customary in most lands. If he eats from layered pastry bread, he does not break his oath because it is not absolute bread unless he intended it, because it can bear that. If he eats rice bread in Iraq, he does not break his oath because it is not customary among them; even if he were in Tabaristan or a land where that is their food, he breaks his oath. He does not break his oath by barley bread if he is Egyptian because they customarily eat only wheat bread; a Hijazi or Yemeni breaks his oath by corn bread because they customarily eat it. Khubz includes kamaj because it is bread and more; specificity is by the name of the addition, not by deficiency. He does not break his oath by tharid (bread soaked in broth) because it is not called absolute bread. In al-Khulasa: He swore not to eat from this bread and ate it after it crumbled; he does not break his oath because it is not called bread. He does not break his oath by thick porridge or [ططماج]. He does not break his oath if he grinds it and drinks it. From Abu Hanifa regarding a trick to eat it: that he grind it and throw it into his porridge and cook until the bread becomes ruined. The investigator Ibn al-Humam was asked about a Bedouin accustomed to eating barley bread who entered a town where the custom is to eat wheat bread; he swore not to eat bread. He said: I said it is only concluded according to his own custom, so he breaks his oath by barley because it is not concluded according to people's custom unless the one swearing engages in it; he is among them, so his speech is diverted to that, and this is absent from one who does not agree with them, rather he is opposed to them. In al-Zahiriyyah: He breaks his oath by eating zamawrad, which is cut from bread circularly after being stuffed with eggs etc. If he eats bread moistened, he breaks his oath. In al-Khaniyyah: He breaks his oath by eating thin bread. It should be specified that this refers to the thin bread of Baysan in Egypt; as for thin bread stuffed with sugar and almonds, it does not fall under the name bread in our custom, as is not hidden. In al-Zahiriyyah: If he swears not to eat the bread of so-and-so the baker (al-khabizah), and the baker is the one who places the bread in the oven without kneading it and preparing it for placing; if he eats the bread of the one who placed it, he breaks his oath; as for the wheat, it remains as is, otherwise no.
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on oaths regarding flour and bread. The core ruling is that an oath not to eat a specific flour is broken by eating its bread, not by swallowing the raw flour, because custom (urf) diverts the meaning to the usual product. Similarly, if one swears not to eat bread, he breaks it by eating the common bread of his town (wheat or barley) but not by unusual breads like rice bread unless he intends them. The text introduces a hilah (legal trick) from Abu Hanifa to avoid breaking the oath: grinding the bread and mixing it into porridge until it is no longer recognisable as bread. The passage also addresses regional variations: a Bedouin accustomed to barley bread who moves to a wheat-bread town and swears not to eat bread is judged by his own custom, so barley bread breaks his oath. The discussion of thin bread (ruqaq) shows that only certain types are considered bread in Egyptian custom. The baker example distinguishes between the one who prepares the dough and the one who bakes; the oath applies to the baker's bread only if she performed the baking. The text relies on istihsan (juristic preference) based on custom, not explicit textual proof.

Key Terms
الحيلة (al-hilah): A permissible legal trick to avoid breaking an oath without lying, such as grinding bread into porridge.
العرف الخاص (al-urf al-khass): Individual custom specific to a person, as opposed to the general custom of the town.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is a permissible way to eat bread without breaking an oath not to eat it?
A) Eat a small piece.
B) Grind it into porridge until ruined.
C) Give it to someone else to eat.
D) Wash it down with water.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school provides a permissible way to avoid breaking an oath by transforming the object (hilah), and an individual's custom governs his oath when it differs from the general public.

Oaths Regarding Roasted and Cooked Food
Defining shawa' and tabikh in oaths, with focus on meat and custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default Hanafi meanings of 'roast' and 'cooked food' in oaths, identify the role of custom, and apply the ruling to scenarios including exceptions.

Core Idea
In oaths, 'roast' means roasted meat; 'cooked food' means meat in water, unless custom or intent expands it.

When someone swears not to eat 'roast' or 'cooked food', what exactly do those terms cover? The Hanafi school, using istihsan, relies on custom (urf) to determine the default meaning. This lesson unpacks the detailed definitions from the classical texts, including the scope of meat, broth, and even rice and lentils when cooked with specific fats.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous term in the oath
Recognize that the oath uses a general term like 'shawa'' or 'tabikh' which could apply to many foods. The legal question is its scope.
The oath binds the swearer to a specific category.
2
Apply the default custom-based interpretation
Hanafi law holds that 'shawa'' defaults to roasted meat, and 'tabikh' to meat cooked in water, based on prevalent custom. This is istihsan because literal generalization is impractical.
Exception: if the swearer intended a different meaning, that intention governs.
3
Consider exceptions and expansions
Note that 'tabikh' can extend to foods cooked with fat (wadak) like rice or lentils, as per some Hanafi sources (al-Khulasa). However, oil or ghee cooking does not count. Also, broth of meat breaks the oath.
Custom may vary by region; the mufta bihi is that custom determines the scope.
4
Distinguish related acts (who is the cook)
The text discusses that merely heating a pot or lighting a fire does not make one a cook. The cook is the one who adds spices and composes the dish.
This affects oaths tied to a specific person's cooking.
5
Apply to concrete cases
Use the rules to evaluate scenarios: e.g., eating roasted eggplant does not break an oath against 'roast'; eating lentils cooked in wadak may break an oath against 'cooked food'.
Always check local custom if in doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (والشواء والطبيخ على اللحم فإذا حلف لا يأكل الشواء لا يحنث إلا بأكل اللحم دون الباذنجان والجزر لأنه يراد به اللحم المشوي عند الإطلاق إلا أن ينوي ما يشوى من بيض وغيره لمكان الحقيقة وكذا إذا حلف لا يأكل الطبيخ فهو على ما يطبخ من اللحم وهذا استحسان اعتباراً للعرف، وهذا لأن التعميم متعذر فيصرف إلى خاص وهو متعارف وهو اللحم المطبوخ بالماء إلا إذا نوى غير ذلك لأن فيه تشديداً، وإن أكل من مرقه يحنث لما فيه من أجزاء اللحم ولأنه طبيخاً وإن كان لا يسمى يسمى لحماً كما قدمناه. وفي البدائع: حلف لا يأكل من طبيخ امرأته فسخنت له قدراً قد طبخها غيره أنه لا يحنث لأن الطبخ فعل من طبخ وهو الفعل الذي يسهل به أكل اللحم وذلك وجد من الأول لا منها اهـ. وفي التجريد: قيل اسم الطبخ يقع بوضع القدر لا بإيقاد النار، وقيل لو أوقد غيرها فوضعت هي القدر لا يحنث. اهـ. وفي عرفنا ليس واضع القدر طابخاً قطعاً ومجرد الإيقاد كذلك، ومثله يسمى صبي الطباخ يعني معينه والطباخ هو المركب بوضع التوابل وإن لم يوقد؛ كذا في فتح القدير. ويرد على المصنف شيئان الأول أن الطبيخ ليس هو اللحم خاصة وإنما هو ما يطبخ بالماء من اللحم حتى إن ما يتخذ قلية من اللحم لا يسمى طبيخاً فلا يحنث به كما صرح به في التبيين وغيره فإن قيل إنه أراد به المطبوخ بالماء قلنا: لا ذلك في الشواء لأنه لا يصح يحنث فيه إذا أكل لحماً مطبوخاً بالماء لأن اللحم المشوي هو الذي لم يطبخ بالماء وقد جعلهما واحداً. والثاني أن الطبيخ لا يختص بالمطبوخ من اللحم لما في الخلاصة أنه يحنث بالأرز إذا طبخ بودك، وكذا العدس كما في الظهيرية بخلاف ما إذا طبخ بزيت أو سمن. قال ابن سماعة: الطبيخ يقع على الشحم أيضاً. زاد في البدائع أنه يقع على ما طبخ بالألية أيضاً. قال في فتح القدير: ولا شك أن اللحم بالماء طبيخ وإنما الكلام في أنه المتعارف الظاهر أنه لا يختص به اهـ. وأشار المصنف رحمه الله إلى أنه لو أكل سمكاً مطبوخاً لا يحنث لأنه لا طبيخاً في العرف كما صرح به في البدائع. وفي المغرب: الودك من الشحم أو اللحم ما تحلب منه وقول الفقهاء ودك الميتة من ذلك اهـ. وحاصله أنه الدهن الخاص وهو دهن الشحم أو اللحم. قال في تهذيب القلانسي: وما يطبخ الأدهان يسمى مزورة اهـ. ومراده غير دهن اللحم والشحم كما قدمناه فعلى هذا لو حلف لا يأكل طبيخاً لا يحنث بأكل المزروة التي تفعل للمريض.
His statement: (And roast and cooked food are concerning meat. If he swears not to eat roast, he only breaks his oath by eating meat, not by eggplant or carrots, because what is meant at absolute is roasted meat unless he intends something that is roasted like eggs etc. due to the literal meaning. Likewise, if he swears not to eat cooked food, it applies to what is cooked from meat. This is istihsan considering custom. This is because generalization is impossible, so it is diverted to a specific that is customary, which is meat cooked in water unless he intends otherwise because there is severity in it. If he eats its broth, he breaks his oath because of the parts of meat in it and because it is cooked food even if it is not called meat as we mentioned earlier. In al-Bada'i: He swore not to eat from his wife's cooked food, so she heated a pot that someone else had cooked; he does not break his oath because cooking is the act of the one who cooks, which is the action that facilitates eating meat, and that was done by the first, not her. In al-Tajrid: It was said the name of cooking occurs by placing the pot, not by lighting the fire. And it was said if someone else lit the fire and she placed the pot, he does not break his oath. In our custom, the one who places the pot is definitely not the cook, nor is the mere lighting of the fire. Someone like that is called the cook's assistant, i.e., his helper; the cook is the one who composes by adding spices even if he did not light the fire; thus in Fath al-Qadir. Two objections are raised against the author: First, cooked food is not exclusively meat; rather it is what is cooked in water from meat, such that what is made as a qaliyya (fried dish) of meat is not called cooked food so he does not break his oath by it, as clarified in al-Tabyin and others. If it is said that he meant by it what is cooked in water, we say that is not so in roast because it is not correct that he breaks his oath when eating meat cooked in water, because roasted meat is that which is not cooked in water, and he made them one. Second, cooked food is not exclusive to meat cooked [in water] because in al-Khulasa it is stated that he breaks his oath by rice if cooked with fat (wadak), and similarly lentils as in al-Zahiriyyah, unlike if cooked with oil or ghee. Ibn Sama'ah said: Cooked food applies to fat as well. Added in al-Bada'i that it applies to what is cooked with tail fat. In Fath al-Qadir: There is no doubt that meat with water is cooked food; but the discussion is whether it is the customary one; the apparent view is that it is not exclusive to it. The author indicated that if he eats fish cooked, he does not break his oath because it is not called cooked food in custom as clarified in al-Bada'i. In al-Mughrib: Wadak is from fat or meat, what exudes from it; and the jurists' statement 'wadak of a dead animal' is from that. Its summary: it is specific fat, which is fat of suet or meat. In Tahdhib al-Qalanisi: What is cooked with oils is called muzawwara. Its meaning is other than fat of meat and suet as we mentioned; so upon this, if he swears not to eat cooked food, he does not break his oath by eating muzawwara that is prepared for the sick.
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage continues the chapter on oaths, now focusing on roast (shawa') and cooked food (tabikh). The Hanafi position, based on istihsan considering custom, is that when one swears not to eat 'roast', the default meaning is roasted meat, not other roasted items like vegetables, unless the swearer intended otherwise. Similarly, 'cooked food' defaults to meat cooked in water, not fried or dry-cooked dishes. Eating the broth of such meat breaks the oath because it contains meat particles and is considered part of the cooked food. The text addresses multiple aspects: (1) If a wife merely heats a pot that someone else cooked, the oath on her cooking is not broken because she did not perform the cooking act. (2) The definition of cooking: placing the pot or lighting the fire? Custom says the cook is the one who adds spices, not merely the assistant. (3) Two objections to the author's restriction: first, tabikh is not only meat but includes other items like rice and lentils when cooked with fat (wadak); second, tabikh is not exclusive to meat cooked in water—even fat can be called tabikh. However, fish cooked in water is not called tabikh in custom. The passage also clarifies that wadak is fat from meat or suet, and oil-based dishes (muzawwara) for the sick are not tabikh. The ruling relies on custom (urf) as a source of istihsan, showing that Hanafi fiqh prioritizes customary usage in interpreting ambiguous terms in oaths.

Key Terms
الشواء (al-shawa'): Roasted meat, by default; unless the swearer intends otherwise.
الطبيخ (al-tabikh): Meat cooked in water; can extend to other foods cooked with fat (wadak) but not oil or ghee.
الودك (al-wadak): Fat that exudes from meat or suet; used in cooking.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man swears not to eat 'cooked food' (tabikh), and then eats lentils cooked with fat (wadak), does he break his oath according to the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, because tabikh includes all foods cooked with fat.
B) No, because tabikh is only meat cooked in water.
C) Yes, according to some Hanafi sources like al-Khulasa, if cooked with wadak.
D) No, unless he specifically intended lentils.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, the meaning of ambiguous food terms like 'roast' and 'cooked food' is determined by custom (urf), not literal generality, with specific exceptions for items cooked with fat (wadak).

What Counts as 'Food' in Oaths: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Vinegar
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the definition of 'food' in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between food, medicine, and drink in the context of oaths, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding vinegar.

Core Idea
In oaths, 'food' is defined by custom, not literal edibility; vinegar's status is disputed between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

When a person swears not to eat 'food,' what exactly does that include? In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, we enter an intra-Hanafi debate: does vinegar count as food? Imam Muhammad says yes; Imam Abu Yusuf says no. The answer lies in understanding how custom ('urf) shapes the meaning of terms in legal contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central issue
Recognize that the passage addresses what breaks an oath not to eat 'food.'
The author restricts previous discussion to cooked food, then broadens to general food oaths.
2
Apply the general rule
Common food items like bread and fruit break the oath; medicines like scammony do not.
Medicines are excluded even if they have taste.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Muhammad holds that vinegar is food (breaking the oath); Imam Abu Yusuf holds it is not.
This is recorded in al-Khaniyyah and al-Zahiriyyah.
4
Understand the role of custom
Al-Quduri reconciles: literal meaning is broad, but custom restricts. The mufta bihi follows Abu Yusuf, dependent on local custom.
Also note: drinks like nabidh are not food, and small amounts of food still break the oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيد المصنف بالطبيخ لأنه لو حلف لا يأكل طعاماً فأكل خبزاً أو فاكهة أو غير ذلك مما يؤكل على وجه التطعم كان حانثاً، وإن أكل ما له طعم لكن لا يؤكل على وجه التطعم كالسقمونيا ونحو ذلك لا يحنث في يمينه. كذا في الخانية. وفي الظهيرية: حلف لا يأكل طعاماً فأكل ملحاً أو خلاً أو كامخاً أو زيتاً يحنث في يمينه، هكذا رواه ابن رستم عن محمد وقال: كل شيء يؤكل فهو طعام فقد جعل محمد الخل طعاماً. وقال أبو يوسف: الخل ليس بطعام. قال القدوري في كتابه: وحقيقة الطعام ما يطعم ولكن يختص في العرف ببعض الأشياء، فإن السقمونيا وما أشبه ذلك من الأدوية الكريهة لا تسمى طعاماً. اهـ. وفي البدائع: لو حلف لا يأكل طعاماً فأكل شيئاً يسيراً يحنث لأن قليل الطعام طعام. وفي المحيط: لو حلف لا يأكل من طعام فلان فأكل من نبيذه لم يحنث والنبيذ شراب.
The author restricted [the discussion] to cooked food because if he swore not to eat food and ate bread, fruit, or other things eaten for nourishment, he would be breaking his oath. If he ate something with taste but not eaten for nourishment, like scammony and the like, he does not break his oath. Thus in al-Khaniyyah. In al-Zahiriyyah: He swore not to eat food and ate salt, vinegar, khamkh (pickled condiment), or oil; he breaks his oath. Thus narrated by Ibn Rustom from Muhammad, who said: Everything that is eaten is food; so Muhammad considered vinegar food. Abu Yusuf said: Vinegar is not food. Al-Quduri said in his book: The literal meaning of food is what is eaten, but it is specified in custom by some things; scammony and similar unpleasant medicines are not called food. In al-Bada'i: If he swore not to eat food and ate a small amount of something, he breaks his oath because a little food is food. In al-Muhit: If he swore not to eat from so-and-so's food and drank his nabidh (wine), he does not break his oath because nabidh is a drink.
Source pages p. 542

Commentary
This passage concludes the section on oaths about food. The author restricts the preceding discussion to cooked food because the general term 'food' (ta'am) has its own rulings. The Hanafi position: if one swears not to eat food, he breaks it by eating common food items like bread or fruit, but not by eating medicines like scammony (a laxative) because they are not considered food in custom. The passage highlights an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani) considers vinegar to be food, so eating it breaks an oath not to eat food. Imam Abu Yusuf disagrees, stating vinegar is not food. Al-Quduri reconciles by noting that the lexical meaning of 'ta'am' is broader, but custom restricts it; medicines are excluded. Another ruling: eating a small amount of food still breaks the oath. Finally, if one swears not to eat from a specific person's food, drinking his nabidh (wine) does not break the oath because nabidh is classified as a drink, not food. This distinction between food and drink is important in Hanafi oath jurisprudence.

Key Terms
الطعام (al-ta'am): Everything commonly eaten for nourishment, excluding medicines and drinks like nabidh.
السقمونيا (al-saqmuniya): Scammony, a medicinal herb with a bitter taste, not considered food.
النبيذ (al-nabidh): An alcoholic drink made from dates or grapes; not considered food.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears not to eat 'food' and then eats a small amount of salt. According to the text from al-Zahiriyyah and the Hanafi mufta bihi, which statement is true?
A) He breaks his oath because salt is edible.
B) He does not break his oath because salt is a mineral.
C) He breaks his oath only if the local custom considers salt a food.
D) He does not break his oath because salt is not mentioned in the text.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oath jurisprudence, the definition of 'food' is governed by custom, not literal edibility; this resolves the intra-madhhab dispute on vinegar.

Definition of 'Cooked Meat' (طبيخ) in Oaths
Defines what constitutes طبيخ for oaths based on custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi reliance on custom ('urf) to determine what constitutes 'طبيخ' in oaths and state the subsidiary rulings including broth, rice with fat, fish, and the role of intention.

Core Idea
In oaths, 'طبيخ' means meat cooked with water — custom overrides literal generality.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the definition of 'طبيخ' (cooked meat) in the context of oaths. The Hanafi school employs istihsan to defer to customary usage ('urf) when the literal meaning is too broad. You will learn not only the default definition but also the nuances: broth is included, rice cooked with animal fat triggers hanth, fish is excluded, and intra-Hanafi differences over fat and tail fat. The overarching principle is that local custom determines the scope.

Guided Path
1
State the primary ruling
Identify the default definition of 'طبيخ' based on custom: meat cooked with water.
This is the starting point; intention can override.
2
Explore included items
Broth is included because it contains meat particles. Rice and lentils cooked with animal fat (wadak) are also included.
The key is the presence of meat or animal fat, not just water.
3
Identify excluded items
Fish cooked with water is excluded by custom. Dishes cooked with oil or clarified butter (e.g., muzawwara) are not 'طبيخ'.
Oils other than wadak do not render food 'طبيخ'.
4
Analyze subsidiary scenarios
If a wife heats a pot cooked by another, she is not considered the cook. Placing the pot alone does not constitute cooking in custom.
Cooking is attributed to the one who actually performs the act that makes meat easy to eat.
5
Recognize intra-Hanafi differences
Ibn Sama'a includes fat and tail fat under 'طبيخ', while the author restricts it to meat. The mufta bihi is that local custom resolves the difference.
Custom is the ultimate arbiter, not the classical texts alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا استحسان اعتباراً للعرف، وهذا لأن التعميم متعذر فيصرف إلى خاص وهو متعارف وهو اللحم المطبوخ بالماء إلا إذا نوى غير ذلك لأن فيه تشديداً، وإن أكل من مرقه يحنث لما فيه من أجزاء اللحم ولأنه طبيخاً وإن كان لا يسمى يسمى لحماً كما قدمناه. وفي البدائع : حلف لا يأكل من طبيخ امرأته فسخنت له قدراً قد طبخها غيرها أنه لا يحنث لأن الطبخ فعل من طبخ وهو الفعل الذي يسهل به أكل اللحم وذلك وجد من الأول لا منها اهـ. وفي التجريد : قيل اسم الطبخ يقع بوضع القدر لا بإيقاد النار، وقيل لو أوقد غيرها فوضعت هي القدر لا يحنث .اهـ. وفي عرفنا ليس واضع القدر طابخاً قطعاً ومجرد الإيقاد كذلك، ومثله يسمى صبي الطباخ يعني معينه والطباخ هو المركب بوضع التوابل وإن لم يوقد ؛ كذا في فتح القدير . .. ويرد على المصنف شيئان الأول أن الطبيخ ليس هو اللحم خاصة وإنما هو ما يطبخ بالماء من اللحم حتى إن ما يتخذ قلية من اللحم لا يسمى طبيخاً فلا يحنث به كما صرح به في التبيين وغيره فإن قيل إنه أراد به المطبوخ بالماء قلنا : لا ذلك في الشواء لأنه لا يصح يحنث فيه إذا أكل لحماً مطبوخاً بالماء لأن اللحم المشوي هو الذي لم يطبخ بالماء وقد جعلهما واحداً. والثاني أن الطبيخ لا يختص بالمطبوخ من اللحم لما في الخلاصة أنه يحنث بالأرز إذا طبخ بودك، وكذا العدس كما في الظهيرية بخلاف ما إذا طبخ بزيت أو سمن. قال ابن سماعة : الطبيخ يقع على الشحم أيضاً . زاد في البدائع أنه يقع على ما طبخ بالألية أيضاً. قال في فتح القدير : ولا شك أن اللحم بالماء طبيخ وإنما الكلام في أنه المتعارف الظاهر أنه لا يختص به اهـ. وأشار المصنف رحمه الله إلى أنه لو أكل سمكاً مطبوخاً لا يحنث لأنه لا طبيخاً . في العرف كما صرح به في البدائع. وفي المغرب : الودك من الشحم أو اللحم ما تحلب منه وقول الفقهاء ودك الميتة من ذلك اهـ. وحاصله أنه الدهن الخاص وهو دهن الشحم أو اللحم. قال في تهذيب القلانسي: وما يطبخ الأدهان يسمى مزورة اهـ. ومراده غير دهن اللحم والشحم كما قدمناه فعلى هذا لو حلف لا يأكل طبيخاً لا يحنث بأكل المزروة التي تفعل للمريض.
And this is istihsan, considering custom. And this is because generalization is impossible, so it is diverted to a specific which is customary, and it is meat cooked with water unless he intended otherwise, because there is strictness in that. And if he eats from its broth, he breaks his oath because of the parts of meat in it, and because it is cooked [food] even if it is not called meat as we have mentioned. In al-Bada'i': If he swore not to eat from his wife's cooking, and she heated a pot for him that another had cooked, he does not break his oath because cooking is the act of the one who cooked, and it is the act that makes eating meat easy, and that was found from the first, not from her. In al-Tajrid: It is said that the name 'cooking' applies by placing the pot, not by lighting the fire. And it is said that if another lit the fire and she placed the pot, he does not break his oath. In our custom, the one who places the pot is definitely not a cook, and mere lighting is likewise. And like that, one called the cook's boy, meaning his assistant, and the cook is the one who combines by placing spices even if he did not light the fire; thus in Fath al-Qadir. Two objections are raised against the author: First, that cooked meat is not specifically meat, but rather what is cooked with water from meat, such that meat prepared as a 'qaliyya' (dry-fried) is not called cooked meat, so he does not break his oath, as stated in al-Tabyin and others. If it is said that he meant cooked with water, we say: Not so, because roasted meat, if he eats meat cooked with water, is incorrect because roasted meat is that which has not been cooked with water, and he made them one. Second, that cooked meat is not specific to cooked meat from meat, because in al-Khulasa: He breaks his oath with rice if cooked with wadak (fat), and similarly lentils as in al-Zahiriyya, contrary to if cooked with oil or clarified butter. Ibn Sama'a said: Cooked meat applies to fat as well. It was added in al-Bada'i' that it applies to what is cooked with tail fat as well. He said in Fath al-Qadir: There is no doubt that meat with water is cooked meat; the discussion is whether it is customary, and apparently it is not specific to it. And the author (may Allah have mercy on him) indicated that if he ate cooked fish, he does not break his oath because it is not called cooked meat in custom, as stated in al-Bada'i'. And in al-Maghrib: 'Wadak' from fat or meat is what drips from them, and the jurists' saying 'wadak of carrion' is from that. Its summary: that it is specific fat, and it is the fat of fat or meat. He said in Tahdhib al-Qalanisi: What is cooked with oils is called muzawwara. And his intent is other than the fat of meat and fat as we have mentioned. So on this basis, if he swore not to eat cooked meat, he does not break his oath by eating the muzawwara that is made for the sick.
Source pages p. 542

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the definition of 'طبيخ' (cooked meat) in the context of oaths. The author begins by stating that the default interpretation for 'طبيخ' in oaths is based on custom (عرف) rather than literal definition, because it is impossible to generalize all forms of cooking. Thus, the term is restricted to the customary meaning: meat cooked with water. However, if the person intended a specific type, that intention is followed due to strictness. Broth (مرق) is included because it contains meat particles and is considered 'طبيخ' even if not called meat. Several subsidiary rulings are then cited: in al-Bada'i', if a man swore not to eat his wife's cooking, and she merely heated a pot cooked by another, he does not break his oath because the cooking act was not hers. In al-Tajrid, there is a difference over whether placing the pot alone constitutes 'طبخ' (cooking), but the custom is that the one who places the pot is not considered a cook. Two objections are raised against the author's limitation of 'طبيخ' to meat: first, that some meat preparations like 'قلية' (dry-fried) are not called 'طبيخ', so they should not cause oath-breaking; second, that 'طبيخ' can include rice and lentils cooked with fat (ودك), as opposed to oil or clarified butter. Ibn Sama'a extends it to fat and tail fat. The author then clarifies that fish cooked with water is not called 'طبيخ' in custom, so it does not break the oath. Finally, 'مزورة' (a medicinal preparation made with oils) is not included because it uses oils other than meat fat. The overarching principle is that custom ('urf) controls the interpretation of such terms in oaths.

Key Terms
طبيخ (tabīkh): In oaths, what is customarily considered cooked meat, typically meat cooked with water.
ودك (wadak): The fat that drips from meat or fat; used in cooking.
عرف ('urf): Custom, used as the basis for interpreting terms in oaths when literal meaning is too broad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears 'I will not eat your cooking,' and his wife heats a pot that her mother cooked. Does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because the pot contains food.
B) Yes, because his wife heated it.
C) No, because his wife did not cook the food.
D) No, because he did not eat.

Key Takeaway
Custom ('urf) is the decisive factor in interpreting 'طبيخ' for oaths, not literal definition.
