Rulings on Oath Regarding 'Food' (طعام)
Lists intra-Hanafi dispute and specific rulings on oaths regarding 'food'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on what breaks an oath not to eat 'food', including the intra-madhhab dispute between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding vinegar and nabidh.

Core Idea
The Hanafi definition of food in oaths depends on custom; vinegar and nabidh are disputed between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq details the rulings on oaths involving 'food' (طعام). It establishes that anything customarily eaten for nourishment breaks the oath, while medicines and non-nourishing items do not. The passage also highlights an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Muhammad considers vinegar and nabidh as food, while Abu Yusuf does not. Additionally, special cases like small amounts, medicine with taste, mixed food, and duress are addressed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The passage states that anything eaten for nourishment (تطعم) breaks the oath. Examples include bread and fruit.
2
Recognize the exception for non-nourishing items
Items with taste but not eaten for nourishment, like scammony (سقمونيا), do not break the oath.
3
Understand the custom-based definition
Al-Quduri explains that 'food' is customarily specific; some items like salt and vinegar are considered food by default.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Compare Muhammad’s view (all edible items are food) with Abu Yusuf’s view (vinegar and nabidh are not food). Note the practical consequence.
This dispute affects whether the oath is broken.
5
Apply the rulings to special cases
Small amounts count, nabidh is disputed, medicine with sweetness breaks the oath, mixing with another's food breaks the oath, and carrion under duress does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيد المصنف بالطبيخ لأنه لو حلف لا يأكل طعاماً فأكل خبزاً أو فاكهة أو غير ذلك مما يؤكل على وجه التطعم كان حانثاً، وإن أكل ما له طعم لكن لا يؤكل على وجه التطعم كالسقمونيا ونحو ذلك لا يحنث في يمينه . كذا في الخانية وفي الظهيرية : حلف لا يأكل طعاماً فأكل ملحاً أو خلاً أو كامخاً أو زيتاً يحنث في يمينه، هكذا رواه ابن رستم عن محمد وقال : كل شيء يؤكل فهو طعام فقد جعل محمد الخل طعاماً. وقال أبو يوسف : الخل ليس بطعام. قال القدوري في كتابه : وحقيقة الطعام ما يطعم ولكن يختص في العرف ببعض الأشياء، فإن السقمونيا وما أشبه ذلك من الأدوية الكريهة لا تسمى طعاماً .اهـ. وفي البدائع : لو حلف لا يأكل طعاماً فأكل شيئاً يسيراً يحنث لأن قليل الطعام طعام وفي المحيط : لو حلف لا يأكل من طعام فلان فأكل من نبيذه لم يحنث والنبيذ شراب يسمى عند أبي يوسف . وقال محمد هو طعام. ولو حلف لا يشتري طعاماً ما لا يحنث إلا بشراء الحنطة والدقيق والخبز استحساناً، وفي الواقعات : حلف لا يأكل طعاماً فأكل دواء إن كان من الدواء الذي لا يكون له طعم ولا يكون غداء ويكون مراً كريهاً لا يحنث لأنه لا طعاماً، وإن كان دواء له حلاوة مثل الحلنجبين يحنث لأن له طعماً ويكون به غداء. حلف لا يأكل من طعام فلان فأكل من خله بطعام نفسه أو بزيته أو بملحه حنث لأنه أكل من طعامه . وفي البدائع: حلف لا يأكل طعاماً فاضطر إلى أكل ميتة فأكل منها لم يحنث .
The author restricted [the discussion] to cooked meat because if he swore not to eat food and ate bread, fruit, or other things that are eaten for nourishment, he breaks his oath. And if he ate something that has taste but is not eaten for nourishment, like scammony (sakamuniya) and the like, he does not break his oath. Thus in al-Khaniyya. And in al-Zahiriyya: If he swore not to eat food and ate salt, vinegar, kāmkha, or oil, he breaks his oath. Thus Ibn Rustom narrated from Muhammad, and he said: Everything that is eaten is food. So Muhammad considered vinegar food. And Abu Yusuf said: Vinegar is not food. Al-Quduri said in his book: The reality of food is that which is eaten, but customarily it is specific to certain things. Scammony and similar unpleasant medicines are not called food. In al-Bada'i': If he swore not to eat food and ate a small amount, he breaks his oath because a little of food is food. And in al-Muhit: If he swore not to eat from so-and-so's food and drank from his nabidh (date drink), he does not break his oath, and nabidh is called a drink according to Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: It is food. And if he swore not to buy food, he does not break his oath except by buying wheat, flour, and bread by istihsan. And in al-Waqi'at: If he swore not to eat food and ate medicine, if it is medicine that has no taste, is not a meal, and is bitter and unpleasant, he does not break his oath because it is not food. And if the medicine has sweetness like halanjabin (a sweetened medicine), he breaks his oath because it has taste and is used as a meal. If he swore not to eat from so-and-so's food and ate from his vinegar with his own food, or with his oil, or with his salt, he breaks his oath because he ate from his food. And in al-Bada'i': If he swore not to eat food and was forced to eat carrion and ate from it, he does not break his oath.
Source pages p. 542

Commentary
This passage presents several rulings regarding oaths not to eat 'طعام' (food). The author of the matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) restricts the earlier discussion because the ruling for 'طبيخ' differs from 'طعام'. The general principle is that anything commonly eaten for nourishment (تطعم) qualifies as 'طعام', so bread and fruit break the oath. However, medicines like scammony, which are not eaten for sustenance, do not break the oath. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Muhammad considers vinegar and nabidh (date drink) as food, while Abu Yusuf disagrees. Al-Quduri clarifies that the term 'طعام' is customarily restricted. A small amount of food still counts. If the food is mixed with another's vinegar, oil, or salt, the oath is broken because he ate from that person's property. Eating carrion under duress does not break the oath because it is not normally considered food. These rulings highlight the importance of custom and intent in oaths.

Key Terms
طعام (ṭa'ām): In oaths, what is customarily eaten for nourishment, excluding medicines and forced items.
نبيذ (nabīdh): A drink made from dates or raisins; considered food by Muhammad, drink by Abu Yusuf.
سقمونيا (saqamūniyā): Scammony, a bitter medicinal herb; not considered food in oaths.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No, because it is medicine.
B) Yes, because it has taste and is used as a meal.
C) No, because it is not called food.
D) Yes, because all liquids are food.

Key Takeaway
The definition of 'food' in oaths is based on custom, and intra-madhhab disputes exist on borderline items like vinegar and nabidh.

Defining 'Head' in Oaths: The Role of Custom
Defines 'head' in oaths by local custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the meaning of 'رأس' in oaths based on local custom and apply the Hanafi rule to determine whether an oath is broken.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oaths, 'head' means only what is customarily sold and eaten in the oath-taker's town.

When someone swears not to eat 'head,' what exactly are they promising? Is it any head, or only certain ones? The Hanafi school holds that the answer depends on local custom. This lesson unpacks the definition of 'رأس' in oaths, the intra-Hanafi difference on which animals are included, and why custom overrides literal meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Term and its Context
Recognize that the passage defines 'رأس' (head) specifically in the context of oaths (الأيمان). The legal question is: when someone swears not to eat a head, what counts as a head?
The term appears in fiqh chapters on oaths and expiation.
2
Identify the Primary Determinant
The text states that the oath refers to what is sold in the oath-taker's town (مصره). This makes local custom (العرف) the primary determinant, not a universal literal definition.
The author emphasizes 'ما يباع في مصره' – what is sold in his town.
3
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
There is a difference: Imam Abu Hanifa includes heads of cattle and sheep; the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) restrict to sheep only. This difference is attributed to changing customs over time.
The text says 'وهو اختلاف عصر' – a difference of era.
4
Identify the Mufta Bihi (Operative Position)
The mufta bihi is what is customary in each locality. The mufti must base the fatwa on the custom of the town where the oath occurred.
The author says 'فوجب على المفتي إن يفتي بما هو المعتاد في كل مصر'.
5
Apply to a Scenario
Given a scenario (e.g., a man in a town where only sheep heads are sold swears not to eat head), determine if eating a cow head breaks the oath. Apply the principle: if cow heads are not sold locally, the oath is not broken.
Excluded heads: those not customarily sold, e.g., pork or human flesh, even if called 'head'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والرأس ما يباع في مصره) فلو حلف لا يأكل رأساً انصرفت يمينه إلى ما يكبس في التنانير في تلك البلدة وتباع فيها من رؤوس الإبل والبقر والغنم وهو المراد بقوله ما يباع في مصره أي من الرؤوس غير نيء. وخصه في الجامع الصغير برؤوس البقر والغنم عند الإمام، وعندهما بالغنم خاصة وهو اختلاف ،عصر، وفي زماننا هو خاص بالغنم فوجب على المفتي إن يفتي بما هو المعتاد في كل مصر وقع فيه حلف الحالف كما أفاده في المختصر . وما في التبيين من أن الأصل اعتبار الحقيقة اللغوية إن أمكن العمل بها وإلا فالعرف إلى آخره مردود لأن الاعتبار إنما هو للعرف، وتقدم أن الفتوى على أنه لا يحنث بأكل لحم الخنزير والآدمي ولذا قال في فتح القدير ولو كان هذا الأصل المذكور منظوراً إليه لما تجاسر أحد على خلافه في الفروع وفي البدائع والاعتماد إنما هو على العرف وبما ذكرناه اندفع ما ذكره الأسبيجابي أنه في الأكل يقع على الكل إذا أكل ما يسمى رأساً، وفي الشراء يقع على الرأس البقر والغنم عنده وعندهما على الغنم خاصة، ولا يقع على رأس الإبل بالإجماع لما علمت أنه في الأكل خاص بما يباع في مصره. وفي المغرب يكبس في التنور يطم به التنور أو يدخل فيه من كبس الرجل رأسه في قميصه إذا أدخله
His saying: 'And the head is what is sold in his town.' So if he swore not to eat a head, his oath refers to what is stuffed in ovens in that town and sold there from heads of camels, cattle, and sheep. That is what is meant by his saying 'what is sold in his town' — meaning from heads, not raw. In al-Jami' al-Saghir, it is specified as heads of cattle and sheep according to Imam [Abu Hanifa], and according to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] specifically sheep, and this is a difference of era. And in our time, it is specific to sheep, so the mufti must give fatwa according to what is customary in each town where the oath occurred, as al-Mukhtasar stated. And what is in al-Tabyin that the principle is to consider the literal meaning if possible, otherwise custom, is rejected because consideration is only for custom. And it has been stated that the fatwa is that one does not break his oath by eating pork or human flesh. Hence he said in Fath al-Qadir: If this mentioned principle were regarded, no one would dare to oppose it in the branches. And in al-Bada'i', reliance is only on custom. And by what we have mentioned, the objection of al-Asbijabi is repelled: that in eating, it applies to everything if one eats what is called a head, and in buying, it applies to the head of cattle and sheep according to him, and according to the two specifically sheep, and it does not apply to camel's head by consensus, because we know that in eating it is specific to what is sold in his town. And in al-Maghrib: 'yukbas' in the oven means covering the oven with it or entering it, from 'kabasa' the man his head into his shirt when he inserts it.
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'رأس' (head) in the context of oaths not to eat head. The primary determinant is local custom: what heads are commonly sold and eaten in the oath-taker's town. In al-Jami' al-Saghir, there is a difference: Imam Abu Hanifa includes heads of cattle and sheep, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad restrict to sheep only. This difference is attributed to changing customs over time. In the present era (of the author), the custom is sheep, so the mufti should base the fatwa on what is customary in each locality. The text criticizes the approach in al-Tabyin that starts with literal meaning, asserting that the reliable principle is to rely entirely on custom, as stated in Fath al-Qadir and al-Bada'i'. The objection of al-Asbijabi — that in eating, any head qualifies, while in buying it is restricted — is refuted by the fact that eating is also restricted to what is sold locally. Pork and human flesh are excluded by default, as they are not customarily eaten. The term 'يكبس' (yukbas) is explained as stuffing into an oven.

Key Terms
رأس (ra's): In oaths, refers to the cooked heads of animals sold in the local market, typically sheep.
مصر (miṣr): The town or locality of the oath-taker; determines the applicable custom.
يكبس (yukbas): To stuff into an oven (for cooking).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, what determines whether eating a certain animal's head breaks an oath not to eat 'head'?
A) The literal dictionary definition of 'head'.
B) The type of animal (e.g., sheep vs. camel).
C) What is customarily sold and eaten in the oath-taker's town.
D) The opinion of the nearest mufti.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, the meaning of 'head' is determined by local custom, not by universal definition, and the mufti must issue fatwa based on the custom of the locality where the oath was made.

Defining 'Fruit' in Oaths: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Custom and Enjoyment
Definition of 'fruit' with intra-Hanafi dispute and custom-based tarjih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'fruit' in oaths, explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the tarjih based on custom to determine which items break an oath.

Core Idea
Fruit in oaths is defined by customary enjoyment; the Sahibayn's inclusive view is mufta bihi today due to changed custom.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the definition of 'fruit' (fākiha) in oaths not to eat fruit. Imam Abu Hanifa limited fruit to items like apples, watermelon, and apricots, excluding grapes, pomegranates, and fresh dates. His disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, included those items because they are prized and enjoyed. The later Hanafi tradition resolves this by appealing to custom: the ruling changes with time, and in our era, eating any of these would break the oath. Understanding this dispute teaches us how 'urf influences legal definitions.

Guided Path
1
State the general principle
Identify the definition of fruit (fākiha) as what is customarily enjoyed before/after meals beyond the usual, and that it can be fresh or dry.
This is agreed upon by all Hanafi imams.
2
Identify the disputed items
List items that all agree are fruit (apples, watermelon, apricots) and those disputed (grapes, pomegranates, fresh dates). Note that cucumbers and gherkins are excluded by consensus.
The dispute is only over a narrow set.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Understand why Abu Hanifa excludes the disputed items: they are also used for nutrition and medicine, weakening the 'enjoyment' aspect, and their dried forms become staples or spices.
He focuses on primary purpose.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's reasoning
Understand why Abu Yusuf and Muhammad include them: they are among the most prized fruits and give great enjoyment, satisfying the definition.
They prioritize customary enjoyment over other uses.
5
Apply the tarjih based on custom
Refer to al-Kashf al-Kabir's resolution: the difference is due to changing custom, and in our era, custom favors the Sahibayn's view, so the mufta bihi is that all these items are fruit.
This shows the role of 'urf in evolving fatwa.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والفاكهة التفاح والبطيخ والمشمش لا العنب والرمان والرطب والقثاء والخيار وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : يحنث في الرمان والعنب والرطب أيضاً، والأصل أن الفاكهة اسم لما يتفكه به قبل الطعام وبعده أي يتنعم به زيادة على المعتاد والرطب واليابس فيه سواء بعد أن يكون التفكه به معتاداً حتى يحنث بيابس البطيخ وهذا المعنى موجود في التفاح وأخواتها فيحنث بها وغير موجود في القثاء والخيار لأنهما من البقول بيعاً وأكلاً فلا يحنث بهما . وأما العنب والرطب والرمان فهما يقولان معنى التفكه موجود فيهما فإنها أعز الفواكه والتنعم بها يفوق التنعم بغيرها، وأبو حنيفة يقول إن هذه الأشياء مما يتغذى بها ويتداوى بها فأوجب قصوراً في معنى التفكه في حالة البقاء، ولهذا كان اليابس منها من التوابل أو من الأقوات. وذكر في الكشف الكبير أن هذا اختلاف عصر وزمان فأبو حنيفة أفتى على حسب عرفه وتغير العرف في زمانهما وفي عرفنا ينبغي أن يحنث بالاتفاق ا هـ . وفي الظهيرية قال محمد في الأصل : للاستعمال
His saying: 'And fruit is apples, watermelon, and apricots, not grapes, pomegranates, fresh dates, cucumbers, and gherkins.' This is according to Abu Hanifa. And the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] said: He breaks his oath with pomegranates, grapes, and fresh dates as well. The principle is that fruit (fakiha) is a name for what one enjoys (yatafakkahu) before and after food, i.e., what is enjoyed beyond the usual, and fresh and dry are the same, provided that enjoying it is customary, such that he breaks his oath with dry watermelon. This meaning exists in apples and their likes, so he breaks his oath with them, and it is absent in cucumbers and gherkins because they are vegetables in trade and eating, so he does not break his oath with them. As for grapes, fresh dates, and pomegranates, the two say that the meaning of enjoyment exists in them because they are the most precious fruits, and enjoyment from them exceeds enjoyment from others. Abu Hanifa says that these things are used for nourishment and medicine, thus there is a deficiency in the meaning of enjoyment in the case of preservation, and therefore their dry forms are used as spices or staple foods. It is mentioned in al-Kashf al-Kabir that this is a difference of era and time: Abu Hanifa gave fatwa according to his custom, and the custom changed in their time, and in our custom he should break his oath by consensus. In al-Zahiriyya, Muhammad said in al-Asl: For usage...
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage addresses the definition of 'فاكهة' (fruit) in oaths not to eat fruit. Imam Abu Hanifa limits it to fruits like apples, watermelon, apricots, and excludes grapes, pomegranates, fresh dates, cucumbers, and gherkins. His two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) include pomegranates, grapes, and fresh dates as well, based on the principle that fruit is something enjoyed before or after meals. They argue that these items are among the most enjoyable fruits. Abu Hanifa counters that these items are also eaten as nourishment or medicine, so the enjoyment aspect is diminished, and their dried forms are used as spices or staples (not as fruit). Interestingly, all agree that cucumbers and gherkins are not fruit because they are treated as vegetables. The commentary in al-Kashf al-Kabir suggests that this difference is due to changing customs: Abu Hanifa's era had different practices, whereas in later times, the custom shifted so that the disciples' view may be more applicable. In the author's time, the fatwa is that he breaks his oath by consensus on all these items. This illustrates the impact of 'urf on legal rulings.

Key Terms
فاكهة (fākiha): In oaths, what is customarily enjoyed before or after meals as a luxury, excluding vegetables and staple foods.
تفكه (tafakkuh): Enjoying fruit or delicacies beyond regular meals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Cucumbers
B) Fresh dates
C) Neither
D) Both

Key Takeaway
In today's custom, any item commonly enjoyed as fruit before/after meals counts as fruit in oaths, following the Sahibayn's inclusive definition.

Defining Ta'am in Oaths: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Defining 'ta'am' and rulings on medicine, other's food, and carrion in oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the definitions of 'ta'am' held by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and apply these definitions to determine whether an oath is broken in cases involving medicine, condiments, and carrion.

Core Idea
An oath about food is defined by custom and nutrition—bitter medicine is not food, but carrion under necessity does not break the oath.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explores the scope of 'food' (ta'am) in oath contexts. The Hanafi school saw an internal disagreement: Abu Yusuf limited 'food' to staples, while Muhammad included condiments. The commentary applies these definitions to real scenarios—eating medicine, eating with another's condiment, and eating carrion under duress. Understanding these nuances is essential for precise fatwa on oath-breaking.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definitional dispute
Identify what is at issue: the definition of 'ta'am' and whether it includes condiments (idam).
The text contrasts Abu Yusuf's view (condiment is not food) with Muhammad's (it is food).
2
State each imam's position
Abu Yusuf says idam is not food; Muhammad says it is. Note that this is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
Later fatwa follows custom.
3
Apply to the medicine case
Determine when medicine breaks an oath: if it has no taste and no nourishment (bitter) it does not; if sweet and nutritious (halanjabin) it does.
The key is whether it serves as a meal (ghidha').
4
Apply to eating with another's condiment
Even if you use your own food, adding his condiment constitutes eating from his food, breaking the oath.
This shows that condiment is considered part of his food in a broader sense.
5
Apply to carrion under necessity
Eating carrion under compulsion (idtirar) does not break the oath because carrion is not normally considered food.
Necessity changes the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفاكهة التفاح والبطيخ والمشمش لا العنب والرمان الرطب والقثاء والخيار والادام ما يسمى عند أبي يوسف . وقال محمد هو طعام. ولو حلف لا يشتري طعاماً ما لا يحنث إلا بشراء الحنطة والدقيق والخبز استحساناً، وفي الواقعات : حلف لا يأكل طعاماً فأكل دواء إن كان من الدواء الذي لا يكون له طعم ولا يكون غداء ويكون مراً كريهاً لا يحنث لأنه لا طعاماً، وإن كان دواء له حلاوة مثل الحلنجبين يحنث لأن له طعماً ويكون به غداء. حلف لا يأكل من طعام فلان فأكل من خله بطعام نفسه أو بزيته أو بملحه حنث لأنه أكل من اهـ. وفي البدائع: حلف لا يأكل طعاماً فاضطر إلى أكل ميتة فأكل منها لم يحنث . طعامه
And fruit: apples, watermelon, apricots, not grapes, pomegranates, fresh dates, cucumbers, and cucumbers; and condiment is what is called (something) according to Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: it is food. If he swore not to buy food, he does not break his oath except by buying wheat, flour, and bread by istihsan. In al-Waqi'at: He swore not to eat food, so he ate medicine: if it is medicine that has no taste and is not a meal and is bitter and displeasing, he does not break his oath because it is not food. If it is medicine that has sweetness like halanjabin, he breaks his oath because it has taste and is a meal with it. He swore not to eat from the food of so-and-so, so he ate from his vinegar with his own food or his oil or his salt, he breaks his oath because he ate from his food. In al-Bada'i': He swore not to eat food, then was compelled to eat carrion and ate from it, he does not break his oath.
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
The passage begins with a summary of definitions from the matn for 'fruit' and 'condiment,' but the main focus here is on the definition of 'ta'am (food). Abu Yusuf defines condiment (idam) as something used for dipping, while Muhammad considers it food. The commentary then provides additional rulings: buying food is restricted to grains and bread by istihsan; eating medicine only breaks the oath if it is nutritious and palatable; eating from another's food with one's own condiment still counts; and eating carrion under necessity does not break the oath. These rulings clarify the scope of 'food' in oaths.

Key Terms
الطعام (al-ta'am): Anything that is eaten as nourishment, including condiments per Muhammad.
الإدام (al-idam): A condiment used to dip bread, e.g., vinegar, salt, oil.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) No, because it lacks taste and nourishment.
C) Yes, if it is sweet.
D) No, unless it is halanjabin.

Key Takeaway
The definition of 'ta'am' in oaths is not fixed; it depends on custom and the nutritive quality of the item, with carrion under necessity excluded.

Defining 'Ra's' in Oaths: Custom Overrides Linguistics
Defining 'ra's' by custom and intra-Hanafi differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that custom defines the scope of ambiguous terms in oaths, and explain the intra-madhhab difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on this issue.

Core Idea
In oaths, 'ra's' means what local custom sells, not every animal head.

When someone swears not to eat a 'head', which animals are covered? The Hanafi school teaches that legal interpretation must look to common practice, not dictionary definitions. This lesson unpacks an intra-Hanafi difference and shows how custom ('urf) becomes the decisive factor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal context and term
Recognize that the dispute is about interpreting the word 'ra's' (head) in an oath not to eat it. The matn states 'what is sold in his city' – a clue that custom matters.
The source is from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq.
2
State the intra-Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa held that 'ra's' includes heads of cows and sheep; his disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) restricted it to sheep only. This difference is recorded in al-Jami' al-Saghir.
Note that camel heads are universally excluded in the classical positions.
3
Understand the role of custom
The text declares that custom ('urf) is the ultimate reference, not linguistic meaning. Even if a head is linguistically a head, if it is not customarily sold and eaten in that locale, it is not covered by the oath.
The commentary refutes al-Asbijabi's view that linguistic meaning should be primary.
4
Apply the custom principle to determine the mufta bihi
The mufti must investigate what heads are commonly baked and sold in the swearer's city. The ruling then matches that custom. In the commentator's time, only sheep heads were common, so fatwa followed Sahibayn.
This shows the dynamic nature of Hanafi fiqh: rulings adapt to time and place.
5
Recognize the thamarah (practical consequence)
The practical outcome: in a city where only camel heads are sold, an oath covers only camel heads; where cow heads are also sold, it includes cows. The difference between the imams becomes irrelevant when custom is known.
The thamarah is that the oath's scope is determined by local market practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والرأس ما يباع في مصره) فلو حلف لا يأكل رأساً انصرفت يمينه إلى ما يكبس في التنانير في تلك البلدة وتباع فيها من رؤوس الإبل والبقر والغنم وهو المراد بقوله ما يباع في مصره أي من الرؤوس غير نيء. وخصه في الجامع الصغير برؤوس البقر والغنم عند الإمام، وعندهما بالغنم خاصة وهو اختلاف عصر، وفي زماننا هو خاص بالغنم فوجب على المفتي إن يفتي بما هو المعتاد في كل مصر وقع فيه حلف الحالف كما أفاده في المختصر . وما في التبيين من أن الأصل اعتبار الحقيقة اللغوية إن أمكن العمل بها وإلا فالعرف إلى آخره مردود لأن الاعتبار إنما هو للعرف، وتقدم أن الفتوى على أنه لا يحنث بأكل لحم الخنزير والآدمي ولذا قال في فتح القدير ولو كان هذا الأصل المذكور منظوراً إليه لما تجاسر أحد على خلافه في الفروع وفي البدائع والاعتماد إنما هو على العرف وبما ذكرناه اندفع ما ذكره الأسبيجابي أنه في الأكل يقع على الكل إذا أكل ما يسمى رأساً، وفي الشراء يقع على الرأس البقر والغنم عنده وعندهما على الغنم خاصة، ولا يقع على رأس الإبل بالإجماع لما علمت أنه في الأكل خاص بما يباع في مصره. وفي المغرب يكبس في التنور يطم به التنور أو يدخل فيه من كبس الرجل رأسه في قميصه إذا أدخله
His saying: (And the head is what is sold in his city). So if he swore not to eat a head, his oath applies to what is baked in ovens in that town and sold there from heads of camels, cows, and sheep, and that is the meaning of his saying 'what is sold in his city', i.e., from heads not raw. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir, he specified it as heads of cows and sheep according to al-Imam, and according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) specifically sheep, and it is a difference of time. And in our time, it is specific to sheep, so the mufti must give fatwa according to what is customary in each city where the swearer made his oath, as stated in al-Mukhtasar. What is in al-Tabyin that the principle is to consider the linguistic meaning if possible, otherwise custom, etc., is rejected because consideration is only for custom. And it has preceded that the fatwa is that he does not break his oath by eating pork or human flesh, and therefore it was said in Fath al-Qadir: If this mentioned principle were considered, no one would dare to oppose it in the branches. And in al-Bada'i', reliance is only on custom. And by what we have mentioned, the objection of al-Asbijabi is refuted: that in eating, it applies to all if he eats what is called a head, and in buying, it applies to the head of cows and sheep according to him, and according to them specifically sheep, and it does not apply to the head of camels by consensus, because you know that in eating it is specific to what is sold in his city. And in Morocco, they bake in the oven, covering the oven with it, or entering it, from 'kabasa' the man his head into his shirt when he inserts it.

Commentary
This section addresses the term 'ra's (head)' in oaths. The matn states that 'head' refers to what is sold in the swearer's city. The commentary explains that this means heads that are roasted in ovens and sold, including camel, cow, and sheep heads. There is an intra-Hanafi difference: Imam Abu Hanifa specifies cows and sheep, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad specify only sheep. However, the decisive factor is custom ('urf). In the commentator's time, only sheep heads were common, so the mufti should follow custom. The text refutes the idea that linguistic meaning should be prioritized, asserting that custom is the primary consideration. The ruling that eating pork or human flesh does not break an oath is cited to support this, as those are not considered 'head' by custom.

Key Terms
الرأس (al-ra's): In oaths, the head of an animal that is sold in the local market, typically roasted.
العرف (al-'urf): Customary practice; the primary determinant for interpreting terms in oaths.

Key Takeaway

Defining Fruit in Oaths: The Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Defining 'fruit' for oaths with intra-Hanafi dispute and custom-based resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi custom-based definition of fruit for oaths, explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the rule to determine oath-breaking in hypotheticals.

Core Idea
Custom, not botany, defines 'fruit' in Hanafi oaths; pleasure is the key criterion.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explores how the Hanafi school defines 'fruit' (fakiha) when someone swears not to eat fruit. The central issue is a classic intra-madhhab dispute: Abu Hanifa excludes grapes, pomegranates, and fresh dates, while the two Sahibayn include them. The commentary reveals that the dispute is resolved by the dominant custom ('urf) of the time and place, making this an excellent case study in the flexibility of Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling's context
The passage addresses a person who swears an oath not to eat fruit. The legal question is: which items break the oath (cause hanth) under this oath? The answer depends on the definition of 'fruit'.
Oaths are interpreted based on the common understanding of the swearer's community.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his rationale
Abu Hanifa holds that apples, watermelons, and apricots are fruit, but grapes, pomegranates, fresh dates, and cucumbers are not. His criterion is that fruit is eaten purely for pleasure (tafakkuh), not as a staple or medicine. He argues that grapes and dates are also nourishing and medicinal, reducing their pleasurable character.
Cucumbers and broad beans are excluded because they are vegetables, not eaten for pleasure.
3
State the Sahibayn's counter-position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani argue that grapes, pomegranates, and fresh dates are indeed fruit because they are prized for their taste and eaten for pleasure, often as the choicest fruits. They see the pleasure element as clearly present.
This disagreement shows that even within the school, the same principle (tafakkuh) can be applied differently.
4
Understand the resolution by custom
The commentary from al-Kash al-Kabir explains that Abu Hanifa's ruling reflected the custom of his time, while in the Sahibayn's era, custom changed and these items were also considered fruit. The final ruling depends on the prevailing custom ('urf). In later times, all such items are included by consensus (ittifaq).
This highlights the principle that custom can override earlier rulings when the social reality has changed.
5
Apply the custom rule to specific cases
The passage then lists many examples: mulberries, jujube, walnuts, sugarcane, fresh vs. dry fruits, seasonal variations. The consistent rule is: whatever people customarily consider fruit (eaten for pleasure) falls under the oath. Dry forms may be excluded if they are not customarily eaten as fruit.
For instance, dry watermelon is fruit because it is eaten dry; dry walnuts are a condiment, not fruit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والفاكهة التفاح والبطيخ والمشمش لا العنب والرمان والرطب والقثاء والخيار وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : يحنث في الرمان والعنب والرطب أيضاً، والأصل أن الفاكهة اسم لما يتفكه به قبل الطعام وبعده أي يتنعم به زيادة على المعتاد والرطب واليابس فيه سواء بعد أن يكون التفكه به معتاداً حتى يحنث بيابس البطيخ وهذا المعنى موجود في التفاح وأخواتها فيحنث بها وغير موجود في القثاء والخيار لأنهما من البقول بيعاً وأكلاً فلا يحنث بهما . وأما العنب والرطب والرمان فهما يقولان معنى التفكه موجود فيهما فإنها أعز الفواكه والتنعم بها يفوق التنعم بغيرها، وأبو حنيفة يقول إن هذه الأشياء مما يتغذى بها ويتداوى بها فأوجب قصوراً في معنى التفكه في حالة البقاء، ولهذا كان اليابس منها من التوابل أو من الأقوات. وذكر في الكشف الكبير أن هذا اختلاف عصر وزمان فأبو حنيفة أفتى على حسب عرفه وتغير العرف في زمانهما وفي عرفنا ينبغي أن يحنث بالاتفاق ا هـ . وفي الظهيرية قال محمد في الأصل : التوت فاكهة. وعن أبي يوسف أن العناب فاكهة. وفي الأصل الجوز فاكهة . قال القدوري : ثمر الشجر كلها فاكهة إلا الرمان والعنب والرطب والبطيخ من الفواكه ؛ هكذا ذكر القدوري. وروى الحاكم الشهيد في المنتقى عن أبي يوسف ذكر شمس الأئمة السرخسي في شرحه أن البطيخ ليس من الفواكه فإنه ذكر أن ما لا يؤكل يابسه فاكهة فرطبه لا يكون فاكهة. وقال أبو حنيفة : ليس الباقلاء الأخضر بفاكهة والحاصل أن العبرة في جميع ذلك للعرف فما يؤكل على سبيل التفكه عادة ويعد فاكهة في العرف يدخل تحت اليمين وما لا فلا ا هـ . وفي المحيط : ما روي أن الجوز واللوز من الفاكهة هو في عرفهم أما في عرفنا فإنه لا يؤكل للتفكه. وقال محمد قصب السكر والبسر الأحمر فاكهة ولو حلف لا يأكل من فاكهة العام وثمار العام فإن كان في أيام الفاكهة الرطبة فهو على الرطب، فإن أكل اليابس لا يحنث، وإن كان في غير وقتها فهو على اليابس وهذا استحسان لتعارف الناس إطلاق اسم الفاكهة في وقت الرطب على الرطب دون اليابس ا هـ . وفي البدائع : لو حلف لا يأكل فاكهة فأكل زبيباً أو تمراً أو حب الرمان لا يحنث بالإجماع والجوز رطبه فاكهة ويابسه إدام
His saying: (And fruit: apples, watermelon, apricots, not grapes, pomegranates, fresh dates, cucumbers, and cucumbers. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He breaks his oath with pomegranates, grapes, and fresh dates as well. The principle is that fruit is a name for what is eaten for pleasure before and after food, i.e., to enjoy as an extra beyond the usual, and fresh and dry are equal in it, provided that the pleasure is customary, so that he breaks his oath with dry watermelon. This meaning exists in apples and their likes, so he breaks his oath with them, and does not exist in cucumbers and cucumbers because they are vegetables in sale and eating, so he does not break his oath with them. As for grapes, fresh dates, and pomegranates, the two (Sahibayn) say the meaning of pleasure exists in them because they are the choicest fruits and the enjoyment surpasses others. Abu Hanifa says these things are eaten for nourishment and medicinal purposes, so this causes a deficiency in the meaning of pleasure in the state of satiety, and therefore their dry forms are among spices or staple foods. It was mentioned in al-Kash al-Kabir that this is a difference of time and era; Abu Hanifa gave fatwa according to his custom, and custom changed in their time, and in our custom it is appropriate that he breaks the oath by agreement. In al-Zahiriyya, Muhammad said in the original: mulberries are fruit. And from Abu Yusuf that jujube is fruit. In the original, walnuts are fruit. Al-Quduri said: fruits of trees are all fruit except pomegranates, grapes, fresh dates, and watermelon are among fruits; thus mentioned al-Quduri. Al-Hakim al-Shahid narrated in al-Muntaqa from Abu Yusuf; Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi mentioned in his commentary that watermelon is not among fruits, because he mentions that what is not eaten dry is fruit, so its fresh form is not fruit. And Abu Hanifa said: broad beans are not fruit. The conclusion is that the criterion in all this is custom; so what is eaten for pleasure customarily and is considered fruit in custom falls under the oath, and what is not, then no. In al-Muhit: what is narrated that walnuts and almonds are fruit is according to their custom; as for our custom, it is not eaten for pleasure. And Muhammad said: sugarcane and unripe dates are fruit. And if he swears not to eat from the fruit of the year and the produce of the year, if it is during the season of fresh fruit, it applies to fresh; if he eats dry, he does not break his oath; if it is not during its season, it applies to dry, and this is istihsan because people customarily apply the name of fruit to fresh during fresh season, not to dry. In al-Bada'i': If he swears not to eat fruit and eats raisins, dry dates, or pomegranate seeds, he does not break his oath by consensus. And walnuts: fresh is fruit, dry is condiment.
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the definition of 'fruit' (fakiha) in oaths. The matn quotes Abu Hanifa's position that grapes, pomegranates, and fresh dates are not fruit, while apples, watermelon, and apricots are. The Sahibayn disagree and include the former. The commentator explains the underlying principle: fruit is eaten for pleasure (tafakkuh), not as a staple or medicine. Abu Hanifa argues that grapes, dates, and pomegranates are also used as food and medicine, reducing their pleasurable aspect. The commentary notes that this is a difference of custom and era; later custom changed, and in the commentator's time, all would be included. The text then provides additional rulings from various sources: mulberries, jujube, walnuts, sugarcane, etc. The overarching rule is that custom ('urf) determines what is considered fruit. The passage ends with a ruling that raisins, dry dates, and pomegranate seeds do not break an oath by consensus, and that fresh walnuts are fruit while dry walnuts are condiment.

Key Terms
الفاكهة (al-fakiha): Anything eaten for pleasure (tafakkuh) customarily, not as a staple or medicine.
التفكه (al-tafakkuh): Eating for pleasure, as a treat beyond necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Botanical classification
B) The personal view of the swearer
C) Custom ('urf) of the community
D) The ruling of the Sahibayn

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the definition of 'fruit' for oaths is determined by custom, not biology; pleasure (tafakkuh) is the guiding principle, and any item customarily eaten for pleasure qualifies, regardless of botanical classification.

Defining Halwa for Oaths: Custom and Species-Based Ruling
Defining 'halwa' and related terms by custom, with rulings on honey and sugar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define what constitutes 'halwa' in Hanafi oath rulings and apply the custom-based definition to determine oath violations.

Core Idea
Halwa is defined by the absence of a sour counterpart in its species, not by sweetness alone.

In this passage, the author clarifies what sweets (halwa) mean in the context of oaths. The definition hinges on custom and the nature of the food item: if a fruit or food has a naturally occurring sour variant, it is not considered halwa. This lesson will guide you through the key distinctions and their practical implications for oath violations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Recognize the terms halwa, shahd, sukker and their definitions from the text.
Halwa is defined by the absence of sour counterpart; shahd is mixed honey; sukker must be eaten as solid.
2
Apply the custom-based definition
Determine whether a given sweet item is halwa by checking if its species includes a sour variant. If yes, it is not halwa.
Custom of the time and place may refine the definition.
3
Distinguish between shahd and asal
Understand that shahd is mixed honey, asal is pure. Eating asal does not break an oath on shahd.
The text explicitly states this distinction.
4
Analyze the sugar ruling
Eating sugar by sucking and swallowing the melt does not count as eating sugar; the substance must be consumed as food.
This is a specific exception based on the form of consumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيد المصنف بالفاكهة لأنه لو حلف لا يأكل الحلواء فالحلواء عندهم كل حلو ليس من جنسه حامض وما كان من جنسه حامض فليس بحلواء، والمرجع فيه إلى العرف فيحنث بأكل الخبيص والعسل والسكر والناطف والرب والرطب والتمر وأشباه ذلك، وكذا روى المعلى عن محمد إذا أكل تيناً رطباً أو يابساً لأنه ليس من جنسها حامض فخلص معنى الحلاوة فيه ولو أكل عنباً حلواً أو بطيخاً حلواً أو رماناً حلواً أو إجاصاً حلواً لم يحنث لأن من جنسه ما ليس بحلواء، وكذا الزبيب، وكذا إذا حلف لا يأكل حلواة فهو مثل الحلواء ؛ كذا في البدائع. وحاصله أن الحلو والخلواء والحلاوة واحد، وهذا ليس في عرفنا فإن في عرفنا الحلو اسم للعسل المطبوخ على النار بنشاء ونحوه، وأما الحلواء والحلاوة فاسم لسكر أو عسل أو ماء عنب طبخ على النار وعقد حتى صار جامداً كالعقيد والفانيذ والحلاوة الجوزية والسمسمية ونحوها. وكذا قال في الظهيرية. قال القدوري : المرجع في هذا إلى عادات الناس فعلى هذا لا يحنث في الفانيذ والعسل والسكر في بلادنا ا هـ . ولو حلف لا يأكل شهداً فأكل عسلاً لا يحنث لأن العسل اسم للصافي والشهد اسم للمختلط، ولو حلف لا يأكل سكراً فأكل سكراً بفيه وجعل يمتصه حتى ذاب فابتلع ماءه لم يحنث ; كذا الظهيرية أيضاً .
The author restricted to fruit because if he swears not to eat halwa, then halwa according to them is every sweet thing of whose kind there is no sour thing, and whatever of its kind is sour is not halwa. The reference is to custom. So he breaks his oath by eating khabis, honey, sugar, natif, rub, fresh dates, dry dates, and similar. Likewise, al-Mu'alla narrated from Muhammad: if he eats fresh or dry figs, because there is no sourness in its kind, so the meaning of sweetness is pure in it. If he eats sweet grapes, sweet watermelon, sweet pomegranate, or sweet plums, he does not break his oath because among its kind there is what is not halwa. Likewise, raisins. Likewise, if he swears not to eat hulawa, it is like halwa. Thus in al-Bada'i'. The summary is that 'hulw', 'halwa', and 'halawat' are one. But this is not our custom; in our custom, 'hulw' is a name for honey cooked on fire with starch etc., while 'halwa' and 'halawat' are names for sugar, honey, or grape juice cooked on fire and thickened until solid, like 'aqid, fanidh, walnut halwa, sesame halwa, etc. So said in al-Zahiriyya. Al-Quduri said: The reference is to people's customs; accordingly, he does not break his oath with fanidh, honey, and sugar in our country. If he swears not to eat shahd and eats 'asal, he does not break his oath because 'asal is name for pure honey and shahd is name for mixed. If he swears not to eat sugar and eats sugar with his mouth and sucks it until it melts and swallows its water, he does not break his oath; thus in al-Zahiriyya as well.
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage explains the rulings for sweets (halwa/hulw) in oaths. The author had previously discussed fruit; here he clarifies that halwa is distinct. The definition: halwa is any sweet that has no naturally occurring sour counterpart in its family. For example, figs are inherently sweet, so eating them breaks an oath not to eat halwa. Grapes, watermelon, pomegranate, plums have sour varieties, so even if the eaten ones are sweet, they are not halwa because the category includes sour ones. The text notes that in the author's own custom, the terms are different: 'hulw' refers to cooked honey pudding, while 'halwa' refers to solid sweets like fanidh. Therefore, in that custom, eating fanidh, honey, or plain sugar does not breach an oath. Further rulings: 'shahd' is mixed honey, 'asal' is pure; eating 'asal' does not break an oath on 'shahd'. Eating sugar by sucking and swallowing its water does not count as eating sugar (since the substance is not consumed as a solid).

Key Terms
الحلواء (al-halwa): Any sweet substance of which there is no sour variant in its category.
الشهد (al-shahd): Mixed honey, not pure.
السكر (al-sukkar): Sugar; eating by sucking and swallowing the dissolved water does not break oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if someone swears not to eat halwa and eats sweet watermelon, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because it is sweet.
B) No, because watermelon has a sour counterpart.
C) Yes, if it is sweet watermelon.
D) No, because watermelon is not a sweet category.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi, what counts as halwa for an oath depends on whether the species has a naturally sour variant, subject to custom.

Definition of Idam in Oaths: Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad
Defining 'idam' with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'idam' (condiment) for oaths and distinguish between the views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding its scope.

Core Idea
Condiment in oaths is dip, not any side dish — the definition determines oath-breaking.

The definition of 'idam' (condiment) is crucial when a person swears an oath regarding eating a certain condiment. The Hanafi school has an internal dispute: Abu Yusuf limits it to items that are dipped into bread, while Muhammad includes anything eaten with bread. This lesson unpacks the definition and its implications for oath fulfillment.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position from the matn
Read the matn definition: idam is what is dipped into bread, like vinegar, salt, oil. Note the explicit exclusions: meat, eggs, cheese.
This is the base text (matn) definition.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Contrast Abu Yusuf's definition (something that dyes/mixes with bread) with Muhammad's definition (anything eaten with bread).
The dispute is about the essential characteristic: dipping vs. accompaniment.
3
Trace the basis of each view
Abu Yusuf focuses on the linguistic sense of 'idam' as that which is dipped (sabgh). Muhammad relies on broader customary usage.
No specific dalil is given; it's an usul-based difference in interpreting custom (urf).
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The text attributes the narrower definition to Abu Yusuf and states it is also the view of Abu Hanifa (as per al-Zayla'i). This is the relied-upon position.
The authority weight is zahir al-riwayah.
5
Apply the definition to examples
Classify given items: oil and vinegar are idam because they are dipped; meat, eggs, cheese are not because they are not typically dipped.
This has practical consequences for oath-breaking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والإدام ما يصطبغ به كالخل والملح والزيت لا اللحم والبيض والجبن) أي هو شيء يصبغ الخبز إذا اختلط .به وهذا عند أبي يوسف وقال محمد: هو ما يؤكل مع الخبز قوله : (وهذا عند أبي يوسف عبارة الزيلعي وهذا عند أبي حنيفة» وهو الظاهر من قول أبي يوسف .
His saying: (And condiment is what is dipped into, like vinegar, salt, oil, not meat, eggs, and cheese). That is, it is something that dyes the bread when mixed with it. And this is according to Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: It is what is eaten with bread. His saying: (And this according to Abu Yusuf is the expression of al-Zayla'i, and this according to Abu Hanifa) and it is the apparent from the saying of Abu Yusuf.
Source pages p. 544

Commentary
The final section defines 'idam' (condiment) as given in the matn: something used for dipping bread, such as vinegar, salt, oil. Meat, eggs, and cheese are explicitly excluded. The commentary presents the intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf defines it as something that dyes or mixes with bread, while Muhammad defines it as anything eaten with bread. The text then notes that the expression in the matn is attributed to Abu Yusuf (as per al-Zayla'i) and that this is also the apparent view of Abu Hanifa. Thus, the relied-upon definition is that of Abu Yusuf, limiting condiment to items used for dipping.

Key Terms
الإدام (al-idam): A condiment used to dip bread, e.g., vinegar, salt, oil; excludes meat, eggs, cheese.
الصبغ (al-sabgh): The act of dipping or mixing into bread.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is considered 'idam' in an oath?
A) Meat
B) Eggs
C) Oil
D) Cheese

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, 'idam' refers specifically to dipping substances like vinegar and oil, not to all accompaniments—this narrow definition is the relied-upon view.

Defining Fruit in Oaths: Custom as the Final Arbiter
Intra-Hanafi dispute on defining fruit for oaths, concluding with custom as decisive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that custom determines what constitutes fruit for oath purposes and apply it to specific examples from the text.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, 'fruit' for oaths is defined by custom (urf), not by a fixed botanical or linguistic list.

When someone swears not to eat fruit, what counts as fruit? Is it a botanical category, or does it depend on how people actually use the term? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a chain of Hanafi authorities debating specific items, only to settle on a single principle: custom (al-urf) is the final reference. The ruling changes with time, place, and how an item is consumed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Problem
The core question: what falls under an oath to avoid 'fruit'? The answer determines whether the oath is broken (حنث).
The problem is not botanical; it is legal and depends on common usage.
2
Catalog the Specific Imam Opinions
List the items explicitly mentioned: mulberry (fruit, by agreement), jujube (Abu Yusuf: fruit), walnut (originally fruit; later custom changed), pomegranate/grapes/fresh dates/watermelon (Al-Quduri: not fruit), fresh broad beans (Abu Hanifa: not fruit), sugarcane and red unripe dates (Muhammad: fruit).
Note the disagreement on each item; the pattern reveals the underlying logic.
3
Extract the Underlying Principle
Despite the differences, all imams ultimately refer to custom (العرف). The text states: 'والحاصل أن العبرة في جميع ذلك للعرف' – the criterion in all of this is custom. What is eaten for refreshment (تفكه) customarily is fruit.
Custom is a source of law (masdar) in Hanafi fiqh, especially in non-ritual matters.
4
Observe the Impact of Time and Place
Al-Muhit notes that walnuts and almonds were fruit in an earlier custom but no longer are. This shows that custom is dynamic, not static.
A ruling based on custom may shift over generations.
5
Apply to Seasonal Distinctions
Muhammad's ruling on 'fruit of the year' shows that in season, fresh fruit is intended; out of season, dried fruit applies. This is istihsan based on how people actually speak.
The same word 'fruit' can refer to different things at different times.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التوت فاكهة. وعن أبي يوسف أن العناب فاكهة. وفي الأصل الجوز فاكهة . قال القدوري : ثمر الشجر كلها فاكهة إلا الرمان والعنب والرطب والبطيخ من الفواكه ؛ هكذا ذكر القدوري. وروى الحاكم الشهيد في المنتقى عن أبي يوسف ذكر شمس الأئمة السرخسي في شرحه أن البطيخ ليس من الفواكه فإنه ذكر أن ما لا يؤكل يابسه فاكهة فرطبه لا يكون فاكهة. وقال أبو حنيفة : ليس الباقلاء الأخضر بفاكهة والحاصل أن العبرة في جميع ذلك للعرف فما يؤكل على سبيل التفكه عادة ويعد فاكهة في العرف يدخل تحت اليمين وما لا ا هـ . وفي المحيط : ما روي أن الجوز واللوز من الفاكهة هو في عرفهم أما في عرفنا فإنه لا يؤكل للتفكه. وقال محمد قصب السكر والبسر الأحمر فاكهة ولو حلف لا يأكل من فاكهة العام وثمار العام فإن كان في أيام الفاكهة الرطبة فهو على الرطب، فإن أكل اليابس لا يحنث، وإن كان في غير وقتها فهو على اليابس وهذا استحسان لتعارف الناس إطلاق اسم الفاكهة في وقت الرطب على الرطب دون اليابس ا هـ . وفي البدائع : لو حلف لا يأكل فاكهة فأكل زبيباً أو تمراً أو حب الرمان لا يحنث بالإجماع والجوز رطبه فاكهة ويابسه إدام
The mulberry is fruit. And from Abu Yusuf that the jujube is fruit. And in the original text, the walnut is fruit. Al-Quduri said: All tree fruits are fruit except pomegranate, grapes, fresh dates, and watermelon are from fruits; thus mentioned Al-Quduri. And Al-Hakim Al-Shahid narrated in Al-Muntaqa from Abu Yusuf, Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi mentioned in his commentary that watermelon is not from fruits because he mentioned that what is not eaten dry is fruit, so its fresh form is not fruit. And Abu Hanifa said: Fresh broad beans are not fruit. And the summary is that the criterion in all that is custom (urf), so what is eaten for refreshment customarily and is considered fruit in custom enters under the oath, and what is not, no. And in Al-Muhit: What is narrated that walnuts and almonds are from fruit is in their custom, but in our custom, they are not eaten for refreshment. And Muhammad said: Sugarcane and red unripe dates are fruit. And if one swears not to eat from the fruit of the year and the fruits of the year, then if it is the days of fresh fruit, it applies to fresh; if he eats dry, he does not break the oath; and if it is not its time, then it applies to dry, and this is istihsan due to the people's custom of applying the name fruit during the time of fresh to fresh, not to dry. And in Al-Bada'i: If one swears not to eat fruit and eats raisins, dates, or pomegranate seeds, he does not break the oath by consensus. And walnuts: their fresh is fruit, their dry is condiment.
Source pages p. 544

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the definition of fruit (فاكهة) in the context of oaths. It is an intra-Hanafi dispute where various opinions from Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad are presented. The overall conclusion is that custom (urf) is the final arbiter. The text first lists specific items: mulberry, jujube, and walnut are considered fruit in early sources. Al-Quduri excludes pomegranate, grapes, fresh dates, and watermelon from being fruit, though they are called 'fruits' in a general sense. Al-Sarakhsi explains that watermelon is not fruit because it is not eaten dry; the principle is that anything not eaten dry is not fruit in its fresh form. Abu Hanifa excludes fresh broad beans. The summary emphasizes that custom determines what is eaten for refreshment (تفكه). Al-Muhit notes that walnuts and almonds were fruit in earlier custom but not in later custom. Muhammad adds sugarcane and red unripe dates. He also addresses oaths regarding 'fruit of the year': the reference changes with seasons. Al-Bada'i provides a consensus that raisins, dates, and pomegranate seeds are not fruit for oath purposes, and fresh walnuts are fruit while dry walnuts are a condiment (إدام). This shows the importance of prevailing custom in legal definitions.

Key Terms
فاكهة (fākiha): In this passage, fruit is defined by custom as what is eaten for refreshment (تفكه). It is not a fixed botanical category but a customary one for oath purposes.
عرف (ʿurf): Custom or common practice of the people in a particular time and place. Used as the final criterion for determining what constitutes fruit in oaths.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that allows departure from strict analogy in favor of custom or convenience. Used here to prefer the ruling based on what people commonly call fruit in season.
تفكه (tafakkuh): Eating for refreshment or pleasure, as opposed to eating as a staple or condiment. Key concept in distinguishing fruit from other foods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if someone swears not to eat fruit and then eats a dried walnut?
A) He breaks the oath because walnuts are fruit in all cases.
B) He does not break the oath because dried walnut is considered a condiment, not fruit.
C) It depends on the season.
D) He breaks the oath only if he eats it fresh.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oath law, 'fruit' is defined by custom (urf): what people customarily eat for refreshment and call fruit governs the ruling, not a fixed list.

What Is Considered Halwa (Sweet) in Oaths?
Defining halwa in oaths based on custom and the sour-counterpart rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi definition of halwa (sweet) based on custom and the sour-counterpart rule to determine whether an oath is broken.

Core Idea
Halwa is defined by custom and the absence of a sour counterpart—sweet grapes do not break an oath on halwa.

In oaths on eating specific foods, the exact definition of terms matters. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that 'halwa' is not simply any sweet food; it is defined by two criteria: (1) it must be sweet, and (2) it must have no naturally occurring sour counterpart of its kind. Custom ('urf) is the final reference. Thus, certain sweet items like figs and dates are halwa, while sweet grapes or sweet pomegranates are not, because sour versions exist. This lesson unpacks the rule and its application.

Guided Path
1
Establish the definition of halwa
The text defines halwa as 'every sweet that has no sour from its kind.' Identify that the core condition is the absence of a naturally sour variety of the same fruit or food.
The defining characteristic is not sweetness alone but the lack of a sour counterpart.
2
Understand the role of custom ('urf)
The text states 'the reference for it is custom' (wa al-marji' fihi ila al-'urf). Recognize that the classification of halwa may vary by region and time.
Custom overrides a generic linguistic definition.
3
Apply the definition to examples
Test each item: figs have no sour counterpart → halwa; grapes have sour varieties → not halwa, even if sweet. Work through the examples in the text.
Also note that raisins (zabib) are treated like grapes.
4
Recognize the impact of local custom
Al-Quduri notes that in his lands, fanidh, honey, and sugar are not called halwa, so an oath on halwa is not broken. Consider how a mufti would check local usage.
The mufta bihi is that custom is decisive; always investigate the 'urf of the swearer.
5
Distinguish related terms (shahd vs. asal, solid vs. liquefied sugar)
The passage also covers oaths on 'shahd' (mixed honey) and 'sukkar' (sugar). Eating 'asal (pure honey) does not break an oath on 'shahd'. Sucking sugar until it melts and swallowing the liquid does not break an oath on eating solid sugar.
These rulings further illustrate that terms are applied according to their customary meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيد المصنف بالفاكهة لأنه لو حلف لا يأكل الحلواء فالحلواء عندهم كل حلو ليس من جنسه حامض وما كان من جنسه حامض فليس بحلواء، والمرجع فيه إلى العرف فيحنث بأكل الخبيص والعسل والسكر والناطف والرب والرطب والتمر وأشباه ذلك، وكذا روى المعلى عن محمد إذا أكل تيناً رطباً أو يابساً لأنه ليس من جنسها حامض فخلص معنى الحلاوة فيه ولو أكل عنباً حلواً أو بطيخاً حلواً أو رماناً حلواً أو إجاصاً حلواً لم يحنث لأن من جنسه ما ليس بحلواء، وكذا الزبيب، وكذا إذا حلف لا يأكل حلواة فهو مثل الحلواء ؛ كذا في البدائع. وحاصله أن الحلو والخلواء والحلاوة واحد، وهذا ليس في عرفنا فإن في عرفنا الحلو اسم للعسل المطبوخ على النار بنشاء ونحوه، وأما الحلواء والحلاوة فاسم لسكر أو عسل أو ماء عنب طبخ على النار وعقد حتى صار جامداً كالعقيد والفانيذ والحلاوة الجوزية والسمسمية ونحوها. وكذا قال في الظهيرية. قال القدوري : المرجع في هذا إلى عادات الناس فعلى هذا لا يحنث في الفانيذ والعسل والسكر في بلادنا ا هـ . ولو حلف لا يأكل شهداً فأكل عسلاً لا يحنث لأن العسل اسم للصافي والشهد اسم للمختلط، ولو حلف لا يأكل سكراً فأكل سكراً بفيه وجعل يمتصه حتى ذاب فابتلع ماءه لم يحنث ؛ كذا الظهيرية أيضاً .
The author [of Kanz al-Daqa'iq] restricted [the chapter] to fruit, because if he swears not to eat halwa, then halwa according to them is every sweet thing that has no sour from its kind; and what has sour from its kind is not halwa. The reference for it is custom. So he breaks the oath by eating khabis, honey, sugar, natif, rubb, fresh dates, dry dates, and the like. Likewise, al-Mu'alla narrated from Muhammad: if he eats fresh or dry figs, because there is no sour from its kind, so the meaning of sweetness is pure in it. And if he eats sweet grapes, sweet watermelon, sweet pomegranate, or sweet plums, he does not break the oath because from its kind there is what is not halwa. Likewise raisins. And likewise if he swears not to eat hulawa, it is like halwa; thus in al-Bada'i. The summary is that 'hulw', 'khulawa', and 'hulawa' are one. And this is not in our custom, for in our custom 'hulw' is the name for honey cooked on fire with starch and the like. As for 'halwa' and 'hulawa', they are a name for sugar, honey, or grape water cooked on fire and thickened until it solidifies, like 'aqid, fanidh, walnut halwa, sesame halwa, and the like. And likewise said in al-Zahiriyya. Al-Quduri said: The reference for this is the customs of people; upon this, he does not break the oath with fanidh, honey, and sugar in our lands. And if he swears not to eat shahd and eats 'asal, he does not break the oath because 'asal is the name for pure [honey] and shahd is the name for mixed. And if he swears not to eat sugar and puts sugar in his mouth and sucks it until it melts and swallows its water, he does not break the oath; likewise al-Zahiriyya as well.
Source pages p. 544

Commentary
This passage defines 'halwa' (حلواء) and related terms in the context of oaths. It begins by noting that the matn author restricted the discussion on fruit, because the rulings on halwa follow similar principles. The key definition: halwa is any sweet item that does not have a naturally sour counterpart (i.e., there is no sour variety of that fruit or food). For example, figs have no sour variety, so they are halwa. But grapes, watermelon, pomegranate, and plums all have sour varieties, so even if they are sweet, they are not considered halwa. The reference for the definition is custom (عرف). The text then gives examples of items that break the oath: khabis (a sweet dish), honey, sugar, natif (a type of halwa), rubb (thick syrup), fresh and dry dates, etc. The summary explains that in the author's custom, 'hulw' specifically refers to honey cooked with starch, while 'halwa' and 'hulawa' refer to cooked solid sweets. Al-Quduri states that the criterion is people's customs, so in his region, fanidh, honey, and sugar do not break the oath. The passage also covers related oaths on 'shahd' (pure honey) and sugar: eating 'asal (ordinary honey) does not break an oath on 'shahd'; sucking sugar until it liquefies and swallowing the water does not break an oath on eating solid sugar. These rulings reinforce that the literal application of terms is modified by custom and common usage.

Key Terms
حلواء (ḥalwā'): A sweet food item that has no sour counterpart from its kind; defined by custom. In the text, it includes khabis, honey, sugar, natif, rubb, dates, etc.
عرف (ʿurf): Custom or common usage, the decisive factor in classifying foods as halwa or not.
شهد (shahd): Pure honey from the comb, as opposed to 'asal (ordinary honey). In oaths, the distinction matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) He does not break the oath because pomegranate has a sour variety, so it is not considered halwa.
C) He breaks the oath only if the pomegranate is pure sweet.
D) The ruling depends on whether he also eats bread with it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the definition of 'halwa' in oaths is determined by custom and the absence of a sour counterpart, not by mere sweetness.

Defining Idam in Oaths: Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad
Defining idam (condiment) with intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on the definition of idam for oaths, identify the mufta bihi position, and apply the definition to determine oath-breaking.

Core Idea
Custom defines idam; Muhammad's broader view (anything eaten with bread) is the fatwa.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi dispute: what counts as 'idam' (condiment) in an oath? Abu Yusuf limits it to liquids that 'dye' bread, while Muhammad includes any food typically eaten with bread. The text from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents arguments from both sides, settles on Muhammad's view due to custom, and explores practical implications. Understanding this dispute sharpens your skill in applying custom (urf) in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
First, clearly state Abu Yusuf's definition (idam = what dyes bread, i.e., liquids) and Muhammad's definition (idam = anything eaten with bread). Note that Abu Hanifa's view aligns with Abu Yusuf according to al-Zayla'i.
2
Trace the derivation (talil)
Examine how each imam derives his definition. Abu Yusuf relies on the etymology (mu'adama = compatibility through mixing) and the concept of secondary consumption (tabaiyya). Muhammad interprets mu'adama as simple compatibility and invokes custom.
The text explains that liquids like vinegar are not eaten alone, so they are secondary; meat can be eaten alone, so it is not secondary unless intended.
3
Identify the thamarah (practical consequence)
Work through the example: if someone swears not to eat idam, does eating meat alone break the oath? Under Abu Yusuf, no; under Muhammad, yes. This is the operative difference.
Also note the reverse scenario in Tamartashi's comparison with the 'loaf' oath.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The text cites al-Qalanisi and al-Muhit: the fatwa is on Muhammad's view because of custom (urf). Abu al-Layth also adopted it. Confirm that custom overrides the literal derivation.
Custom is the primary evidence; the hadith about meat and the story of Muawiya are considered unnecessary or weak.
5
Apply to new cases
Practice with examples: Are dates, grapes, vegetables idam? Under Muhammad, they are not if typically eaten alone, but custom may change the ruling. Understand that the definition is flexible based on prevailing practice.
Also note that if one eats idam alone, the oath phrasing matters: 'not to eat idam' is broken, but 'not to eat idam with bread' is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في قوله : (والإدام ما يصطبغ به كالخل والملح والزيت لا اللحم والبيض والجبن) أي هو شيء يصبغ الخبز إذا اختلط به وهذا عند أبي يوسف وقال محمد: هو ما يؤكل مع الخبز قوله : (وهذا عند أبي يوسف عبارة الزيلعي وهذا عند أبي حنيفة» وهو الظاهر من قول أبي يوسف . وهو رواية عن أبي يوسف لأن الإدام من المآدمة وهي الموافقة وكل ما يؤكل مع الخبز موافق له كاللحم والبيض ونحوه ولهما أن الإدام ما يؤكل تبعاً والتبعية في الاختلاط حقيقة ليكون قائماً به، وفي أن لا يؤكل على الانفراد حكماً وتمام الموافقة في الامتزاج أيضاً، والخل المائعات لا تؤكل وحدها بل تشرب والملح لا يؤكل بانفراده عادة ولأنه يذوب فيكون تبعاً بخلاف اللحم وما يضاهية لأنه يؤكل وحده إلا أن ينويه لما فيه من التشديد. والعنب والبطيخ ليس بإدام بالإجماع وهو الصحيح. وبهذا ظهر أن تخصيص الزيلعي الإدام بالمائع صحيح في الملح أيضاً باعتبار أنه يذوب في الفم ويحصل به صبغ الخبز. والاصطباغ افتعال من الصبغ ولما كان ثلاثيه وهو صبغ متعدياً إلى واحد جاء الافتعال منه لازماً فلا يقال اصطبغ الخبز لأنه لا يصل إلى المفعول حتى يقام مقام الفاعل إذا بنى الفعل له فإنما يقام غيره من الجار والمجرور ونحوه فلذا يقال اصطبغ به وذكر القلانسي في تهذيبه أن الفتوى على قول محمد للعرف ا هـ . وفي المحيط : وقول محمد أظهر وبه أخذ الفقيه أبو الليث ا هـ . ويكفيه الاستدلال بالعرف الظاهر لأن مبناها عليه فلا حاجة إلى الاستدلال له بالحديث «سيد إدامكم اللحم والحكاية أن ملك الروم كتب إلى معاوية أن ابعث إلى بشر إدام على يد شر رجل فبعث إليه جبناً على يد رجل يسكن في بيت أصهاره وهو من أهل اللسان لأن كونه سيده لا يستلزم أن يكون منه إذ يقال في الخليفة سيد العجم وليس هو منهم. وأما حكاية معاوية فيتوقف الاستدلال بها على صحتها وهي بعيدة إذ يبعد من إمام عادل أن يتكلف إرسال شخص إلى بلاد الروم ملتزماً لمؤنته لغرض مهمل لكافر والسكن في بيت الصهر قط أن يكون الساكن شر رجل فآثار البطلان تلوح على هذه القضية كما في فتح القدير. قال التمرتاشي : وهذا الاختلاف بينهم على عكس اختلافهم فيمن حلف لا يأكل إلا رغيفاً فأكل معه البيض ونحوه لم يحنث عندهما، وحنث عند محمد. وإذا أكل الإدام وحده فإن كان حلف لا يأكل إداماً حنث، وإن كان حلف لا يأتدم بإدام لا يحنث بأكله وحده فلا بد من أن يأكل معه الخبز كما أشار إليه في الكشف الكبير. وفي المحيط : قال محمد : التمر والجوز ليس بإدام لأنه يفرد بالأكل في الغالب فكذا العنب والبطيخ والبقل لأنه لا يؤكل تبعاً للخبز بل يؤكل وحده غالباً، وكذلك سائر الفواكه حتى لو كان في موضع يؤكل تبعاً للخبز غالباً يكون إداماً عنده اعتباراً للعرف ا.هـ. وفي الظهيرية : والبقل ليس بإدام بلا خلاف على الأصح. وفي البدائع : سئل محمد عمن حلف لا يأكل خبزاً مأدوماً فقال: الخبز المأدوم الذي يثرد ثرداً يعني في المرق والخل وما أشبهه فقيل له : فإن ثرد في ماء وملح فلم ير ذلك لا يوجب قوله : (وحنث عند محمد) هو يقول إنه قد يؤكل وحده مقصوداً فلا يصير تبعاً للخبز بالشك
In his statement: (And al-idam is what is used to dip bread, like vinegar, salt, and oil, not meat, eggs, and cheese) i.e., it is something that dyes bread when mixed with it. This is according to Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: It is what is eaten with bread. His statement: (This is according to Abu Yusuf; it is the wording of al-Zayla'i, and this is according to Abu Hanifa) and it is the apparent from the statement of Abu Yusuf. And it is a narration from Abu Yusuf, because al-idam comes from al-mu'adama which is compatibility, and everything eaten with bread is compatible with it like meat, eggs, and the like. And for the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?) that al-idam is what is eaten as a secondary, and the secondary nature is real in mixing to be standing by it, and in that it is not eaten alone legally, and complete compatibility is also in mixing. And vinegar and liquids are not eaten alone but drunk; salt is not eaten alone customarily and because it dissolves and becomes secondary, unlike meat and what resembles it because it is eaten alone unless he intends it due to the severity. And grapes and watermelon are not idam by consensus, and that is correct. And by this it appears that al-Zayla'i's specification of idam as liquid is correct also for salt, considering that it dissolves in the mouth and dyes the bread. And 'isbigha' is a verbal noun from 'sabgha', and since its triliteral is 'sabagha' transitive to one object, the verbal noun came from it as intransitive, so it is not said 'isbagha al-khubz' because it does not reach the object until it is placed in the position of the subject when the verb is built for it; rather, something else from the prepositional phrase and the like is placed. Hence it is said 'isbagha bihi'. And al-Qalanisi mentioned in his Tahdhib that the fatwa is on the statement of Muhammad due to custom. And in al-Muhit: Muhammad's statement is more apparent, and al-Faqih Abu al-Layth adopted it. And it suffices to use the apparent custom as evidence because it is based on it, so there is no need to use the hadith 'The best of your idam is meat' and the story that the king of Rome wrote to Muawiya: 'Send to me some idam with the worst man,' so he sent him cheese with a man who lived in the house of his in-laws and was a fluent speaker, because his being a master does not necessitate that he is from them, for it is said about the caliph 'master of the non-Arabs' while he is not from them. As for the story of Muawiya, relying on its authenticity is problematic, and it is far-fetched because it is unlikely that a just imam would go to the trouble of sending a person to Rome bearing the expenses for a neglected purpose to a disbeliever, and living in the house of the in-laws just because the resident was the worst man; so signs of falsehood appear on this case as in Fath al-Qadir. Al-Tamartashi said: This difference between them is opposite to their difference in the case of one who swears not to eat except a loaf, then eats with it eggs and the like: he does not break the oath according to the two, and breaks according to Muhammad. And if one eats idam alone, then if he swore not to eat idam, he breaks; if he swore not to eat idam with bread (la ya'tadim bi-idam), then eating it alone does not break; he must eat with it bread as indicated in al-Kashf al-Kabir. And in al-Muhit: Muhammad said: Dates and walnuts are not idam because they are usually eaten alone; likewise grapes, watermelon, and vegetables because they are not eaten as a secondary to bread but are eaten alone mostly; and likewise the rest of fruits, even if in a place they are mostly eaten as a secondary to bread, they become idam according to him considering custom. And in al-Zahiriyya: Vegetables are not idam without dispute according to the most correct view. And in al-Bada'i: Muhammad was asked about one who swore not to eat bread with idam (khubz ma'dum), so he said: Bread with idam is that which is crumbled (tharid) i.e., in broth, vinegar, and the like. It was said to him: What if he crumbles it in water and salt? He did not consider that to be idam. His statement: (And he breaks according to Muhammad) is because it may be eaten alone intentionally, so it does not become secondary to bread by doubt.
Source pages p. 544

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into the definition of 'idam' (إدام, condiment) in the context of oaths. It is an intra-Hanafi dispute with two main positions: Abu Yusuf defines idam as something that dyes or colors bread when mixed (like liquids), while Muhammad defines it as anything eaten with bread (including solids like meat). The text also attributes a view to Abu Hanifa via al-Zayla'i's wording, aligning with Abu Yusuf. The supporting arguments: Muhammad derives idam from 'mu'adama' (compatibility), so anything compatible with bread qualifies. The two (Abu Yusuf and presumably Abu Hanifa) argue that idam is eaten as a secondary (taba'), which is truly realized by mixing and being unable to be eaten alone. Vinegar and salt are not eaten alone; meat is eaten alone unless intended as idam. The text then notes that custom is the ultimate arbiter, and al-Qalanisi states the fatwa is on Muhammad's view. Al-Muhit prefers Muhammad's view. The hadith about meat being the best idam is not needed because custom suffices. The story about Muawiya is critiqued as unreliable. Al-Tamartashi points out the reversal of disagreement in a related case. The passage then covers specifics: if someone eats idam alone, the ruling depends on how the oath is phrased; if he swore not to eat idam, he breaks; if he swore not to eat idam with bread, he must eat it with bread. Muhammad's examples: dates, walnuts, grapes, watermelon, and vegetables are not idam because they are typically eaten alone; however, custom can change that. The passage ends with a ruling from Muhammad about bread crumbled in water and salt not being considered idam, and a note that Muhammad's view that breaking the oath occurs is because such items can be eaten alone intentionally, so they do not become secondary by doubt.

Key Terms
إدام (idām): Condiment or relish eaten with bread. Disputed: Abu Yusuf defines as what dyes bread (liquids), Muhammad as anything eaten with bread.
اصطباغ (iṣṭibāgh): Verbal noun meaning 'dipping' or 'dyeing' bread in a condiment. Derived from 'صبغ' (to dye).
مآدمة (mu'ādama): Compatibility or harmony; etymological root of 'idam'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view (mufta bihi), if someone swears not to eat idam and eats meat alone, what is the ruling?
A) He does not break the oath because meat is not idam.
B) He breaks the oath because meat is considered idam under Muhammad's view, which is the fatwa.
C) He breaks the oath only if he eats meat with bread.
D) The ruling depends on whether the meat is cooked.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the definition of idam for oaths is determined by custom (urf), and the fatwa adopts Muhammad's view that idam includes anything customarily eaten with bread, including meat and eggs.
