Custom Determines Lunch and Dinner in Oaths
Explanatory expansion on oaths and meals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that custom (urf) defines what constitutes lunch and dinner in oaths, and identify when an oath is violated.

Core Idea
Custom defines what lunch and dinner are in oaths, not a fixed clock or food.

When someone swears not to eat lunch or dinner, what exactly do those terms mean? The Hanafi school relies on local custom (urf) to define the meal, its time, and the quantity needed to break the oath. This lesson unpacks the key conditions and shows why a city-dweller drinking milk may not violate his oath, while a Bedouin would. Understanding this illustrates the broader principle that custom carries legal weight in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath context
First, recognize we are dealing with an oath (yamin) regarding eating a specific meal: lunch (ghadā') or dinner ('ashā'). The ruling about violation depends on defining these terms.
The oath is about the act of eating (al-taghaddī, al-ta‘ashshī), not the food name.
2
Note the role of custom
The commentator emphasizes that 'ghadā'' is the name of the food eaten at a customarily determined time. The time and type of food are based on what people of that locale customarily eat. This is a key Hanafi principle: al-‘urf mu‘tabar (custom is considered).
Custom overrides fixed definitions – each town's lunch is what they customarily eat at that time.
3
Extract the conditions for violation
From Qāḍīkhān and al-Tabyīn, we learn: (1) continuous eating intending satiety, (2) more than half of satiety, (3) food must be customary for that locale. A city-dweller drinking milk to fullness does not violate if milk is not their custom, but a Bedouin does.
Quantity threshold: more than half-satiety; one or two mouthfuls are insufficient.
4
Recognize the ikhtilaf on start time
There is a dispute within the Hanafi school about when lunch begins. Al-Asbījābī and al-Khulāṣa say sunrise, but the relied-upon view according to the author is dawn (fajr), because custom does not call eating before sunrise 'lunch'. This shows that custom can resolve ikhtilaf when it is consistent.
The author says 'yanbaghī an yakūna huwa al-mu‘tamad' – this is the relied-upon view.
5
Apply the same principles to dinner and other times
The same conditions apply to dinner ('ashā'). Its time is from noon to midnight in early custom, but in later custom (e.g., Egypt) it is after ‘Aṣr. Also, the commentator discusses 'ghudwa', 'ḍaḥwa', 'taṣbīḥ', and 'masā’’ – all defined by custom in oath contexts.
The term 'al-‘ashā’' (with fatha) is the food; 'al-‘ishā’’ (with kasra) is the evening prayer time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والغداء الأكل من الفجر إلى الظهر أي التغدي الأكل في هذا الوقت وإنما فسرناه به لأن الغداء في الحقيقة بفتح الغين المعجمة والمد اسم لما يؤكل في الوقت الخاص لا للأكل. وقد ترك المصنف قيدين ذكرهما قاضيخان في فتاواه فقال : التغدي الأكل المترادف الذي يقصد به الشبع في وقت خاص وهو ما بعد طلوع الفجر إلى زوال الشمس مما يتغدى به عادة وغداء كل بلدة ما تعارفه أهل تلك البلدة. وفي التبيين ومقدار ما يحنث به من الأكل أن يكون أكثر من نصف الشبع لأن اللقمة واللقمتين لا تسمى غداء عادة، وجنس المأكول يشترط أن يكون ما يأكله أهل بلدته عادة حتى لو شرب اللبن وشبع لا يحنث إن كان حضرياً وإن كان بدوياً يحنث ا هـ . وفي المحيط : لو حلف لا يتغدى فهو على الخبز فلو تغدى الأرز والتمر واللبن لم يحنث إن كان غير بدوي ولو حلف على فعل ماض بأن قال والله ما تغديت اليوم وقد تغدى بأرز وسمن ينبغي أن يحنث، وإن تغدى المصري بالعنب لم يحنث إلا أن يكون من أهل الرساتيق ممن عادتهم التغدي بالعنب في وقته ا هـ. اختلف في أول وقته فذكر الأسبيجابي أنه طلوع الشمس وهكذا في الخلاصة، وينبغي أن يكون هو المعتمد للعرف لأن الأكل قبل طلوع الشمس لا يسمونه غداء. وأشار المصنف رحمه الله إلى أنه لو حلف ليأتينه غدوة فأتاه بعد طلوع الفجر إلى نصف النهار فقد بر وهو غدوة لأنه وقت الغداء كما في البدائع : وأما الضحوة فمن بعد طلوع الشمس من التي تحل فيها الصلاة إلى نصف النهار لأنه وقت الصلاة الضحى. قال محمد: إذا حلف لا يصبح فالتصبيح عندي ما بين طلوع الشمس وارتفاع الضحى الأكبر؛ فإذا ارتفع الضحى الأكبر ذهب وقت التصبيح لأن التصبيح تفعيل من الصباح والتفعيل للتكثير فيقتضي زيادة على ما يفيده إلا صباح ا هـ قوله : (والعشاء منه إلى نصف الليل) أي التعشي الأكل من الزوال إلى نصف الليل، وأما العشاء - بفتح العين والمد - فاسم للمأكول في هذا الوقت كما تقدم في الغداء. والشرطان السابقان في التغدي يأتيان هنا قلنا وإنما كان كذلك لأن ما بعد الظهر يسمى عشاء - بكسر العين - ولهذا يسمى الظهر إحدى صلاتي العشاء في الحديث. وذكر الإمام الأسبيجابي أن هذا في عرفهم وأما في عرفنا فوقت العشاء بعد صلاة العصر ا هـ. وهذا هو الواقع في عرف أهل مصر لأنهم يسمون ما يأكلونه بعد الزوال وسطانية. قيد بالعشاء لأن السحور هو الأكل بعد نصف الليل إلى طلوع الفجر مأخوذ من السحر وهو قريب السحر لكن روى المعلى عن محمد فيمن حلف لا يكلمه إلى السحر قال : إذا دخل ثلث الليل الأخير فليكلمه لأن وقت السحر ما قرب من الفجر. وقال هشام عن محمد : المساء مساآن أحدهما إذا زالت الشمس ألا ترى أنك تقول إذا زالت كيف أمسيت. والمساء الآخر إذا غربت الشمس فإذا حلف بعد الزوال لا يفعل كذا على غيبوبة الشمس لأنه لا يمكن حمل اليمين على المساء الأول فيحمل على الثاني ؛ البدائع .
His statement: '(And al-ghadā’ is eating from dawn to noon)' meaning al-taghaddī (eating lunch) is eating at this time. We only explained it thus because al-ghadā’ in reality—with a fatha on the ghayn and madd—is a name for what is eaten at a specific time, not for the act of eating. The author omitted two conditions which Qāḍīkhān mentioned in his Fatāwā: He said: al-taghaddī is continuous eating by which satiety is intended at a specific time, which is from after the break of dawn to the zenith of the sun, from what is customarily eaten as lunch; and the lunch of each town is what its people customarily know. In al-Tabyīn: The amount that constitutes a violation (ḥanath) in eating is that it be more than half of satiety, because one or two mouthfuls are not called lunch customarily. The type of food is conditioned to be what the people of his town customarily eat, such that if he drinks milk and is satiated, he does not violate if he is a city-dweller, but if he is a Bedouin he violates. End quote. In al-Muḥīṭ: If he swears not to eat lunch, it is understood as bread; so if he eats rice, dates, and milk, he does not violate if he is not a Bedouin. If he swears about a past act, saying 'By Allah, I did not eat lunch today' while he had eaten rice and clarified butter, he should violate. If an Egyptian eats grapes, he does not violate unless he is from the countryside whose custom is to eat grapes at that time. End quote. There is a difference regarding its beginning time; al-Asbījābī mentioned that it is sunrise, and likewise in al-Khulāṣa, and it should be the relied-upon (view) due to custom, because eating before sunrise is not called lunch. The author indicated that if he swears to come to him at ghudwa (morning time) and he comes after dawn until noon, he has fulfilled his oath, because ghudwa is the time of lunch, as in al-Badā’i‘. As for ḍaḥwa, it is from after sunrise when prayer is permissible until noon, because it is the time of the Ḍuḥā prayer. Muḥammad said: If he swears not to do something in the morning (lā yuṣbiḥ), then 'morning' according to me is between sunrise and the rising of the major forenoon; when the major forenoon rises, the time of morning ends, because 'morning' (taṣbīḥ) is a verbal noun from ṣabāḥ, and the taf‘īl form indicates intensification, so it entails more than what would be indicated by 'not morning'. End quote. His statement: (And al-‘ashā’ is from it (i.e., noon) to midnight) meaning al-ta‘ashshī (eating dinner) is eating from noon to midnight. As for al-‘ashā’—with a fatha on the ‘ayn and madd—it is a name for what is eaten at this time, as was previously mentioned for lunch. The two conditions previously mentioned for lunch come here as well. We say this is because what is after noon is called ‘ishā’—with a kasra on the ‘ayn—and for this reason noon is called one of the two ‘ishā’ prayers in the hadith. Imam al-Asbījābī mentioned that this was in their custom, but in our custom the time of ‘ishā’ is after the ‘Aṣr prayer. End quote. This is the reality in the custom of the people of Egypt, because they call what they eat after noon 'wusṭāniyya'. It is restricted by 'al-‘ashā’' because al-saḥūr (pre-dawn meal) is eating after midnight until dawn, derived from saḥar (dawn), which is near dawn. However, al-Mu‘allā narrated from Muḥammad regarding one who swears not to speak to him until dawn (saḥar): He said: When the last third of the night enters, he may speak to him, because the time of saḥar is what is near dawn. Hishām said from Muḥammad: Evening (masā’) is of two types: one is when the sun declines—do you not see that you say when it declines 'How have you entered evening?'—and the other evening is when the sun sets. So if he swears after noon not to do something until evening, it is understood as sunset, because the oath cannot be interpreted as the first evening; therefore it is interpreted as the second. Al-Badā’i‘.
Source pages p. 546

Commentary
This passage from al-Baḥr al-Rā’iq is an expansion upon the matn of Kanz al-Daqā’iq. The commentator explains the definition of 'ghadā’' (lunch) and '‘ashā’' (dinner) in the context of oaths. The key point is that in Islamic law, when a person swears not to eat lunch or dinner, the determination of what constitutes lunch/dinner, their times, and the quantity of food required to break the oath is based on local custom (urf), not on a fixed legal definition. The commentator first clarifies that 'ghadā’' is the name of the food eaten at that specific time, not the act of eating. Qāḍīkhān adds two conditions: the eating must be continuous and intended for satiety, and the type of food must be what is customary in that locale. For example, if a city-dweller drinks milk and becomes full, he does not break his oath because milk is not typically a lunch food in cities, whereas a Bedouin would break his oath. Similarly, the quantity must be more than half of satiety; one or two mouthfuls are not sufficient. The commentator then discusses the starting time of lunch: although some say it begins at sunrise, the relied-upon view is that it begins at dawn (fajr) because custom does not call eating before sunrise 'lunch'. The commentator also cites examples about past oaths and about different times of day (ghudwa, ḍaḥwa, ṣabāḥ, taṣbīḥ). Moving to dinner (‘ashā’), the commentator explains that its time is from noon to midnight, and the same conditions apply. However, there is a difference of custom: in the early Arab custom, dinner time was after noon, but in later custom (especially in Egypt) it is after ‘Aṣr. The commentator also includes a discussion about 'masā’' (evening) and 'saḥar' (dawn) based on narrations from Muḥammad al-Shaybānī. This passage is crucial for understanding how custom (urf) plays a fundamental role in determining the scope of oaths in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
الغداء (al-ghadā’): In the context of oaths, it refers to the meal eaten between dawn and noon, which is customarily considered lunch. The term itself is the name of the food, not the act of eating.
التغدي (al-taghaddī): The act of eating lunch, defined as continuous eating intending satiety at a specific time.
العشاء (al-‘ashā’): The meal eaten from noon to midnight, customarily considered dinner. With a fatha on ‘ayn, it is the food; with a kasra, it refers to the time (i.e., evening prayer time).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a city-dweller swears 'I will not eat lunch' and then drinks milk until satiated during lunchtime, does he violate his oath?
A) Yes, because he ate until satiated.
B) No, because milk is not customarily eaten as lunch in a city.
C) Yes, because drinking milk is a form of eating.
D) No, because the quantity must be more than half of satiety.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, the definition of lunch and dinner depends on local custom, not a fixed schedule or food list.

Intention to Specify a Particular Item in an Oath is Not Accepted
Ruling that specifying a particular food, clothing, or drink in an oath is not valid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on specifying a particular item in a general oath and explain the linguistic reasoning behind why such intention is invalid.

Core Idea
A general oath is violated by any act; internal intention cannot specify what was not uttered.

This lesson examines a critical principle in Hanafi oath law: when a person swears using a general verb like 'I will not eat,' they cannot later claim they meant only a specific food to avoid violation. The Hanafi school holds that such an intention is not accepted, whether in court or religiously. The source passage presents the ruling and a sophisticated theoretical clarification from Fatḥ al-Qadīr. By the end, you will understand why the omitted object in these oaths cannot be specified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the opening statement: 'If you wear, or eat, or drink, and intend a specific thing, he is not believed at all.' What is the practical consequence?
The oath is violated by any act, not just the intended one.
2
Understand the initial rationale
The text from al-Hidāyah says the intention is futile because the object (food, drink) is not explicitly mentioned—it is merely implied (muqtaḍā). Why does that make specification invalid?
Implied elements do not carry explicit generality that can be specified.
3
Analyze the critique from Fatḥ al-Qadīr
Ibn al-Humām argues that the object is not muqtaḍā but maḥdhūf (omitted for brevity). Distinguish the two: muqtaḍā is necessary to make the statement true/valid; maḥdhūf is not. Give examples from the text.
This is a linguistic refinement, not a change in ruling.
4
Trace the conclusion
Even if the object is maḥdhūf, its generality cannot be specified. Why? Because the intent is only to negate the act itself, and the omitted object is not in the ruling of what is uttered. Connect to the consensus on time/place.
The generality of the omitted object is fixed and cannot be limited by intention.
5
Apply to examples
Test the ruling on cases from al-Badā’i‘ and Fatḥ al-Qadīr: swearing not to ride (intending horse), not to bathe (intending janāba), not to marry (intending a specific woman), not to live in a house (intending with rent). In each case, why is the intention invalid?
Contrast with oaths that include explicit attributes, where specification is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إن لبست أو أكلت أو شربت ونوى معيناً لم يصدق أصلاً) أي لا قضاء ولا ديانة لأن النية إنما تصح في الملفوظ والثوب والطعام والماء غير مذكور تنصيصاً والمقتضى له فلغت نية التخصيص فيه كما في الهداية وغيرها، فحنث بأي شيء أكل أو شرب أو لبس وتعقبهم في فتح القدير بأن التحقيق أن المفعول في لا آكل ولا ألبس ليس من باب المقتضى لأن المقتضى ما يقدر لتصحيح المنطوق وذلك بأن يكون الكلام مما يحكم بكذبه على ظاهره مثل رفع الخطأ والنسيان أو بعدم صحته شرعاً مثل أعتق عبدك عني، وليس قول القائل لا آكل يحكم بكذب قائله بمجرده ولا متضمناً حكماً يصح شرعاً، نعم المفعول أعني المأكول من ضروريات وجود فعل الأكل ومثله ليس من باب المقتضى وإلا كان كل كلام كذلك إذ لا بد أن يستدعي معناه زماناً أو مكاناً فكان لا يفرق بين قولنا الخطأ والنسيان مرفوعان وبين قام زيد وجلس عمرو فإنما هو من باب حذف المفعول اقتصاراً أو تناسياً. وطائفة من المشايخ وإن فرقوا بين المقتضى والمحذوف وجعلوا المحذوف يقبل العموم قلنا لك أن تقول : إن عمومه لا يقبل التخصيص، وقد صرح من المحققين جمع بأن من العمومات ما لا يقبل التخصيص مثل المعاني إذا قلنا بأن العموم من عوارض المعاني من عوارض الألفاظ وغير ذلك فكذلك هذا المحذوف إذ ليس في حكم المنطوق لتناسيه وعدم الالتفات إليه إذ ليس الغرض إلا الإخبار بمجرد الفعل على ما عرف أن الفعل المتعدي قد ينزل منزلة اللازم لما قلنا والاتفاق على عدم صحة التخصيص في باب المتعلقات من الزمان والمكان حتى لو نوى لا يأكل في مكان دون آخر أو زمان لا تصح نيته بالاتفاق ا هـ . وفي البدائع : حلف لا يركب ونوى الخيل لا يصدق قضاء ولا ديانة. وفي فتح القدير : حلف لا يغتسل أو لا ينكح وعنى من جنابة أو امرأة دون امرأة لا يصدق أصلاً، وكذا لا يسكن دار فلان وعنى بأجر ولم يسبق قبل ذلك كلام بأن استأجرها منه أو استعارها فأبى فحلف ينوي السكن بالإجارة والإعارة لا يصح أصلاً. وكذلك لو حلف لا يتزوج امرأة ونوى كوفية أو بصرية لا يصح لأنه يصح نية تخصيص الصفة، ولو نوى حبشية أو عربية صحت كما هو
His statement: '(If you wear, or eat, or drink, and intend a specific thing, he is not believed at all)' meaning neither judicially nor religiously, because intention is only valid in what is explicitly uttered, and the garment, food, and water are not mentioned explicitly, and they are implied (by the verb); thus the intention of specification is futile, as in al-Hidāyah and others. He therefore violates with anything he eats, drinks, or wears. They criticized him in Fatḥ al-Qadīr, saying: In truth, the object in 'I will not eat' or 'I will not wear' is not from the category of what is implied (muqtaḍā), because what is implied is what is assumed to correct the utterance, such that the speech is considered false in its apparent meaning, like 'mistake and forgetfulness are lifted' or such that it is legally invalid, like 'free your slave from me'. The statement 'I will not eat' is not considered false merely in its utterance, nor does it contain a legal ruling that is valid. Yes, the object—meaning the eaten thing—is from the necessities of the existence of the act of eating, and the like is not from the category of what is implied; otherwise, every speech would be such, since any speech necessarily requires its meaning to have a time or place, so there would be no difference between our saying 'mistake and forgetfulness are lifted' and 'Zaid stood up and Amr sat down'. It is merely a case of omission of the object for brevity or disregard. A group of scholars, although they distinguished between what is implied (muqtaḍā) and what is omitted (maḥdhūf) and considered the omitted to accept generality, we say to you: Its generality does not accept specification. Indeed, a group of verifiers stated that some general things do not accept specification, such as meanings—if we say that generality is an accident of meanings, not of words, etc. So likewise this omitted (object) is not in the ruling of what is uttered due to its disregard and lack of attention; the intent is only to inform of the mere act, as it is known that a transitive verb may be treated as intransitive for what we said; and there is agreement on the invalidity of specification in the category of related matters (muta‘alliqāt) of time and place, such that even if he intends not to eat in a particular place or time, his intention is not valid by consensus. End quote. In al-Badā’i‘: He swore not to ride, and intended a horse; he is not believed judicially nor religiously. In Fatḥ al-Qadīr: He swore not to bathe or not to marry, and meant from janāba or a specific woman; he is not believed at all. Likewise, he swore not to live in so-and-so’s house and meant with rent, and there was no prior speech that he had rented it from him or borrowed it, so he swears intending living by rent or borrowing; it is not valid at all. Similarly, if he swore not to marry a woman and intended a woman from Kufa or Basra, it is not valid, because intending a specific attribute is valid; but if he intended an Ethiopian or Arab woman, it is valid, as is (the ruling).
Source pages p. 547

Commentary
This passage addresses a critical principle in Hanafi oath jurisprudence: when a person swears using a general verb like 'I will not eat', 'I will not drink', or 'I will not wear', they cannot later claim that they meant a specific item (e.g., only bread, only a particular garment) to avoid the oath. The ruling is that such an intention is not accepted, either in a court (qaḍā’) or religiously (diyāna). The reason given in al-Hidāyah is that the object is not explicitly mentioned, only implied by the verb, so specifying it is futile. However, the commentator Ibn al-Humām in Fatḥ al-Qadīr offers a more nuanced analysis: he argues that the object in 'I will not eat' is not a 'muqtaḍā' (something implied to correct the speech) but rather an omitted object for brevity. He distinguishes between two types of omission: muqtaḍā (implied to make the statement true or legally valid) and maḥdhūf (omitted for conciseness). The maḥdhūf can be general, but its generality cannot be specified by intention. This is because the speaker's intent is simply to negate the act itself, not to specify a particular object. Moreover, there is consensus that one cannot specify a particular time or place in such oaths. The passage then provides several examples from al-Badā’i‘ and Fatḥ al-Qadīr to illustrate: swearing not to ride and intending only horses, swearing not to bathe and intending only janāba, swearing not to live in a house and intending only with rent, etc. In all these cases, the intention is not accepted. However, there is an exception: if the oath includes an explicit adjective (e.g., 'I will not marry a woman from Kufa'), then the intention to specify that attribute is valid. This shows the precision of Hanafi legal reasoning regarding verbal utterances and intention.

Key Terms
المقتضى (al-muqtaḍā): Something that must be assumed in the speech to make it true or legally valid, even if not expressed.
المحذوف (al-maḥdhūf): An omitted element in speech that is not necessary for the statement's truth or validity, but is left out for brevity.
التخصيص (al-takhṣīṣ): Specifying a general term to a particular subset, which is not valid in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears 'I will not eat' and intends only bread. He later eats rice. According to the Hanafi school, does he violate his oath?
A) No, because he intended only bread.
B) Yes, because intention to specify a particular food is not accepted.
C) No, because rice is not bread.
D) Yes, because he ate any food at all.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a general oath cannot be limited by internal intention; the oath is violated by any act that falls under the general verb.

intermediate
10 min
Intention to Specify in Oaths Without Object: Not Accepted
Intra-Hanafi dispute on reasoning for invalidating intention in oaths without explicit object

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intention in oaths without explicit object and distinguish between valid and invalid specifications by applying the concepts of muqtada and mahdhuf.

Core Idea
Intention to specify in an oath without explicit object is invalid; the oath covers all instances.

When someone swears 'I will not eat' without naming a specific food, can they later claim they intended only a particular dish? The Hanafi school rules that such intention is not accepted—neither in court nor in conscience. However, the reasoning behind this rule sparked an intra-Hanafi debate between the authors of al-Hidayah and al-Fath al-Qadir. This lesson unpacks that debate and clarifies when specification of an attribute (like race) is valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the matn: 'If he wears or eats or drinks and intends a specific thing, it is not accepted at all.' This means the oath is general; by eating anything, he breaks it.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position from al-Hidayah.
2
Understand the Muqtada Argument
The initial reasoning is that the object (food, clothing) is not mentioned explicitly nor is it muqtada (implicitly required for the utterance to be true). Therefore, the oath remains general and no specification is allowed.
Muqtada is what must be estimated to correct the utterance, e.g., the object in 'free your slave from me' is muqtada.
3
Examine the Critique from Fath al-Qadir
Ibn al-Humam argues that the object in 'I will not eat' is not muqtada because the statement is not false on its face without it. Rather, the object is mahdhuf (deleted for brevity or forgetfulness).
The critique changes the reasoning but does not change the ruling.
4
Distinguish Muqtada vs. Mahdhuf
Muqtada is necessary to correct the utterance; mahdhuf is merely omitted. The object in oaths is mahdhuf, and its generality does not accept specification. This is because the purpose is to negate the act absolutely.
This distinction is key to understanding why time/place specifications are also invalid by consensus.
5
Apply to Examples
Review the examples: swearing not to ride (intending horse) => invalid; swearing not to marry a woman (intending a Kufan) => invalid; but intending Habashiyya (a type) => valid because it specifies a genus attribute already inherent in the noun 'woman'? Actually, the text says intention to specify an attribute is valid, meaning if the intended specification is part of the genus (like race), it is accepted.
The distinction: specifying an additional condition (e.g., with rent) is invalid; specifying a subcategory of the genus is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إن لبست أو أكلت أو شربت ونوى معيناً لم يصدق أصلاً) أي لا قضاء ولا ديانة لأن النية إنما تصح في الملفوظ والثوب والطعام والماء غير مذكور تنصيصاً والمقتضى له فلغت نية التخصيص فيه كما في الهداية ،وغيرها، فحنث بأي شيء أكل أو شرب أو لبس وتعقبهم في فتح القدير بأن التحقيق أن المفعول في لا آكل ولا ألبس ليس من باب المقتضى لأن المقتضى ما يقدر لتصحيح المنطوق وذلك بأن يكون الكلام مما يحكم بكذبه على ظاهره مثل رفع الخطأ والنسيان أو بعدم صحته شرعاً مثل أعتق عبدك عني، وليس قول القائل لا آكل يحكم بكذب قائله بمجرده ولا متضمناً حكماً . يصح شرعاً، نغم المفعول أعني المأكول من ضروريات وجود فعل الأكل ومثله ليس من باب المقتضى وإلا كان كل كلام كذلك إذ لا بد أن يستدعي معناه زماناً أو مكاناً فكان لا يفرق بين قولنا الخطأ والنسيان مرفوعان وبين قام زيد وجلس عمرو فإنما هو من باب حذف المفعول اقتصاراً أو تناسياً. وطائفة من المشايخ وإن فرقوا بين المقتضى والمحذوف وجعلوا المحذوف يقبل العموم قلنا لك أن تقول : إن عمومه لا يقبل التخصيص، وقد صرح من المحققين جمع بأن من العمومات ما لا يقبل التخصيص مثل المعاني إذا قلنا بأن العموم من عوارض المعاني من عوارض الألفاظ وغير ذلك فكذلك هذا المحذوف إذ ليس في حكم المنطوق لتناسيه وعدم الالتفات إليه إذ ليس الغرض إلا الإخبار بمجرد الفعل على ما عرف أن الفعل المتعدي قد ينزل منزلة اللازم لما قلنا والاتفاق على عدم صحة التخصيص في باب المتعلقات من الزمان والمكان حتى لو نوى لا يأكل في مكان دون آخر أو زمان لا تصح نيته بالاتفاق ا هـ . وفي البدائع : حلف لا يركب ونوى الخيل لا يصدق قضاء ولا ديانة. وفي فتح القدير : حلف لا يغتسل أو لا ينكح وعنى من جنابة أو امرأة دون امرأة لا يصدق أصلاً، وكذا لا يسكن دار فلان وعنى بأجر ولم يسبق قبل ذلك كلام بأن استأجرها منه أو استعارها فأبى فخلف ينوي السكن بالإجارة والإعارة لا يصح أصلاً. وكذلك لو حلف لا يتزوج امرأة ونوى كوفية أو بصرية لا . لأنه يصح نية تخصيص الصفة، ولو نوى حبشية أو عربية صحت كما هو
His statement: (If he wears or eats or drinks and intends a specific thing, it is not accepted at all) meaning neither in court nor in conscience because intention is only valid in what is uttered, and the garment, food, and water are not explicitly mentioned, nor are they implied (muqtada), so the intention to specify is void as in al-Hidayah and others. So he breaks his oath by anything he eats, drinks, or wears. And they are criticized in Fath al-Qadir by stating that the object in 'I will not eat' or 'I will not wear' is not of the type of muqtada because muqtada is what is estimated to correct the utterance, such that the statement would be deemed false on its face, like 'mistake and forgetfulness are lifted' or invalid legally like 'free your slave from me.' The statement 'I will not eat' is not deemed false just by itself, nor does it contain a legal ruling. Rather, the object, i.e., the eaten thing, is a necessary part of the existence of the act of eating, and such is not from the type of muqtada; otherwise every speech would be such, since it necessarily implies a time or place. Thus there would be no difference between saying 'mistake and forgetfulness are lifted' and 'Zaid stood' and 'Amr sat.' This is simply deletion of the object for brevity or forgetfulness. And a group of mashaykh distinguished between muqtada and mahdhuf, making the mahdhuf accept generality. We say: its generality does not accept specification. Some verifiers have stated that among general terms there are those that do not accept specification, like meanings if we say generality is a property of meanings as opposed to words, etc. So too this mahdhuf is not ruled like utterance due to its being forgotten and not attended to, since the purpose is only to inform of the mere act, as it is known that a transitive verb may be treated as intransitive as we said. And there is agreement on the invalidity of specification in the realm of time and place, such that if he intends not to eat in a particular place or time, his intention is invalid by consensus. In al-Bada'i': He swore not to ride and intended a horse; it is not accepted legally or religiously. In Fath al-Qadir: He swore not to bathe or not to marry and intended from janabah or a particular woman; it is not accepted at all. Likewise, he swore not to live in so-and-so's house and intended with rent, and there was no prior mention of renting or borrowing; his intention is invalid. Also, if he swore not to marry a woman and intended a Kufan or Basran, it is not accepted because intention to specify an attribute is valid, but if he intended a Habashiyya or an Arabiyya, it is valid.
Source pages p. 547

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute over the ruling on intention in oaths without an explicit object. The matn states that if someone swears not to eat, drink, or wear something and intends a specific item, his intention is not accepted—neither in court nor in conscience. The basis is that the object is not mentioned explicitly or implied, so the oath is general. The commentator then presents a critique from Fath al-Qadir (Ibn al-Humam) arguing that the object in such oaths is not 'muqtada' (implicitly required) but rather a deletion for brevity, and that general terms under deletion cannot be specified. Both sides agree on the ruling but differ on the reasoning. The passage then gives examples from al-Bada'i' and Fath al-Qadir to illustrate: if one swears not to ride and intends a horse, it is not accepted. But if one swears not to marry a woman and intends a specific attribute like Habashiyya, that intention is valid because the attribute is specified in the utterance? Actually the examples show that specifying a race or type is valid, but specifying a particular location or rent is not. The key distinction is between specifying the genus (e.g., Habashiyya) versus specifying an additional condition (e.g., rent) that is not part of the genus.

Key Terms
المقتضى (al-muqtada): What is implicitly required for the utterance to be correct; the author argues the omitted object is not muqtada.
المحذوف (al-mahdhuf): What is omitted for brevity or forgetfulness, not necessary for correctness; here the object is considered mahdhuf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling presented, if someone swears 'I will not marry a woman' and intends a particular woman (e.g., his cousin) but does not mention her, is his oath broken if he marries another woman?
A) No, because his intention restricts the oath to that woman.
B) Yes, because the oath is general and the intention is invalid.
C) Yes, but only if he marries a woman of a different race.
D) No, because the oath is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an oath without an explicit object remains general; any internal intention to specify is invalid, except when the specification is an attribute inherent to the noun (like race in 'woman').

Intention to Specify in Oaths: Religious vs Legal Acceptance
Ruling on specification in oaths: diyanah vs qada' with examples from al-Bada'i'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between diyanatan (religious) and qada'an (legal) acceptance of specification in oaths, and apply the Hanafi principle that nakira in shart is general.

Core Idea
Specifying an indefinite item in an oath is accepted religiously but not legally — the general wording prevails in court.

When a person swears an oath using a general term like 'garment' or 'food,' but inwardly intends a specific item, the Hanafi school makes a critical distinction: the intention is credited between the person and Allah, but the court enforces the literal general meaning. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this distinction and its application in various scenarios from al-Bada'i'.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the central ruling: adding a specific item (e.g., ثوب, طعام, شراب) in an oath is accepted only religiously, not legally.
The key terms are 'diyanatan' (religiously) and 'qada'an' (legally).
2
Understand the legal principle
Grasp why: the added noun is nakira in a conditional clause, which makes it general, just like nakira in negation. Specification contradicts the apparent, so the judge does not accept it.
The principle: 'al-nakirah fi al-shart ta'mm ka-al-nakirah fi al-nafy'.
3
Analyze the examples from al-Bada'i'
Work through the two sets of examples: (a) when specification is on an uttered attribute (accepted religiously) vs. (b) when it is on an unuttered condition (invalid).
Example of 'this standing man' vs. 'this man' without mention of standing.
4
Apply the distinction to a new scenario
Use the principle to decide a hypothetical: if someone swears not to eat meat but intends goat meat only, what is the ruling?
Legally broken, religiously not broken.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah)
The difference: religiously he may not be sinful if he fulfills his intention, but legally he may be liable for oath-breaking and may need kaffarah.
The judge rules based on apparent wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أصلاً ولو زاد ثوباً أو طعاماً أو شراباً دين لا يشرب من دجلة على الكرع بخلاف من ديانة لأنه تخصيص في الجنس. وفي البدائع : لو حلف لا يكلم هذا الرجل وعنى به ما دام قائماً لكنه لم يتكلم بالقيام كانت نيته باطلة وحنث إن كلمه ولو حلف لا يكلم هذا القائم وعنى به ما دام قائماً دین لورود التخصيص على الملفوظ ، وكذلك إذا قال والله لأضر بن فلاناً خمسين وهو ينوي بسوط بعينه فبأي شيء ضربه فقد خرج من يمينه والنية باطلة. ولو قال والله لا أتزوج امرأة وعنى امرأة كان أبوها يعمل كذا وكذا فهو باطل ا هـ . قوله: (ولو زاد ثوباً أو طعاماً أو شراباً دين) أي قبل منه نية التخصيص ديانة لا قضاء لأنه نكرة في الشرط فتعم كالنكرة في النفي لكنه خلاف الظاهر فلا يصدقه القاضي. وفي البدائع : قال والله لا أتزوج امرأة على وجه الأرض ينوي امرأة بعينها قال يصدق فيما بينه الله تعالى بخلاف ما إذا قال لا أشتري جارية ونوى متولدة فإن نيته باطلة لأنه تخصيص الصفة فأشبه الكوفية والبصرية ا هـ .
Originally, and if he adds a garment, food, or drink, it is accepted religiously - he does not break his oath by drinking from the Tigris on the bank unlike the one who does it religiously because it is specification of genus. In al-Bada'i': If he swears not to speak to this man and intends as long as he is standing but did not mention standing, his intention is invalid and he breaks his oath if he speaks to him. If he swears not to speak to this standing man and intends as long as he is standing, it is accepted religiously because specification comes on the uttered word. Similarly, if he says 'By Allah, I will harm so-and-so fifty' and intends a particular whip, then by anything he hits him he has exited his oath and his intention is invalid. If he says 'By Allah, I will not marry a woman' and intends a woman whose father does such-and-such, it is invalid. His statement: (And if he adds a garment, food, or drink, it is accepted religiously) meaning his intention of specification is accepted religiously, not legally, because it is a nakira in a conditional clause, so it is general like nakira in negation, but it is against the apparent so the judge does not accept it. In al-Bada'i': He said 'By Allah, I will not marry a woman on the face of the earth' intending a specific woman; he is believed between him and Allah, unlike if he said 'I will not buy a slave girl' and intended one taken captive (muwallada), his intention is void because it is specification of attribute, resembling the Kufan and Basran.
Source pages p. 548

Commentary
This passage presents a nuanced ruling: if a person adds a specific item (e.g., 'this garment') to the oath, his intention to limit the oath to that item is accepted religiously (between him and Allah) but not legally in court. The reason is that the added word is a nakira (indefinite noun) in a conditional clause, which makes it general, similar to nakira in negation. However, because specifying contradicts the apparent meaning, the judge cannot accept it. The passage then gives examples from al-Bada'i' to distinguish when intention is valid: if the specification is on an uttered attribute (e.g., 'this standing man' and intending 'as long as he stands'), it is accepted religiously; but if the specification is hidden (e.g., 'this man' intending a certain condition without mention), it is invalid. The key is whether the specification is on the explicit words or on something not mentioned. The example of 'fifty' with a whip shows that intending a particular whip is invalid because the whip is not mentioned.

Key Terms
النكرة في الشرط (al-nakirah fi al-shart): An indefinite noun in a conditional clause; in Hanafi usul, it is general like nakira in negation, so it does not accept specification in court but may accept it religiously.
ديانة (diyanatan): Religiously; referring to the acceptance of intention between the person and Allah.
قضاء (qada'an): Legally; referring to the ruling of a judge based on apparent evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, if a person swears 'By Allah, I will not eat any food' but intends only to avoid eating meat, and he eats bread, what is the outcome?
A) He has broken his oath legally and religiously.
B) He has broken his oath legally but not religiously.
C) He has not broken his oath legally or religiously.
D) He has broken his oath religiously but not legally.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between religious and legal acceptance of intention in oaths: the former is a matter between the person and Allah, while the latter is based on the apparent meaning of the words.

Oaths on Leaving and Dwelling: Intention for Specific Types Accepted
Ruling that oaths about leaving and dwelling accept intention of specific type due to their diverse natures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi exception for oaths involving khuruj and musakanah, explain why intention to specify a type is valid, and apply the rule to distinguish acceptable from unacceptable specifications.

Core Idea
Oaths about leaving or dwelling are flexible: intention for a specific type is valid because these acts are inherently diverse.

In Hanafi law, when someone swears an oath mentioning a general act, their intention to specify a particular type is usually not accepted if the specification is not mentioned. However, a well-known exception exists for the acts of leaving (al-khuruj) and dwelling (al-musakanah). Because these acts have distinct types with different legal rulings (e.g., travel vs. non-travel, complete cohabitation vs. general dwelling), the oath-taker's intention to specify a type is honored. This lesson explores the reasoning behind this exception and the scholarly debate surrounding it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The general rule in oaths is that non-verbalized specifications are invalid. For example, swearing not to go out and intending simply to not go out to a specific place is not accepted.
2
Recognize the exception
For khuruj and musakanah, intention to specify a type (e.g., travel, living in one house) is valid. This is because these acts are considered diverse genera with subtypes.
The exception is not for specification of a particular location or person, but for a type of the act itself.
3
Understand the reasoning
The diversity of these acts is evidenced by their different legal rulings (e.g., traveling has different rules from a simple outing). Thus, the Law treats them as multiple types, allowing intention to narrow the oath's scope.
This reasoning is rooted in the concept that actions are defined by their legal consequences.
4
Analyze the critique
Fath al-Qadir challenges this, arguing that all external actions (like leaving) are essentially one type; difference only arises through Shariah rulings. The Hanafi response is that the very existence of different rulings proves the Law considers them distinct.
This debate remains open, but the exception is operative in practice.
5
Apply the rule to scenarios
Test the exception: if A swears not to go out and intends travel, a non-travel outing does not break the oath. But if A intends a specific place like Baghdad (not a type), the intention is invalid, and any outing breaks the oath.
The key is whether the intention specifies a type of the act (e.g., travel) or an external detail (e.g., a location).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخرج عن هذا الأصل فعل الخروج والمساكنة فإذا قال إن خرجت فعبدي حر ونوى السفر مثلاً يصدق ديانة فلا يحنث بالخروج إلى غيره تخصيصاً لنفس الخروج بخلاف ما إذا نوى الخروج إلى مكان خاص كبغداد حيث لا يصح لأن المكان غير مذكور، وكذا لو حلف لا يساكن فلاناً ونوى المساكنة في بيت واحد يصح قالوا : لأن الخروج في نفسه متنوع إلى سفر وغيره حتى أحكامها. وكذا المساكنة متنوعة إلى كاملة. المساكنة في بيت واحد وإلى مطلقة تكون في دار وفيه بحث مذكور في فتح القدير . وهي اختلفت وهي ما قوله: (وخرج عن هذا الأصل الخ الصواب أن يقال ولا يرد على هذا الأصل لأن قوله «لأن الخروج في نفسه متنوع إلى سفر وغيره وكذا المساكنة يفيد أنه في هاتين المسألتين ليس من تخصيص غير الملفوظ بل من تخصيص الملفوظ لأن حاصله أن كلاً من الخروج والمساكنة جنس ذو أنواع فنية فيه نية أحد الأنواع للجنس المذكور فليس من باب المقتضى قوله : (ونوى المساكنة فالبيت واحد يصح) كذا في بعض النسخ وهو الصواب وفي بعضها لا يصح بزيادة «لا» وهو غير صحيح كما لا يخفى قوله : (وفيه بحث مذكور في فتح القدير حيث قال : والحق أن الأفعال الخارجية لا أن تكون إلا نوعاً واحداً لا فرق في ذلك بين الغسل ونحوه وبين الخروج ونحوه من الشراء، فكما أن اتحاد الغسل بسبب أنه ليس إلا إمرار الماء كذلك الخروج ليس إلا قطع المسافة غير أنه يوصف بالطول والقصر في الزمان فلا يصير منقسماً إلى نوعين إلا باختلاف الأحكام شرعاً فإن عند ذلك علمنا اعتبار الشرع إياها كذلك كما في الخروج المختلف الأحكام في السفر غيره والشراء لنفسه وغيره فإنه مختلف حكمهما فيحكم بتعدد النوع في ذلك، ولا يخفى أن المساكنة والسكنى ليس فيهما اختلاف أحكام الشرع لطائفة منهما بالنسبة إلى طائفة أخرى، وكل في نفسه نوع لأن الكل قرار في
And there is an exception to this principle for the acts of leaving and dwelling. So if he says 'If I go out, my slave is free' and intends travel, for example, he is believed religiously, so he does not break his oath by going out for something else, as a specification of the act of leaving itself, unlike if he intends going out to a specific place like Baghdad, where it is not valid because the place is not mentioned. Similarly, if he swears not to live with so-and-so and intends living in one house, it is valid. They said: because leaving itself is diverse into travel and other, and their rulings differ. Similarly, dwelling is diverse into complete dwelling in one house and absolute dwelling in a building. There is discussion mentioned in Fath al-Qadir. And they differed... His statement: (And there is exception to this principle) the correct thing is to say that it is not an objection to this principle because his statement 'because leaving itself is diverse into travel and other, and similarly dwelling' indicates that in these two issues it is not specification of what is not uttered but specification of what is uttered, because the result is that each of leaving and dwelling is a genus with types, so the intention is one of the types of the mentioned genus, thus it is not from the category of muqtada. His statement: (and intended dwelling in one house is valid) as in some copies, and in some copies 'is not valid' with an extra 'la', which is incorrect as is clear. His statement: (and there is discussion mentioned in Fath al-Qadir) where he said: The truth is that external actions cannot be but one type; there is no difference between bath and its like and leaving and its like from purchase. Just as the unity of bath is due to its being only the passing of water, likewise leaving is only the cutting of distance, except that it is described by shortness and length in time, so it does not become divided into two types except by difference of legal rulings. For then we know that the Law considers them such, as in leaving which has different rulings in travel and other, and purchase for oneself and others, for their rulings differ, so multiplicity of type is ruled by that. It is clear that dwelling and residence have no difference in legal rulings for one group versus another, and each is itself a type because all is settlement in
Source pages p. 548

Commentary
This passage addresses an exception to the general rule that intention to specify is invalid. The acts of 'khuruj' (leaving/going out) and 'musakanah' (dwelling/living) are considered diverse in nature because they have different types with different legal rulings (e.g., travel vs. non-travel). Therefore, if someone swears not to go out and intends travel, his intention is accepted religiously because 'going out' is a genus with types. Similarly, swearing not to live with someone and intending one house is valid because 'living' can be of different degrees. The passage then discusses a critique from Fath al-Qadir (Ibn al-Humam) who argues that all external actions are essentially one type unless the Shariah distinguishes between them. For example, washing is just passing water, leaving is just distance. But the Law differentiates travel from non-travel based on different rulings, so multiplicity of type is established. The discussion is left open with a 'بحث' (research). The author of Bahr al-Raiq seems to favor the exception while noting the debate.

Key Terms
الخروج (al-khuruj): The act of going out or leaving; considered a genus with types like travel and non-travel.
المساكنة (al-musakanah): The act of dwelling or living with someone; considered diverse into complete cohabitation in one house and general dwelling in a building.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi exception, if someone swears 'If I go out, my slave is free' and intends to go out for travel, but then goes out for a walk, is the slave freed?
A) Yes, because going out is general and any outing breaks the oath.
B) No, because he intended travel (a type of going out) and the walk is not travel.
C) Yes, because the intention is not accepted for non-travel outings.
D) No, because the oath is invalid due to uncertainty.

Key Takeaway
Intention to specify a type of leaving or dwelling is valid because these acts are considered diverse in Hanafi law, enabling flexibility in oaths.

Intention to Specify a Part of a General Category in Oaths
Explains the ruling on specifying a subset of a general category in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between diyanah and qada in the context of specifying a part of a general category in oaths, and apply the ruling to common scenarios.

Core Idea
Intention to specify a subset in oaths is accepted diyanah but not qada, unless the action has natural subtypes.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a nuanced Hanafi rule regarding intention in oaths. When someone uses a general term but intends only a specific part, that intention is valid before Allah but not enforceable in court. However, for actions like 'going out' or 'cohabiting', the specification is accepted even judicially because these actions have inherent varieties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Read the opening statement: if a person adds a specification like 'garment' or 'food' after a general oath, the intention is accepted diyanah (religiously) but not qada (judicially). Understand that this is the default rule.
The phrase 'دين لا قضاء' is key.
2
Understand the linguistic basis
The indefinite noun in a condition (نكرة في الشرط) implies generality, just like the indefinite in negation. Therefore, the apparent meaning covers all instances, and the judge relies on the apparent meaning unless evidence contradicts.
This is a Hanafi usul principle.
3
Examine exceptions for natural types
Note that actions like 'going out' (خروج) and 'cohabiting' (مساكنة) have distinct natural types (e.g., travel vs. errand). Specifying a type is accepted even in court because the specification pertains to the very nature of the act, not an external attribute.
Refer to the discussion in Fath al-Qadir.
4
Distinguish valid vs. invalid specifications
A specification is invalid if it refers to an attribute not part of the genus (e.g., intending a woman with a specific father's occupation, or a slave-girl born from a union). Contrast this with specifying a type of the action itself.
The examples from Al-Bada'i' illustrate this.
5
Apply to new scenarios
Given a new oath scenario, determine whether the specification is accepted diyanah only or also qada, based on whether the specification concerns a natural subtype or an external attribute.
Practice with the tasks below.

كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في الأكل والشرب واللبس والكلام أصلاً ولو زاد ثوباً أو طعاماً أو شراباً دين لا يشرب من دجلة على الكرع بخلاف من ديانة لأنه تخصيص في الجنس. وفي البدائع: لو حلف لا يكلم هذا الرجل وعنى به ما دام قائماً لكنه لم يتكلم بالقيام كانت نيته باطلة وحنث إن كلمه ولو حلف لا يكلم هذا القائم وعنى به ما دام قائماً دين لورود التخصيص على الملفوظ، وكذلك إذا قال والله لأضر بن فلاناً خمسين وهو ينوي بسوط بعينه فبأي شيء ضربه فقد خرج من يمينه والنية باطلة. ولو قال والله لا أتزوج امرأة وعنى امرأة كان أبوها يعمل كذا وكذا فهو باطل ا هـ . وخرج عن هذا الأصل فعل الخروج والمساكنة فإذا قال إن خرجت فعبدي حر ونوى السفر مثلاً يصدق ديانة فلا يحنث بالخروج إلى غيره تخصيصاً لنفس الخروج بخلاف ما إذا نوى الخروج إلى مكان خاص كبغداد حيث لا يصح لأن المكان غير مذكور، وكذا لو حلف لا يساكن فلاناً ونوى المساكنة في بيت واحد يصح قالوا: لأن الخروج في نفسه متنوع إلى سفر وغيره حتى أحكامها. وكذا المساكنة متنوعة إلى كاملة المساكنة في بيت واحد وإلى مطلقة تكون في دار وفيه بحث مذكور في فتح القدير . قوله: (ولو) زاد ثوباً أو طعاماً أو شراباً دين أي قبل منه نية التخصيص ديانة لا قضاء لأنه نكرة في الشرط فتعم كالنكرة في النفي لكنه خلاف الظاهر فلا يصدقه القاضي. وفي البدائع: قال والله لا أتزوج امرأة على وجه الأرض ينوي امرأة بعينها قال يصدق فيما بينه الله تعالى بخلاف ما إذا قال لا أشتري جارية ونوى متولدة فإن نيته باطلة لأنه تخصيص الصفة فأشبه الكوفية والبصرية ا هـ . قيد المصنف رحمه الله بكونه نوى البعض دون البعض .
Book of Oaths, Chapter of Oaths on Eating, Drinking, Clothing, and Speech. As a principle, and if he adds a garment, food, or drink (as a specification), it is accepted religiously, not like one who drinks from the Tigris on all fours, contrary to one who does so religiously, because it is a specification within the genus. In Al-Bada'i': If he swears not to speak to this man and intends by it as long as he is standing, but he did not speak regarding standing, his intention is invalid, and he breaks his oath if he speaks to him. If he swears not to speak to this standing man and intends by it as long as he is standing, it is accepted religiously because the specification pertains to what is uttered. Similarly, if he says 'By Allah, I will definitely harm so-and-so fifty (times)' intending a specific whip, then he strikes him with anything, he has exited from his oath and the intention is invalid. If he says 'By Allah, I will not marry a woman' and intends a woman whose father works such-and-such, it is invalid. End quote. Excluded from this principle is the action of going out and cohabiting. So if he says 'If I go out, my slave is free' and intends traveling, for example, he is believed religiously, so he does not break his oath by going out for other purposes, as a specification of going out itself, contrary to if he intended going out to a specific place like Baghdad, where it is not valid because the place is not mentioned. Similarly, if he swears not to cohabit with so-and-so and intends cohabiting in one house, it is valid. They said: Because going out itself is varied into travel and other types such that their rulings differ. Similarly, cohabiting is varied into complete cohabitation in one house and general cohabitation in a dwelling, and there is a discussion mentioned in Fath al-Qadir. His statement: '(And if) he adds a garment, food, or drink, it is accepted religiously' means that the intention of specification is accepted religiously, not judicially, because it is indefinite in the condition so it generalizes like the indefinite in negation, but it is contrary to the apparent meaning, so the judge does not believe him. In Al-Bada'i': He said, 'By Allah, I will not marry a woman on the face of the earth' intending a specific woman; he said he is believed between him and Allah, contrary to if he says 'I will not buy a slave-girl' and intends one born from a union, his intention is invalid because it is a specification by attribute, so it resembles (specifying) Kufan or Basran (women). End quote. The author (may Allah have mercy on him) restricted it to when he intends some (of the genus) and not others.
Source pages p. 548

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addresses the issue of intention (niyyah) in oaths when the swearer intends to specify a part of a general category. The principle is that if a person swears using a general term (like 'food' or 'drink') but intends only a specific kind (e.g., wheat bread or a particular drink), that intention is accepted between him and Allah (diyanah) but not in court (qada). This is because the indefinite noun in the condition implies generality (like indefinite in negation), and the intention contradicts the apparent meaning, so the judge does not accept it. Examples are given: swearing not to drink from the Tigris on all fours (a specific manner) is treated differently because it is a specification of the genus (manner of drinking) that is not contrary to the general term. In Al-Bada'i', examples illustrate where specifying an attribute (like a woman whose father works in a certain trade) is invalid because it is a specification by attribute, which is not a true part of the genus. However, an exception is made for actions like 'going out' (khuruj) and 'cohabiting' (musakanah). These actions are considered to have natural types (e.g., going out for travel vs. other purposes) that differ in their legal rulings. Therefore, specifying a particular type (e.g., intending travel when swearing not to go out) is valid both religiously and judicially, because the specification pertains to the very act of going out, not to an external attribute. The discussion from Fath al-Qadir is referenced for further detail. This lesson emphasizes the Hanafi approach to linguistic interpretation and intention in oaths, distinguishing between what is accepted in private conscience and what is enforceable in court.

Key Terms
ديانة (diyānah): The acceptance of an oath as truthful between the person and Allah, regardless of court verification.
قضاء (qaḍāʾ): The judicial acceptance of an oath, requiring outward evidence and subject to court ruling.
تخصيص (takhrīṣ): Specifying a particular part or type within a general category by intention.
نكرة في الشرط (nakirah fī al-sharṭ): An indefinite noun in a conditional clause, which implies generality (like indefinite in negation).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'By Allah, I will not drink water,' but intends only water from the Nile. He later drinks water from a well. According to the Hanafi school, is he considered to have broken his oath?
A) Yes, because the general term 'water' includes all water, and the intention is not accepted in court.
B) No, because his intention to specify Nile water is accepted both diyanah and qada.
C) Yes, but only if the witness brings him to court.
D) No, because the indefinite in condition implies generality only if not specified.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, natural subtypes of an action (like travel vs. errand for 'going out') allow specification even in court, while specifying a particular attribute of a general category (like a specific kind of food) is only accepted diyanah.

Oath with General Term Intending Specific: Truthfulness and Ruling
Exploring the Hanafi distinction between qada and diyana in oath law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the judicial (qada) and religious (diyana) acceptance of intention in oaths and apply the Hanafi ruling when a swearer intends all instances of a general term.

Core Idea
Intention in general oaths is valid before Allah but may not be accepted in court if it contradicts the apparent meaning.

In Hanafi oath law, the words of an oath are interpreted according to their apparent meaning. However, the swearer's intention can specify or generalize that meaning. This passage explores a specific case: when someone swears using a general term like 'food' or 'water' and intends all instances of that term. The key distinction is between how the court (qada) and Allah (diyana) view this intention. By the end of this lesson, you will grasp this nuance and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is an oath using a general term. The ruling concerns whether the swearer is believed when he claims he intended all instances. The primary ruling from al-Muhit is that he is believed both in court and religiously and does not break his oath.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof comes from earlier Hanafi authorities: al-Muhit, al-Bada'i, and al-Kashf al-Kabir. Each provides a perspective on the interpretation of general oaths and the effect of intention.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Al-Muhit states the ruling straightforwardly. Al-Bada'i explains that the oath defaults to a part of the genus, but intention can generalize it. Al-Kashf al-Kabir says the default is the minimum, but intention of all is valid religiously. The reasoning is that the word 'food' as a genus can refer to all or some, so intention individualizes it.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The text reports a difference of opinion for oaths by divorce or manumission. Shams al-A'imma reports a view that he is believed even in court; Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar holds he is not believed in court because the intention establishes a reality only proven by intention.
The majority lean towards not believing in court for such oaths.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The phrase 'وهذا يشير إلى أنه لا يصدق قضاء' indicates the preferred view is non-acceptance in court for divorce oaths. The mufta bihi is that intention is accepted religiously, but in court the apparent meaning prevails unless the oath is not by divorce or manumission.
This is a nuanced position balancing textual interpretation and practical feasibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنه لو نوى الكل صدق قضاء وديانة ولا يحنث أصلاً لما في المحيط : لو حلف لا يأكل طعاماً أو لا يشرب شراباً وعنى جميع الأطعمة أو جميع مياه العالم يصدق في القضاء. وفي البدائع : لو قال والله لا آكل الطعام أو لا أشرب الماء أو لا أتزوج النساء فيمينه على بعض الجنس، وإن أراد به الجنس صدق لأنه نوى ما هو حقيقة كلامه وفي الكشف الكبير : إذا قال والله لا أشرب ماء أو الماء أو لا أكل طعاماً أو الطعام أنه يقع على الأدنى لأنه هو المتيقن وهو الكل لولا غيره فيكون فيه معنى الجنسية أيضاً، فإن نوى الكل صحت نيته فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى حتى لا يحنث أصلاً لأنه نوى محتمل كلامه لأنه فرد من حيث إنه اسم جنس لكنه عدد من وجه فلم يتناوله الفرد إلا بالنية؛ كذا في شرح الجامع لفخر الإسلام. وهذا يشير إلى أنه لا يصدق قضاء إن كان اليمين بطلاق أو نحوه لأنه خلاف لظاهر إذ الإنسان إنما نفسه باليمين عما يقدر عليه وشرب كل المياه ليس في وسعه وفيه تخفيف عليه أيضاً. وقال شمس الأئمة : قالوا وإطلاق الجواب دليل على أنه يصدق قضاء وديانة إن كان اليمين بطلاق ونحوه لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه وعن أبي القاسم الصفار أنه لا يصدق قضاء لأنه نوى حقيقة لا تثبت إلا بالنية فصار كأنه نوى المجاز ا هـ
Because if he intends all, he is truthful in court and religiously and does not break his oath at all, according to al-Muhit: If he swears not to eat food or not to drink drink and intends all foods or all waters of the world, he is believed in court. And in al-Bada'i: If he says, 'By Allah, I will not eat food or drink water or marry women,' his oath is upon some of the genus, and if he intends the genus, he is believed because he intended what is the reality of his speech. And in al-Kashf al-Kabir: When he says, 'By Allah, I will not drink water' or 'the water' or 'I will not eat food' or 'the food,' it falls upon the minimum because it is what is certain, and it is all except for other, so it also contains the meaning of genus. If he intends all, his intention is valid between him and Allah Almighty so that he does not break his oath at all, because he intended a possible meaning of his speech, since it is an individual in that it is a noun of genus but it is multiple in one aspect, so the individual does not include it except by intention; thus in Sharh al-Jami' by Fakhr al-Islam. And this indicates that he is not believed in court if the oath is by divorce or similar, because it contradicts the apparent, since a person only swears about what he is capable of, and drinking all waters is not within his capacity, and it is also a leniency for him. And Shams al-A'imma said: They said, the absolute answer is evidence that he is believed in court and religiously if the oath is by divorce or similar, because he intended the reality of his speech. And from Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar that he is not believed in court because he intended a reality that is only established by intention, so it is as if he intended a metaphor.
Source pages p. 549

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof discussing the effect of intention in general oaths. The core issue: when someone swears using a general term (like 'food' or 'water') but intends all instances of that term, is he believed? The text presents multiple authorities. Al-Muhit states outright that he is believed in court and religiously. Al-Bada'i adds that the oath initially falls on some of the genus, but if he intends the genus, he is believed because that matches the literal meaning. Al-Kashf al-Kabir offers a nuanced view: such an oath defaults to the minimum (the least that fulfills the word) because that is certain; but if he intends all, his intention is valid religiously. Then a key distinction is drawn: if the oath is by divorce or manumission, the religious truthfulness does not extend to court because the apparent meaning contradicts what is feasible. Shams al-A'imma reports a difference: some say he is believed even in court for divorce oaths; Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar disagrees. The Hanafi position is that intention in general terms is accepted religiously, but court acceptance depends on the type of oath.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intention in the heart that specifies or restricts the literal meaning of an oath.
قضاء (qadā'): The judicial ruling; what is accepted in court with enforceable consequences.
ديانة (diyānah): Religious truthfulness; what is accepted between the person and Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'By Allah, I will not drink water' intending all water in the world, but drinks a glass of water, what is the ruling according to the relied-upon view?
A) He breaks his oath and must pay kaffarah.
B) He does not break his oath religiously, but in court he would be considered to have broken it if the oath was by divorce.
C) He breaks his oath only if he intended all water deliberately.
D) He does not break his oath at all, even in court.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the intention behind a general oath is accepted between the swearer and Allah, but in court, acceptance depends on whether the oath involves divorce or manumission, where the apparent meaning prevails.

The 'Al-Kar' Distinction in Oaths About Drinking from Rivers
Intra-Hanafi dispute on interpretation of 'min' in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an oath not to drink from a river (direct sipping) and an oath not to drink its water, and state the Hanafi positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the interpretation of the preposition 'min'.

Core Idea
An oath 'not to drink from the river' applies only to direct sipping per Abu Hanifa; the Sahibayn extend it to any drinking method.

In Hanafi oath law, the precise wording matters. The phrase 'I will not drink from the Tigris' triggers a linguistic analysis that divides the imams. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that Abu Hanifa insists on the literal meaning of 'min' (partitivity) and limits the oath to direct sipping (al-kar'), while the Sahibayn follow conventional custom, equating it to any drinking method. Understanding this distinction reveals how Hanafis balance literal text and 'urf when interpreting legal speech.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two oath formulations
The passage contrasts two expressions: (1) 'I swear not to drink from the Tigris' and (2) 'I swear not to drink from the water of the Tigris.' Note that the first uses the preposition 'min' directly with the river, the second with 'water of the river'. This difference changes the ruling.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position on the first formulation
According to Abu Hanifa, 'I swear not to drink from the Tigris' applies only to al-kar' (drinking directly with the mouth from the source). Drinking with a vessel or hands does not break the oath. The particle 'min' indicates partitivity, and its true literal application is sipping, as evidenced by consensus that sipping breaks the oath.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider both formulations the same. They argue that conventional understanding ('urf) treats 'drinking from a river' as any method of consuming its water, including using a vessel. Thus drinking from a cup would break the oath in either case.
4
Analyze the exception for wells and cisterns
The passage notes a consensus: if sipping is impossible (e.g., a well or cistern), the metaphorical meaning (taking water by any vessel) is taken. This shows that when literal meaning is impossible, resort to custom is necessary; when possible, the dispute remains.
5
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if someone swears not to drink from the Tigris and then drinks from a cup, Abu Hanifa says the oath is not broken; the Sahibayn say it is. This affects whether expiation (kaffarah) becomes necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا يشرب من دجلة على الكرع بخلاف ماء دجلة) يعني لو حلف لا يشرب من دجلة فيمينه على الكرع وهو تناول الماء بالفم من موضعه نهراً أو إناء ـ كما في المغرب ـ فلا يحنث لو شرب بإناء أو بيده بخلاف ما لو حلف لا يشرب من ماء دجلة فإنه يحنث بالشرب من إناء أو غيره لأنه بعد الاعتراف بقي منسوباً إليه وهو الشرط وقالا : هما سواء فيحنث بالشرب من إناء لأنه المتعارف -المفهوم - وله أن كلمة «من» للتبعيض وحقيقته في الكرع وهي مستعملة ولهذا يحنث بالكرع اجماعاً فمنعت المصير إلى المجاز وإن كان متعارفاً، والتقييد بدجلة اتفاقي لأن الفرات والنيل كذلك بل وكل نهر . وقيد بالنهر لأنه لو حلف لا يشرب من هذا البئر أو من هذا الجب فإنه يحنث بشربه بالإناء اجماعاً لأنه لا يمكن فيه الكرع فتعين المجاز، وإن كان يمكن الكرع فعلى الخلاف ولو تكلف وشرب بالكرع فيما لا يمكن الكرع لا يحنث لأن الحقيقة والمجاز لا يجتمعان وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو شرب من نهر يأخذ من دجلة لا يحنث في المسألة الأولى لعدم الكرع في دجلة لحدوث النسبة إلى غيره ويحنث في الثانية لأن يمينه انعقدت على شرب ماء منسوب إليها وهي لم تنقطع بمثله، ونظيره ما إذا حلف لا يشرب من ماء هذا الحب فحول إلى حب آخر فشرب منه حنث . وفي البدائع : لو حلف لا يشرب من ماء دجلة فهذا وقوله لا أشرب من دجلة سواء لأنه ذكر الشرب من النهر فكان على الاختلاف ولو حلف لا يشرب من نهر يجري ذلك النهر إلى دجلة فأخذ من من ذلك الماء فشربه لم يحنث لأنه قد صار من ماء دجلة لزوال الإضافة إلى النهر الأول
His statement: (He does not drink from the Tigris upon sipping, unlike the water of the Tigris) means if he swears not to drink from the Tigris, his oath is upon al-kar' (sipping directly with the mouth from its place, whether river or vessel — as in al-Mughrib) so he does not break his oath if he drinks from a vessel or with his hand. Contrast if he swears not to drink from the water of the Tigris, then he breaks his oath by drinking from a vessel or otherwise, because after acknowledgment it remains attributed to it, and that is the condition. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: both are the same, so he breaks his oath by drinking from a vessel because it is the conventional meaning. And his (Abu Hanifa's) argument: the particle 'min' indicates partitivity, and its reality is in sipping, and it is used, therefore he breaks his oath by sipping by consensus, so it prevents resorting to the metaphorical meaning even if it is conventional. The restriction to the Tigris is accidental, because the Euphrates and the Nile are the same, indeed every river. And it is restricted to a river because if he swears not to drink from this well or this cistern, he breaks his oath by drinking from a vessel by consensus, because sipping is not possible there, so the metaphorical meaning is specified. And if sipping were possible, then it is upon the disagreement. And if he strains and drinks by sipping where sipping is not possible, he does not break his oath because reality and metaphor do not combine. And the author indicated that if he drinks from a river that takes from the Tigris, he does not break his oath in the first issue because there is no sipping in the Tigris due to the occurrence of attribution to other than it; and he breaks his oath in the second because his oath was contracted upon drinking water attributed to it, and it is not severed by such. And its analogy is if he swears not to drink from the water of this vessel, then it is transferred to another vessel and he drinks from it, he breaks his oath. And in al-Bada'i: If he swears not to drink from the water of the Tigris, this and his statement 'I will not drink from the Tigris' are the same, because he mentioned drinking from the river, so it is upon the disagreement. And if he swears not to drink from a river that flows into the Tigris, and he takes from that water and drinks it, he does not break his oath because it has become part of the water of the Tigris due to the removal of attribution to the first river.
Source pages p. 550

Commentary
This passage is a parsing exercise that explains the ruling on oaths regarding drinking from a river versus drinking its water. The key is the preposition 'min' (from). According to Abu Hanifa, 'I swear not to drink from the Tigris' means direct sipping (al-kar') — i.e., putting the mouth to the water and drinking directly. So drinking from a cup or hand does not break the oath. But if he swears 'not to drink from the water of the Tigris,' any method of drinking its water breaks the oath because 'water of the Tigris' retains the attribution. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree: they consider both expressions the same, so drinking from a vessel would break the oath in either case because that is the conventional understanding. The text explains Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the word 'min' for partitivity is literally used for sipping, and that usage prevents moving to metaphor (majaz) even if metaphor is common. The restriction to a river is important: for a well or cistern, sipping is impossible, so by necessity, the metaphorical meaning (drinking from any vessel) is taken by consensus. The text also covers secondary issues: if the river is a tributary of the Tigris, the ruling depends on the wording. If he swore not to drink from the Tigris, drinking from the tributary does not break the oath because it is not sipping directly from the Tigris; if he swore not to drink from the water of the Tigris, drinking from the tributary breaks it because the water is still attributed to the Tigris. This shows the precision of Hanafi legal reasoning in oath interpretation based on linguistic nuance.

Key Terms
الكرع (al-kar'): Drinking water directly with the mouth from its source (river, vessel, etc.) without using hands or another utensil.
من (min): The Arabic preposition meaning 'from'; in oath context, it indicates partitivity (تبعيض) according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if someone swears 'I will not drink from the Tigris' (لا أشرب من دجلة) and then drinks from the river using a cup, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because drinking from a cup is still drinking from the river.
B) No, because the oath is specifically on direct sipping (al-kar').
C) Yes, because the Sahibayn say so.
D) No, because the cup is not part of the river.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oath law, the interpretation of 'min' in 'drink from the river' is contested: Abu Hanifa restricts to direct sipping; the Sahibayn extend to any method based on custom; the practical difference is whether drinking by vessel breaks the oath.

Intention in Oaths: General Cannot Be Specified in Court
Intra-Hanafi dispute on intention in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on specific intention in a general oath and distinguish between its validity before Allah and in court, and identify the exceptional case of the oppressed.

Core Idea
Specific intention in a general oath is valid before Allah, but not in court — except for the oppressed.

When a person is forced to swear a general oath but intends a specific time, does his inner intention change the outward ruling? The Hanafi school distinguishes between the spiritual realm and the courtroom. This passage from Ibn Nujaym's Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explores this distinction and introduces a relief for the oppressed following al-Khassaf's opinion.

Guided Path
1
State the apparent madhhab position
Identify the ruling of the Zahir al-Riwaya: the specific intention does not affect the judicial ruling; the oath remains general in court.
This is the default position of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad.
2
Identify al-Khassaf's opposing view
Al-Khassaf holds that the specific intention is valid even in court, but only for the oppressed.
Al-Khassaf is a later Hanafi jurist; his view is from the nawadir layer.
3
Distinguish between judicial and spiritual contexts
Between the swearer and Allah, the specific intention is valid by consensus. In court, the outward wording prevails according to the apparent madhhab.
This distinction is key to understanding the dispute.
4
Understand the exception for the oppressed
If the swearer is in the hands of oppressors and takes al-Khassaf's saying, there is no harm. This provides a relief in cases of duress.
The fatwa remains on the apparent madhhab, but the oppressed may follow the exceptional view.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and tarjih
The fatwa is on the apparent madhhab for general application, but al-Khassaf's opinion is accepted for the oppressed.
The author states 'الفتوى على ظاهر المذهب' indicating the operative ruling.

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the ruling of intention in oaths. It presents the madhhab position (Zahir al-Riwaya) that when a person is made to swear a general oath but intends a specific time, his intention is not effective in court. However, the Hanafi jurist al-Khassaf holds that the intention is valid, but this is only with respect to judicial matters; between the swearer and Allah, the specific intention is accepted by consensus. The fatwa is on the apparent madhhab for judicial proceedings, but the oppressed may follow al-Khassaf's opinion. Al-Ramli's comment clarifies that the statement 'it is not valid' refers specifically to the judicial context, not to the spiritual realm.

Key Terms
نية تخصيص العام (niyyat takhsis al-'amm): The intention to specify a particular meaning within a general expression of an oath.
ظاهر المذهب (zahir al-madhhab): The apparent and most authoritative position within the Hanafi school, typically from the six core books of Imam Muhammad.
الخصاف (al-Khassaf): Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn 'Amr al-Khassaf (d. 261/874), a prominent Hanafi jurist known for his works on oaths and waqf.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a specific inner intention does not change the outward judicial effect of a general oath, except for the oppressed who may follow al-Khassaf's ruling.
