Swearing Not to Speak: Calling a Sleeping Person
Ruling on waking a sleeping person for oath-breaking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition for oath-breaking when calling a sleeping person and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the requirement of waking.

Core Idea
A sleeping person must be woken for the oath to be broken; merely calling without waking is insufficient.

This passage addresses a specific scenario: a person swears not to speak to someone, then calls that person while he is asleep. Does this break the oath? The Hanafi scholars examine the condition of the hearer’s awareness. The text explores the ruling when the call is heard but not understood, and when it is connected to the oath wording.

Guided Path
1
Identify the action and its ruling
The swearer calls a sleeping person and wakes him; the oath is broken because the speech reached the hearer. This is the initial ruling from the matn.
The author stipulates waking as a condition.
2
Examine the condition of waking
The preferred view requires that the swearer actually wakes the sleeper so that the voice is heard. If not woken, it is analogous to calling from a distance where the voice does not reach the hearer, and no oath is broken.
Al-Mabsut narration and 'al-mukhtar' view.
3
Contrast the different positions
Al-Quduri did not require waking; hearing without understanding suffices. Al-Sarakhsi validated even without waking, citing the aman example from al-Siyar al-Kabir, but the commentator distinguishes because aman is established with caution.
The chosen view is the one requiring waking.
4
Apply the rule to connected speech
The speech must be a new utterance after the oath; if it is connected to the oath wording (e.g., 'I swear I will not speak to you, but I have spoken to you'), there is a dispute among the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) and Zufar, analogous to the jug case.
This shows the complexity of oath-breaking conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا يكلمه فناداه وهو نائم فأيقظه أو إلا بإذنه فإذن له ولم يعلم حنث لأنه في المسألة الأولى كلمه وقد وصل إلى سمعه، وقد شرط المصنف أن يوقظه وهي رواية المبسوط وعليه مشايخنا وهو المختار لأنه إذا لم ينتبه كان كما إذا ناداه من بعيد وهو بحيث لا يسمع صوته لا يحنث . ولم يشترطه القدوري يسمع صوته لكنه لم يفهم يسمع المسائل التي جعل النائم فيها وهي من التغافله لم يسمع لكنه يصحح الإمام السرخسي وإن لم يوقظه لما ذكره محمد في السير الكبير : إذا نادى المسلم أهل الحرب بالأمان من موضع يسمعون صوته إلا أنهم لا يسمعون لشغلهم بالحرب فهو أمان اهـ. وقد فرق بأن الأمان يحتاط في إثباته. وقيد بكونه نائماً لأنه لو كان مستيقظاً حنث إن كان بحيث يسمع صوته إن أصغى إليه إذنه، وإن لم يسمع لعارض أمر كان مشغولاً به أو كان أصم وإن كان لا صوته لو أصغى إليه إذنه لشدة البعد لا يحنث ؛ كذا في الذخيرة وفيها: لا يحنث حتى يكلمه بكلام مستأنف بعد اليمين منقطع عنها لا متصل بها، فلو قال موصولاً إن كلمتك فالخلاف فيها بين أئمتنا الثلاثة وبين زفر كما مسألة الكوز
He said: (He does not speak to him, so he called him while he was sleeping and woke him, or except with his permission, so he gave him permission and he did not know, then he breaks the oath.) Because in the first case, he spoke to him, and it reached his hearing. The author stipulated that he wakes him, and this is the narration of al-Mabsut and upon it our teachers rely, and it is the chosen one because if he does not wake up, it is as if he called him from afar while he cannot hear his voice, he does not break the oath. Al-Quduri did not stipulate it: he hears his voice but does not understand; he hears from the cases where the sleeper is considered as not hearing due to his inattention, but he does not hear. Imam al-Sarakhsi validates even if he does not wake him, due to what Muhammad mentioned in al-Siyar al-Kabir: If a Muslim calls the people of war with aman from a place where they can hear his voice, but they do not hear due to being occupied with war, it is aman. And he distinguished because aman is to be cautious in establishing it. He conditioned it with being asleep because if he was awake, he breaks the oath if he was in a position to hear his voice if he had listened; and if he did not hear due to a hindering matter such as being occupied, or he was deaf, or his voice could not reach him if he listened due to great distance, he does not break the oath. Thus in al-Dhakhira. And in it: He does not break the oath until he speaks to him with renewed speech after the oath, disconnected from it, not connected. So if he says connectedly 'I have spoken to you', then there is a dispute among our three imams and Zufar, like the case of the jug.
Source pages p. 556

Commentary
This passage rules on a person who swears not to speak to someone, then calls the sleeping person and wakes him. The Hanafi position is that if the sleeping person is awakened and hears the call, the oath is broken because speech reached his hearing. The commentator discusses a condition: the swearer must wake the sleeper, as per al-Mabsut and preferred by our teachers; if he does not wake, it is like calling from a distance where the voice is not heard — no oath-breaking. Al-Quduri did not require waking; hearing without understanding suffices. Al-Sarakhsi validates even without waking, based on Muhammad's example of aman in war, but distinguishes because aman is established cautiously. The ruling is conditioned by sleeping; if awake, the oath is broken if the voice is audible under normal conditions (hearing but occupied, deaf, or too distant excepted). Additionally, the speech must be a new statement after the oath, not connected. If connected, there is a dispute among the three Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) and Zufar, similar to the jug case.

Key Terms
كلمه بكلام مستأنف (kallamahu bikalam musta'naf): Speaking to him with a new, independent speech after the oath, as opposed to connected speech that begins before the oath finishes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view in this passage, what is required for an oath not to speak to be broken when calling a sleeping person?
A) The sleeper must understand the words spoken.
B) The swearer must wake the sleeper so that he hears the voice.
C) The sleeper must respond verbally.
D) The swearer must call the sleeper by name.

Key Takeaway
To break an oath not to speak, the speech must reach the hearer; for a sleeping person, waking is required according to the chosen Hanafi view.

When Does a Time-Bound Oath Break?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on breach timing for time-bound oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a time-bound oath to an impossible act is broken, explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and identify the intra-Hanafi difference with Abu Yusuf's view.

Core Idea
A time-bound oath breaks at the end of the period, not immediately—the swearer has the whole chance to fulfill.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq refines a key point in Hanafi oath law: the distinction between absolute and time-bound oaths. The commentator critiques a statement from al-Qunya and affirms the established position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Let's trace the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify oath types
Distinguish between absolute (mutlaq) and time-bound (muwaqqata) oaths. The passage focuses on time-bound oaths where a specific period is mentioned.
Absolute = no time limit; time-bound = e.g., 'today' or 'this month'.
2
Ruling for absolute oaths
For absolute oaths, if the swearer becomes incapable, breach occurs immediately. This is the baseline.
The swearer must fulfill promptly; incapacity triggers immediate breach.
3
Apply to time-bound oaths
For time-bound oaths, breach is deferred. The swearer may fulfill at any time within the period, so breach occurs only when the period ends and incapacity is confirmed.
The entire period is a chance to fulfill.
4
Note intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold deferred breach. Abu Yusuf (stronger narration) holds immediate breach because incapacity is present from the start.
Both are within the Hanafi school; the majority view is relied upon.
5
Recognize the authority
The commentator emphasizes that the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is the established one, supported by most commentators, and that al-Qunya's contrary statement is weak.
The mufta bihi is the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (قيد بكون اليمين مطلقة الخ) قال الرملي : مفهومه أنه يحنث بمضي ذلك الوقت. وبه يظهر ضعف ما في القنية من قوله متى الحالف عن الفعل المحلوف عليه واليمين مؤقتة بطلت عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد، فإن الاعتبار لعدم الإمكان لا للعجز. وانظر إلى قولهم قاطبة أنها لو كانت مؤقتة لا يحنث حتى يمضي ذلك الوقت في مقابلة قولهم في المطلقة حنث للحال فحنثه في المؤقتة بمضي الوقت ثابت عندهم كما أطبق عليه الشراح، وقد عللوا المسألة بتصور البر والحنث للعجز عنه إما حالاً في المطلقة أو بعد مضي الوقت في المؤقتة. هذا وقد تقرر أنه لا اعتماد على كل ما قاله صاحب القنية مخالف للقواعد ما لم يعضده نقل من غيره وانظر ما ذكره في النهر في باب التعليق عند الكلام على قوله «وزوال الملك لا يبطلها» فإنه ذكر ما هو المختار للفتوى في مسألة ما لو حلف بالطلاق ليؤدين له اليوم كذا في دينه حنث وهو المختار . وقيد بالفعل لأنه لو حلف على الترك بأن قال إن تركت مس فعبدي حر لم تنعقد يمينه لأن الترك لا يتصور في غير المقدور
He said: (Conditioned upon the oath being absolute, etc.) Al-Ramli said: Its implication is that he breaks the oath at the passing of that time. And by this, the weakness of what is in al-Qunya becomes apparent: his statement 'Whenever the swearer is incapable of the sworn act and the oath is time-bound, it becomes void according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad' — because consideration is for impossibility, not incapacity. And look at their unanimous statement that if it were time-bound, he does not break the oath until that time passes, in contrast to their statement regarding the absolute: he breaks immediately. So his breaking in the time-bound at the passing of time is established with them, as the commentators have agreed. And they have reasoned the issue with the conceivability of fulfillment and breaking due to incapacity: either immediately in the absolute oath or after the passing of time in the time-bound oath. And it has been established that no reliance is placed on everything said by the author of al-Qunya that contradicts the rules unless supported by a transmission from others. And look at what is mentioned in al-Nahr in the chapter of ta'liq, in the discussion of his statement 'And the removal of ownership does not invalidate it' — for he mentioned what is chosen for fatwa in the issue: if he swears by divorce to pay him today such-and-such debt, he breaks it, and it is the chosen one. And it is conditioned upon action because if he swears upon abandonment, such as saying 'If I touch my slave, he is free', his oath is not contracted because abandonment is not conceivable in what is not within one's power.
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This passage refines the ruling on time-bound oaths to perform impossible acts. Al-Ramli explains that if the oath is time-bound (e.g., 'By Allah, I will ascend the sky today'), the swearer does not break it until the time expires, because the time allows flexibility. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold this view, contrary to al-Qunya's claim that it becomes void immediately. The reasoning: for absolute oaths, the swearer must fulfill immediately, so incapacity triggers immediate breach. For time-bound oaths, the swearer has the entire period to fulfill, so breach occurs only at the end when inability is confirmed. Abu Yusuf has two narrations: the stronger one is that he breaks immediately even in time-bound oaths, since incapacity is present from the start. The commentator also notes that the author of al-Qunya is not reliable when contradicting established rules. The passage further clarifies that the ruling applies to oaths to do an action; if the oath is upon abandoning an action, it is not contracted because abandoning is not an act within one's power in a conceivable sense.

Key Terms
مؤقتة (muwaqqatah): A time-bound oath, where the swearer specifies a time period for fulfillment (e.g., 'today' or 'this month').
عجز (ajz): Incapacity to perform the sworn act. In time-bound oaths, incapacity only triggers breach after the time expires, unless the incapacity is permanent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for a time-bound oath to do an impossible act?
A) Immediate breach
B) Breach at end of period
C) Oath is void
D) No breach ever

Key Takeaway
For time-bound oaths, the swearer has the entire period to attempt fulfillment; breach occurs only at the end if still impossible.

Oath not to speak: The condition of waking the sleeper
Explains the ruling on breaking an oath not to speak by calling a sleeping person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on breaking an oath not to speak by calling a sleeping person, and explain why waking is a condition for hanth.

Core Idea
An oath not to speak is broken only if the sleeping addressee wakes up and actually hears the speech.

When you swear not to speak to someone, then call him while he sleeps, does your oath break? The Hanafi school answers: only if he wakes up. This lesson unpacks the proof and the difference with al-Quduri.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is calling a sleeping person after swearing not to speak to him. The ruling is that if he wakes up, the oath is broken (hanth).
2
Locate the condition stipulated
The author (sahib al-Kanz) stipulates that the sleeper must be woken (yūqiẓuhu). This is the narration of al-Mabsūṭ and the chosen (mukhtār) view.
Al-Qudūrī did not require waking; sound reaching the ear suffices.
3
Analyze the proof
The proof is qiyās: if the sleeper does not wake, it is like calling from afar where the voice is not heard, so no hanth. The analogy with amān (granting security in war) is distinguished because amān requires extra caution (ihtiyāt).
The dalīl from al-Siyar al-Kabīr shows that amān is effective even if not heard due to occupation, but that is a special case.
4
Determine the muftā bihī
The relied-upon view is that waking is necessary. This is the position of al-Mabsūṭ and the majority of later scholars.
The practical difference: if he calls and the sleeper does not wake, no expiation (kaffāra) is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (لا يكلمه فناداه وهو نائم فأيقظه أو إلا بإذنه فإذن له ولم يعلم حنث لأنه في المسألة الأولى كلمه وقد وصل إلى سمعه، وقد شرط المصنف أن يوقظه وهي رواية المبسوط وعليه مشايخنا وهو المختار لأنه إذا لم ينتبه كان كما إذا ناداه من بعيد وهو بحيث لا يسمع صوته لا يحنث . ولم يشترطه القدوري يسمع صوته التغافله لم يفهم يسمع المسائل التي جعل النائم فيها وهي من الحنث، كما إذا ناداه وهو بحيث كالمستيقط وهي خمس وعشرون ذكرناها في باب التيمم وصحح الإمام السرخسي وإن لم يوقظه لما ذكره محمد في السير الكبير : إذا نادى المسلم أهل الحرب بالأمان من موضع يسمعون صوته إلا أنهم لا يسمعون لشغلهم بالحرب فهو أمان اهـ. وقد فرق بأن الأمان يحتاط في إثباته. وقيد بكونه نائماً لأنه لو كان مستيقظاً حنث إن كان بحيث يسمع صوته إن أصغى إليه إذنه، وإن لم يسمع لعارض أمر كان مشغولاً به أو كان أصم وإن كان لا صوته لو أصغى إليه إذنه لشدة البعد لا يحنث ؛ كذا في الذخيرة وفيها: لا يحنث حتى يكلمه بكلام مستأنف بعد اليمين منقطع عنها لا متصل بها، فلو قال موصولاً إن كلمتك: فالخلاف فيها بين أئمتنا الثلاثة وبين زفر كما مسألة الكوز فعجز عنه بأن لم يكن معه شيء ولم يجد من يقرضه وأن هذا من المواضع المهمة فكن فيه على بصيرة، وأنت على علم بأن العجز لو أبطل المؤقتة لما حنث هنا أي في مسألة ليصعدن السماء بمضي الوقت فيها فتأمله والله أعلم ا هـ . قلت : الظاهر مراد صاحب القنية العجز العارض في فيكون بياناً لما تقدم من أن شرط بقائها إمكان تصور البر في المستقبل فإذا كانت مؤقتة وكان فيه ماء فصب يحنث لتحقق العجز عن الفعل المحلوف عليه ويدل عليه أنه جعل بطلانها قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد أي خلافاً لأبي يوسف وهذا الخلاف إنما هو في مسألة الكوز كما أما هذه المسألة مر، :: لأنها لو كانت مؤقتة الخ) فإذا قال لأصعدن قوله : مر السماء اليوم فعندهما يحنث في آخر اليوم لأنه لما وقت كان غرضه توسعة الأمر على نفسه حتى يختار الفعل في أي وقت شاء، ولا يحنث بترك الفعل في بعض الأوقات فلا يتعين عليه الفعل إلا في آخر أجزاء الوقت المعين، فإذا لم يجب الفعل قبل ذلك لا يحنث بخلاف المطلق لأنه ليس في كلامه ما يوجب التوسعة فوجب عليه البر كما فرغ من اليمين، فإذا عجز يحنث، وليس في تأخير الحنث إلى آخر جزء من أجزاء حياته فائدة سوى تحقيق البر، فإذا كان العجز ثابتاً عادة لم يفد القول بالتأخير بل نظره في الحنث لأنه لو حنث في آخر أجزاء حياته ربما لا يمكنه التكفير، أما حقيقة بأن كان معسراً أو موسراً وذلك زمان لا يمكنه الوصية والتكفير قبل الحنث لا يجوز فيبقى في ورطة الإثم والعقاب، ولو حنث في الحال يمكنه التكفير وإسقاط الإثم فيحنث في الحال. وعن أبي يوسف روايتان والأصح أن عنده يحنث في الحال في الموقتة أيضاً لتحقق العجز في الحال
His statement: (He does not speak to him, so he called him while he was asleep and woke him up, or except with his permission, so he permitted him and he did not know) He broke the oath because in the first issue he spoke to him and it reached his hearing. The author stipulated that he wake him up, and it is the narration of al-Mabsut, and our shaykhs follow it, and it is the chosen view because if he does not wake up, it is as if he called him from afar where he does not hear his voice, he does not break the oath. Al-Quduri did not stipulate it; he hears his voice... [text continues with discussion of conditions and differences].
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This passage is an expanded ruling from the matn, discussing when an oath not to speak is broken by calling a sleeping person. The author explains that if you swear not to speak to someone, then call him while asleep and he wakes up, you break the oath because your speech reached his hearing. The condition of waking him up is stipulated by the author (صاحب الكنز) and is the relied-upon view. If he does not wake up, it is like calling from afar where he does not hear, so no oath-breaking. Al-Quduri did not require waking; the sound reaching him suffices. There is a detailed discussion about when a sleeping person is considered to have heard, and a comparison with the case of giving aman (security) to non-Muslims in battle. The passage then discusses the issue of inability (عجز) in timed oaths: if someone swears to do something by a specific time and becomes unable, he breaks the oath at the end of the time according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf has two narrations, the sounder being that he breaks immediately because the inability is certain. The example of the jug (كوز) and the oath to ascend heaven illustrate the principle.

Key Terms
حنث (hanth): Breaking an oath by committing the action sworn against or failing to do the action sworn to.
موقتة (muwaqqatah): An oath restricted to a specific time, e.g., 'by tomorrow'.
عجز (ajz): Inability to perform the sworn act, which may trigger immediate oath-breaking in some views.
أيقظ (ayqaza): To wake someone up; the condition for hanth when calling a sleeping person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears not to speak to Zayd, then calls out to him while he is asleep and Zayd does not wake up, does he break his oath according to the relied-upon view?
A) Yes, because his voice reached Zayd.
B) No, because Zayd did not hear.
C) Yes, because the act of calling is itself speech.
D) No, because the oath requires that Zayd actually hears and understands.

Key Takeaway
An oath not to speak is broken only when the speech actually reaches the addressee's understanding, which requires waking a sleeping person.

Inability (ajz) in timed oaths: immediate or deferred hanth?
Explains the ruling on when inability (ajz) causes hanth in timed oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether inability (ajz) causes immediate hanth in a timed oath (muwaqqatah) or defers it to the end of the time.

Core Idea
Inability to perform a timed oath triggers immediate hanth according to Abu Yusuf's sound narration, but hanth at the end of time according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

What if you swear to do something by a specific time, but become unable to do it before that time? Does your oath break immediately or only at the deadline? The Hanafi school has a difference of opinion, which this lesson clarifies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath type
The oath is muwaqqatah — restricted to a specific time (e.g., 'I will climb the heaven today').
2
Identify the cause of hanth
The cause is 'ajz (inability) that makes it impossible to fulfill the oath within the time.
Example: the jug (كوز) case where one swears to drink but the water is lost.
3
State the positions
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: hanth occurs at the end of the time, because the time was meant to give flexibility. Abu Yusuf (sound narration): hanth occurs immediately upon inability, because the inability is certain and deferring would risk leaving the oath unexpiated if death comes before the end.
Abu Yusuf has two narrations; the sounder is immediate hanth.
4
Analyze the reasoning
The practical consequence: immediate hanth allows immediate kaffara, ensuring no delay in expiation; deferred hanth risks dying without expiation. The former is preferred for caution.
The analogy with the jug case: if water is lost, immediate hanth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (لا يكلمه فناداه وهو نائم فأيقظه أو إلا بإذنه فإذن له ولم يعلم حنث لأنه في المسألة الأولى كلمه وقد وصل إلى سمعه، وقد شرط المصنف أن يوقظه وهي رواية المبسوط وعليه مشايخنا وهو المختار لأنه إذا لم ينتبه كان كما إذا ناداه من بعيد وهو بحيث لا يسمع صوته لا يحنث . ولم يشترطه القدوري يسمع صوته التغافله لم يفهم يسمع المسائل التي جعل النائم فيها وهي من الحنث، كما إذا ناداه وهو بحيث كالمستيقط وهي خمس وعشرون ذكرناها في باب التيمم وصحح الإمام السرخسي وإن لم يوقظه لما ذكره محمد في السير الكبير : إذا نادى المسلم أهل الحرب بالأمان من موضع يسمعون صوته إلا أنهم لا يسمعون لشغلهم بالحرب فهو أمان اهـ. وقد فرق بأن الأمان يحتاط في إثباته. وقيد بكونه نائماً لأنه لو كان مستيقظاً حنث إن كان بحيث يسمع صوته إن أصغى إليه إذنه، وإن لم يسمع لعارض أمر كان مشغولاً به أو كان أصم وإن كان لا صوته لو أصغى إليه إذنه لشدة البعد لا يحنث ؛ كذا في الذخيرة وفيها: لا يحنث حتى يكلمه بكلام مستأنف بعد اليمين منقطع عنها لا متصل بها، فلو قال موصولاً إن كلمتك: فالخلاف فيها بين أئمتنا الثلاثة وبين زفر كما مسألة الكوز فعجز عنه بأن لم يكن معه شيء ولم يجد من يقرضه وأن هذا من المواضع المهمة فكن فيه على بصيرة، وأنت على علم بأن العجز لو أبطل المؤقتة لما حنث هنا أي في مسألة ليصعدن السماء بمضي الوقت فيها فتأمله والله أعلم ا هـ . قلت : الظاهر مراد صاحب القنية العجز العارض في فيكون بياناً لما تقدم من أن شرط بقائها إمكان تصور البر في المستقبل فإذا كانت مؤقتة وكان فيه ماء فصب يحنث لتحقق العجز عن الفعل المحلوف عليه ويدل عليه أنه جعل بطلانها قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد أي خلافاً لأبي يوسف وهذا الخلاف إنما هو في مسألة الكوز كما أما هذه المسألة مر، :: لأنها لو كانت مؤقتة الخ) فإذا قال لأصعدن قوله : مر السماء اليوم فعندهما يحنث في آخر اليوم لأنه لما وقت كان غرضه توسعة الأمر على نفسه حتى يختار الفعل في أي وقت شاء، ولا يحنث بترك الفعل في بعض الأوقات فلا يتعين عليه الفعل إلا في آخر أجزاء الوقت المعين، فإذا لم يجب الفعل قبل ذلك لا يحنث بخلاف المطلق لأنه ليس في كلامه ما يوجب التوسعة فوجب عليه البر كما فرغ من اليمين، فإذا عجز يحنث، وليس في تأخير الحنث إلى آخر جزء من أجزاء حياته فائدة سوى تحقيق البر، فإذا كان العجز ثابتاً عادة لم يفد القول بالتأخير بل نظره في الحنث لأنه لو حنث في آخر أجزاء حياته ربما لا يمكنه التكفير، أما حقيقة بأن كان معسراً أو موسراً وذلك زمان لا يمكنه الوصية والتكفير قبل الحنث لا يجوز فيبقى في ورطة الإثم والعقاب، ولو حنث في الحال يمكنه التكفير وإسقاط الإثم فيحنث في الحال. وعن أبي يوسف روايتان والأصح أن عنده يحنث في الحال في الموقتة أيضاً لتحقق العجز في الحال
His statement: (He does not speak to him, so he called him while he was asleep and woke him up, or except with his permission, so he permitted him and he did not know) He broke the oath because in the first issue he spoke to him and it reached his hearing. The author stipulated that he wake him up, and it is the narration of al-Mabsut, and our shaykhs follow it, and it is the chosen view because if he does not wake up, it is as if he called him from afar where he does not hear his voice, he does not break the oath. Al-Quduri did not stipulate it; he hears his voice... [text continues with discussion of conditions and differences].
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This passage is an expanded ruling from the matn, discussing when an oath not to speak is broken by calling a sleeping person. The author explains that if you swear not to speak to someone, then call him while asleep and he wakes up, you break the oath because your speech reached his hearing. The condition of waking him up is stipulated by the author (صاحب الكنز) and is the relied-upon view. If he does not wake up, it is like calling from afar where he does not hear, so no oath-breaking. Al-Quduri did not require waking; the sound reaching him suffices. There is a detailed discussion about when a sleeping person is considered to have heard, and a comparison with the case of giving aman (security) to non-Muslims in battle. The passage then discusses the issue of inability (عجز) in timed oaths: if someone swears to do something by a specific time and becomes unable, he breaks the oath at the end of the time according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf has two narrations, the sounder being that he breaks immediately because the inability is certain. The example of the jug (كوز) and the oath to ascend heaven illustrate the principle.

Key Terms
حنث (hanth): Breaking an oath by failing to perform the sworn act.
موقتة (muwaqqatah): An oath restricted to a specific time.
عجز (ajz): Inability to perform the sworn act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the sound narration of Abu Yusuf, when does hanth occur for a timed oath when the swearer becomes unable to perform it?
A) At the end of the time.
B) Immediately upon inability.
C) Only if he dies before the time.
D) Never.

Key Takeaway
In a timed oath, inability (ajz) that is certain triggers immediate hanth according to the relied-upon view, so expiation becomes due at once.

Connected vs. Disconnected Speech in Oaths
Rulings on when subsequent speech is part of the oath or separate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi distinction between muttasil and munqati speech in oaths and apply it to determine whether an oath is broken.

Core Idea
Connected speech is part of the oath; disconnected speech is a new act that may break it.

When a person makes an oath and then immediately speaks further, does that additional speech violate the oath? The Hanafi school draws a critical distinction between speech that is connected (muttasil) and that which is disconnected (munqati). The ruling hinges on whether the words are a natural completion of the oath or a separate statement. In this lesson, we will explore the principle, analyze examples from the classical texts, and apply them to contemporary scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and subsequent speech
Read the passage and locate where the oath is followed by additional words. Determine if the additional words are immediately after the oath and complete the same thought.
Example: 'You are divorced, go!' — 'go' is connected.
2
Classify as muttasil or munqati
If the words are directly attached without a pause and intended as part of the same utterance, they are muttasil. If there is a new beginning or change of intention, they are munqati.
Use the criteria of continuity and speaker's intent.
3
Apply the ruling
If muttasil, the additional speech does not break the oath. If munqati, it does break the oath if it fulfills the sworn condition.
The relied-upon view is: connected speech no hanth; disconnected speech hanth.
4
Consider special cases and exceptions
The passage gives scenarios like sleeping, greeting a group, salam in prayer, knocking on a door, and indirect speech to a wall. Each requires careful analysis of connection.
For example, salam in prayer is connected to the prayer but may not break an oath not to speak.
5
Understand the proof from companion practice
The story of Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf shows that indirect speech (addressing a wall) avoids breaking the oath, demonstrating that intention matters.
This is a precedent (qawl sahabi) used as evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (أو اخرجي أو فأنت طالق فاذهبي أو اخرجي أو قومي أو شتمها أو زجر متصلاً لا يحنث لأن هذا من تمام الكلام الأول فلا يكون مراداً باليمين إلا أن يريد به كلاماً مستأنفاً. وفي المنتقى : لو قال فاذهبي أو واذهبي لا تطلق، ولو قال اذهبي طلقت لأنه منقطع عن اليمين. وفي نوادر ابن سماعة عن محمد : لا أكلمك يوماً أو غداً حنث لأنه كلمه اليوم بقوله أو غداً ا هـ . وتعقبه في فتح القدير بأنه لا شك في عدم صحته لأنه كلام واحد فإنه إذا أراد أن يحلف على أحد الأمرين لا يقال إلا كذلك. وعلى هذا إذا قال لآخر إذا ابتدأتك بكلام فعبدي حر فالتقيا فسلم كل على الآخر معاً لا يحنث وانحلت يمينه لعدم أن يكلمه بعد ذلك ابتداء، ولو قال لها أن ابتدأتك بكلام وقالت له كذلك لا يحنث إذا كلمها لأنه لم يبتدئها ولا يحنث بعد ذلك لعدم تصور ابتدائها، ولو حلف لا يكلمه فسلم على قوم هو فيهم حنث إلا أن يقصده فيصدق ديانة لا قضاء، أما لو قال السلام عليكم إلا على واحد صدق قضاء عندنا ولو سلم من الصلاة فإن كان إماماً قيل إن كان المحلوف عليه عن يمينه لا يحنث، وإن كان عن يساره حنث لأن الأولى واقعة في الصلاة فلا يحنث بها بخلاف الثانية. وقيل لا يحنث بهما لأنهما في الصلاة من وجه. وكذا عن محمد أنه لا يحنث بهما وهو الصحيح. ولو دق عليه الباب فقال من حنث ولو ناداه المحلوف عليه فقال لبيك أو لبى حنث، ولو كلمه الحالف بكلام لم يفهمه المحلوف عليه ففيه روايتان. ولو أراد أن يأمر بشيء فقال وقد مر المحلوف عليه يا حائط اسمع افعل كيت وكيت فسمعه المحلوف عليه وفهمه لا يحنث لما روي أن عبد الرحمن بن عوف حلف لا يكلم عثمان فكان إذا مر به يقول يا حائط اصنع كذا كذا ويا حائط كان كذا.
His statement: (Or go out, or you are divorced, then go, or go out, or stand up, or insult her, or scold) if connected, he does not break the oath because this is completion of the first speech, so it is not intended by the oath unless he intends it as new speech. In al-Muntaqa: If he said 'go' or 'and go' [following an oath], she is not divorced; and if he said 'go', she is divorced because it is disconnected from the oath. In Nawadir Ibn Sama'ah from Muhammad: 'I will not speak to you today or tomorrow' — he breaks the oath because he spoke to her today by saying 'or tomorrow'. This is refuted in Fath al-Qadir... [continues with various scenarios].
Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This passage presents rulings on whether a phrase immediately following an oath is considered part of the same utterance (connected) or a new independent statement (disconnected), affecting whether the oath is broken. The author distinguishes: if the phrase is connected (متصل) and completes the first sentence, it is not intended by the oath, so no hanth. If it is disconnected (منقطع), it is a new speech and may break the oath. Examples: If a man says to his wife 'You are divorced, go!' — if 'go' is connected, no additional divorce; if he says 'Go!' after a pause, it is a new command and she is divorced. Another scenario: swearing not to speak to someone but greeting a group that includes him — he breaks the oath unless he intended the group (then he is believed religiously but not judicially). Special cases: giving salam in prayer, knocking on a door, etc. The story of Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf shows that indirect speech (speaking to a wall) does not break the oath.

Key Terms
متصل (muttasil): A phrase immediately following the oath without pause, considered part of the same utterance.
منقطع (munqati): A separate phrase after a pause or new intention, considered a new speech act.
ديانة (diyanatan): Religious truth, as opposed to judicial truth; the person's claim is accepted religiously but not in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school, does a person break his oath if he says 'You are divorced, go!' with 'go' immediately connected without pause?
A) Yes, because he spoke additional words.
B) No, because 'go' is connected and part of the same utterance.
C) Yes, because 'go' is a separate command.
D) No, because the oath was about divorce, not about speaking.

Key Takeaway
Connected speech is part of the oath and does not break it; disconnected speech is a new action that may violate the oath.

Connected vs Disconnected Utterances in Oaths of Divorce
Ruling on whether 'fadhhabi' breaks an oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between connected and disconnected utterances in an oath of divorce and state whether the oath is broken.

Core Idea
A command connected to a divorce pronouncement is part of the same statement and does not break an oath; a disconnected command does.

In the Hanafi madhhab, the wording of an oath and its surrounding speech matter. When a man says to his wife 'You are divorced, so go', the word 'fadhhabi' is attached to the divorce with the particle 'fa', making it part of the same utterance. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the difference between connected and disconnected commands, citing al-Muntaqa for the distinction between 'fadhhabi' (connected) and 'idhhabi' (disconnected). Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining whether an oath has been broken.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance and its particle
Look at the words after the divorce pronouncement. Is there a conjunctive particle like 'fa' or 'wa'? Or is the command given without a particle? In the text, 'fadhhabi' has 'fa', 'wadhhabi' has 'wa', and 'idhhabi' has none.
2
Determine connection or disconnection
If the particle is present, the command is connected to the prior statement; it is considered part of the same speech. If the particle is absent (like 'idhhabi'), the command is a new, separate utterance.
3
Apply the rule
Connected utterances do not break an oath because they are not independent speech acts. Disconnected utterances do break the oath because they constitute a new speech act.
4
Note the exception: intention
Even a connected utterance can break the oath if the speaker intends it as a new statement. The default is connection, but intention can override.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأنت طالق فاذهبي أو اخرجي أو قومي أو شتمها أو زجر متصلاً لا يحنث لأن هذا من تمام الكلام الأول فلا يكون مراداً باليمين إلا أن يريد به كلاماً مستأنفاً. وفي المنتقى : لو قال فاذهبي أو واذهبي لا تطلق، ولو قال اذهبي طلقت لأنه منقطع عن اليمين.
So you are divorced, so go, or leave, or stand up, or insult her, or scold continuously – he does not break his oath, because this is part of the first statement, so it is not intended by the oath unless he intends by it a new statement. In al-Muntaqa: If he said 'fadhhabi' or 'wadhhabi' she is not divorced, but if he said 'idhhabi' she is divorced because it is disconnected from the oath.
Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This is a ruling from the chapter on oaths regarding speech. The passage explains when a man says to his wife 'You are divorced, so go (fadhhabi)' or similar commands. If the command is connected directly to the divorce pronouncement (with the particle 'fa' or 'wa'), it is considered part of the same utterance and not a separate speech act intended by the oath. Therefore, he does not break any previous oath not to speak. However, if the command is disconnected (like 'idhhabi' without a conjunctive particle), it is a new utterance and would break the oath. The author cites al-Muntaqa confirming this distinction.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): An oath, specifically an oath not to speak or perform an act, as in this chapter.
حنث (hanath): Breaking an oath by doing what one swore not to do.
منقطع (munqati'): Disconnected from the prior statement, thus a new utterance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a man says to his wife 'You are divorced, so go' (fadhhabi), does this break a previous oath not to speak?
A) Yes, because he spoke more words.
B) No, because 'fadhhabi' is connected to the first statement and not a new speech.
C) Yes, because 'go' is a command and thus a different speech act.
D) No, because he did not repeat the divorce.

Key Takeaway
A command attached to a divorce pronouncement with 'fa' or 'wa' is part of the same statement and does not break an oath, unless a new intention is proven.

The Oath 'La Ukallimuka Yawman Aw Ghadan': Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Ruling on oath-breaking with 'or tomorrow'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the relied-upon Hanafi position does not consider 'or tomorrow' as an oath-breaking speech act and identify the authority weight of the conflicting transmissions.

Core Idea
Phrasing an oath as a conditional alternative does not break the oath because the entire sentence is the oath itself.

This passage from the Nawadir of Ibn Sam'ah records a ruling by Imam Muhammad that saying 'or tomorrow' after swearing not to speak for a day is itself speech, thus breaking the oath. However, later Hanafi jurists like Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir criticize this view, arguing that the utterance is part of the oath, not a separate act. In this lesson, you will learn how the Hanafi school navigates such internal differences and which position is operative for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath statement
The person says 'I will not speak to you a day or tomorrow.' Determine the intended duration and structure of the oath.
Focus on the word 'aw' (or) which presents an alternative.
2
Analyze the word 'or'
In Arabic, 'aw' introduces an alternative. Is the statement one oath or two?
The phrasing suggests a single oath with alternative durations.
3
Apply Imam Muhammad's ruling
Imam Muhammad held that uttering 'or tomorrow' is speech occurring today, so the oath is broken immediately.
This ruling is recorded in Nawadir, which is lesser authority than Zahir al-Riwayah.
4
Consider the criticism from Fath al-Qadir
Ibn al-Humam argues that the entire phrase is one continuous statement; 'aw ghadan' is not a separate speech act.
This critique is based on linguistic analysis of oath formulations.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Later Hanafi authorities accept the critique, so the oath is not broken.
The relied-upon position is that no expiation is due.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي نوادر ابن سماعة عن محمد : لا أكلمك يوماً أو غداً حنث لأنه كلمه اليوم بقوله أو غداً ا هـ . وتعقبه في فتح القدير بأنه لا شك في عدم صحته لأنه كلام واحد فإنه إذا أراد أن يحلف على أحد الأمرين لا يقال إلا كذلك.
In Nawadir Ibn Sam'ah from Muhammad: 'I will not speak to you a day or tomorrow' – he breaks his oath because he spoke to him today by saying 'or tomorrow'. End. And it was criticized in Fath al-Qadir that there is no doubt about its incorrectness, because it is one statement; when he wants to swear by one of two things, it is not said except like that.
Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific ruling from Muhammad al-Shaybani (Imam Muhammad) recorded in the Nawadir of Ibn Sam'ah. The oath 'I will not speak to you for a day or tomorrow' is considered broken if the speaker utters the word 'tomorrow' today, because that utterance itself is speech. However, the commentator of Fath al-Qadir (likely Ibn al-Humam) criticizes this ruling, arguing that the phrase 'or tomorrow' is part of a single statement expressing an alternative, not a separate speech act. The disagreement highlights the importance of intention and the context of the utterance. The passage shows intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Imam Muhammad's view as transmitted by Ibn Sam'ah and the critique by later scholars.

Key Terms
نوادر (nawadir): Rare or non-standard transmissions from the early Hanafi imams, as opposed to Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if someone swears 'I will not speak to you for a day or tomorrow'?
A) The oath is broken immediately because he spoke.
B) The oath is not broken because the words 'or tomorrow' are part of the oath statement.
C) The oath is broken only if he speaks after the oath.
D) The oath is invalid because it is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that words forming part of an oath statement do not break the oath, even if they appear to be additional speech.
